The international pro-family movement

A **[US-driven theo-political]{.underline}** movement calling itself
**[['pro-family' is working to advance policy agendas in Africa, Eastern
Europe, India and Asia]{.mark} through]{.underline}** the establishment
of non-governmental organisations (**[NGOs]{.underline}**)
**[and]{.underline}** increasing advocacy work at **[the]{.underline}**
United Nations (**[UN]{.underline}**). As Croft (2007) and Martin (1999)
have revealed, **[[pro-family foreign policy involves measures
to]{.mark} '[strengthen the family]{.mark}' through opposition to birth
control]{.underline}**, the use of **[condoms]{.underline}** (even in
the fight against HIV/AIDS) **[and denial of]{.underline}** the
**[rights and dignity of sexual minorities.]{.underline}** Furthermore,
'Religious activists have consistently opposed any foreign-policy
initiative that might weaken parental control over children, facilitate
abortion, expand the rights of homosexuals, or devalue the role of the
conventional homemaker and mother' (Martin, 1999:74). The **[growing
influence of an international 'profamily' movement has been noted by
scholars who]{.underline}** have **[studied the [rise of discourses of
the 'natural' or 'traditional' family]{.mark} within domestic contexts
in the West at first]{.underline}**, and more recently their advances
within the UN **[and [across Africa and the Global
South]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Buss & Herman, 2003). Drawing on Christian
beliefs about the sanctity of marriage, pro-family organisations work to
actively 'preserve and promote' the nuclear family model, which is
positioned as the ideal kinship structure. For instance, **[the
[US-based]{.mark} organisation [Family Watch International works with
the UN and internationally to]{.mark}:]{.underline}**
**[[Preserve]{.mark} and promote [the family]{.mark}]{.underline}**,
**[[based on marriage]{.mark} between a man and a woman as the societal
unit that provides the best outcome]{.underline}** for men, women and
children.1 Similarly, Focus on the Family -- which is US based but which
has associate offices in 13 other countries across Asia, Latin America,
Europe, the Middle East and Africa -- sets itself out as: a global
Christian ministry dedicated to helping families thrive. We provide help
and resources for couples to build healthy marriages that reflect God's
design, and for parents to raise their children according to morals and
values grounded in biblical principles.2 **[The
[WCF]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[a project of]{.mark} the
[Illinois-based Howard Centre]{.mark}]{.underline}** for Family,
Religion and Society, **[was established]{.underline}** in 1997 as an
initiative **[to consolidate]{.underline}** various **[pro-life and
anti-gay advocacy organisations]{.underline}**, and to bring together
global conservatives (and elites) who can advance 'family values' at the
levels of national and international policy. The **[WCF [gives global
expression to]{.mark}]{.underline}** the notion that **[the [nuclear
family]{.mark}]{.underline}** is **[[universal to all of humanity and
history]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[[arguing that the family
is]{.underline}]{.mark}** '**[[based on]{.mark} the [marital union of a
man and a woman]{.mark}]{.underline}**, is **[the bedrock of
society]{.underline}**, the **[strength of our nations]{.underline}**,
**[and]{.underline}** the **[hope of humanity]{.underline}** ... the
ultimate foundation of every civilization known to history'. 3 In 2016,
the WCF launched the International Organisation for the Family in Cape
Town, South Africa, where delegates signed the Cape Town Declaration,
confirming their efforts to 'firmly resis\[t\] every push to redefine
marriage: to include same-sex or group bonds, or sexually open or
temporary ones'. Prominent African signatories to the declaration
include the head of the African Christian Democratic Party, Kevin
Meshoe, and the Nigerian ambassador to South Africa, Uche Ajulu-Okeke. A
few months prior to this gathering, **[the WCF collaborated with the
Africa Organisation for Families]{.underline}**, the **[Kenya Conference
of Catholic Bishops]{.underline}**, the **[Kenyan Ministry of Labour and
Social Protection]{.underline}**, the **[Catholic Archdiocese of
Nairobi]{.underline}**, the **[Cameroon-based Global Institute for
Family Integrity and]{.underline}** the **[Nigerian-based Foundation for
African Cultural Heritage]{.underline}** (FACH) **[to host the African
Regional Conference on Families.]{.underline}** Anne Kioko, President of
the African Organisation for Families, explained that **[comprehensive
sex education was amongst the concerns of Kenyan pro-family
advocates]{.underline}**, arguing that such 'programs go way beyond
regular sex education and are designed to change all the sexual and
gender norms of society' (Nyambura, 2016). **[['Pro-family' discourses
advocating for]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[nuclear family
model]{.mark} to be protected]{.underline}** in national and
international policy **[as the only legally recognised form of family
[are]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[becoming [increasingly
influential]{.mark} with[in international policy debates]{.mark} and
imaginaries.]{.underline}** The growing influence of the pro-family
movement has been enabled not only by advocacy work in civil society and
politics, but also in academia. In the United States, for instance, W.
Bradford Wilcox (cited later in this article) is the Director of the
National Marriage Project, which is based at the highly ranked
University of Virginia. Other pro-family advocates who are based in
academic departments include WCF Founder Allan Carlson (Professor of
History, Hillsdale College), Mark Regnerus (Professor of Sociology,
University of Texas-Austin), Everett Piper (Oklahoma Wesleyan
University) and Monique Chireau (Duke University). Beyond universities,
a number of pro-family advocates work from US-based think tanks that are
notoriously conservative, including the Sutherland Institute, the
Witherspoon Institute, the Population Research Institute and the
Heritage Foundation. Increasingly, popular discourse across the
continent construes people who do not subscribe to the heterosexual
nuclear family model not only as 'unAfrican', but also as socially and
economically threatening to the developing state. **[Within these
discourses]{.underline}**, non-heterosexuals, single mothers and
childfree women, those who engage in pre-marital and extra-marital sex
and reproduction, and **[alternative family forms are
scapegoated]{.underline}** for various social challenges, and therefore
experience forms of oppression, namely marginalisation, victimisation
and exclusion (Young, 2009).

Decolonising the family

As Oyěwùmí (2002:1) writes, the **[enforcement and institutionalisation
of the European concept of the 'family' formed]{.underline}** the
**[ideological basis of gender and race]{.underline}** 'as two
fundamental axes along which people became oppressed' **[in colonised
African societies]{.underline}**. As one of the 'scientific designs' of
modernity, **[the nuclear family ideal was]{.underline}** not created
**[by]{.underline}** Africans but by Western **[Europeans and
Americans]{.underline}**, responding to the needs and visions of these
places, not those of Africa (Mignolo, 2011:130). **[As decolonial
scholars]{.underline}** (Smith, 2006; Lugones, 2007; Quijano, 2007;
Mignolo, 2011) **[reveal]{.underline}**, **[there is scope to decolonise
the family]{.underline}**, **[given that [this]{.mark}]{.underline}**
Christian and bourgeois **[[kinship model was an instrumental axis
through which colonial power was]{.mark} implemented
[institutionalised.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The **[control of sexuality
and gender]{.underline}** that this model enabled **[was reinforced by
European control of knowledge]{.underline}** (Quijano, 2007; Grosfoguel,
2013). Within the pro-family movement, the **[['logic of
coloniality']{.underline}]{.mark}** (Quijano, 2007) **[is [reinvigorated
in that]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[normative ways in
which]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[eurocentric nuclear family
model]{.mark} is used [reinforce western
authority]{.mark}]{.underline}** while also protecting systems of
domination that can be legitimated through the nuclear family's
modelling of sexed and gendered hierarchies as natural.
Mid-twentieth-century literature on kinship in Africa (Hunter, 1936;
Krige, 1936; Schapera, 1940; Phillips, 1953; Reuter, 1963) largely
offered descriptive accounts that constructed African kinship systems as
located along a linear path of 'progression' towards 'advanced' family
forms associated with modernisation (Siqwana-Ndulo, 1998:408).
**[[Christonormative European thought constructed the gender binary and
hierarchy as]{.mark} evidence of [enlightened
society]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[with]{.underline}]{.mark}** any
other form of **[[indigenous sex and gender]{.underline}]{.mark}**
practice **[constructed [as 'savage']{.mark} and]{.underline}**,
further, **[as an indication of the need to evangelise and modernise
these societies]{.underline}** (Kitch, 2009). Howard Washington Odum's
1910 publication, Social and Mental Traits of the Negro, illustrates the
ways in which racist ideology drew on patriarchal constructions of
'civilization', reporting that 'in his home life, the Negro is filthy,
careless, and indecent ... as destitute of morals as many of the lower
animals \[and with\] little knowledge of the sanctity of home or marital
relations' (Stacey 1994:66). Such **[sociological and anthropological
scholarship on 'family']{.underline}** and kinship **[in Africa
provided]{.underline}** the **[[epistemic resources used to justify
conquest, colonisation]{.mark} and domination]{.underline}** of the
non-European world. Thus, **[colonised men were constructed as being
unable to govern colonised women]{.underline}**, and therefore unable to
govern themselves (Kitch, 2009). Employing the nuclear family model as a
means of measuring 'savagery' or 'civilization', enlightenment ideology
erected a system of racial classification and ranking through a
gender/sex hierarchical order that positioned male dominance as a signal
of cultural advancement (2009:56). African men, fated to become slaves,
were considered to lack fully formed souls, like women, and the
institution of marriage offered a gendered biblical model that could
justify the capture and enslavement of African people (2009:52). What
**[pro-family accounts of the universality of the 'nuclear
family']{.underline}**, such as those quoted earlier, further
**[obscure]{.underline}** is **[the extent to which African kinship
systems were destroyed throughout]{.underline}** the period of
**[colonial conquest.]{.underline}** At the same time that European
scholars were constructing African kinship structures as inferior
through the normative modern ideal of the nuclear family, they were
actively destroying the kinship systems of colonised people. The
**[[idealised western nuclear family model is]{.underline}]{.mark}**
therefore **[[implicated in economic]{.underline}]{.mark}** and
**[[knowledge practices]{.underline}]{.mark}** of modernity that have
justified racism and constructed some human lives as inferior and
dispensable (Mignolo, 2011:6). Mignolo reinforces the salience of
heteropatriarchy within a power relations, arguing that a global
gender/sex hierarchy 'privileged males over females and European
patriarchy over other forms of gender configuration and sexual
relations' through the invention and institutionalisation of sex
(heterosexual/homosexual) and gender (male/female) binaries (Mignolo,
2011:18). Thus, 'a global racial/ethnic hierarchy that privileged
European people over non-European people' secured the privileged status
of European men over all men and colonised people, as well as European
women (2011:18). These **[[systems]{.mark} were held [in place by
privileging of western knowledges]{.mark} over non-western
knowledges]{.underline}** (which were often targeted for eradication)
**[[and]{.mark} a [spiritual]{.mark}]{.underline}**/religious
**[[hierarchy]{.mark} that privileged Christianity]{.underline}** over
non-Christianity and other non-western spiritualities (2011: 18).

#### The institution of the family is itself a weapon bought and sold in the project of conquest, antiblackness, heteropatriarchy, imperialism, and capitalism -- the white nuclear family as a dual processes of clearing and accumulation is sustained by the control and state power exercised through the family as a monopoly on the proper forms of kinship. The family must be abolished.

**King 18**. Tiffany Lethabo King is an Assistant Professor of Women's,
Gender and Sexuality Studies at Georgia State University. Her research
focuses on black gender formation and sexuality in the African diaspora,
critical geographies, and the intersections of slavery and settler
colonialism in the U.S. and Canada \[\"Black \'Feminisms\' and
Pessimism: Abolishing Moynihan\'s Negro Family,\" 2018, Theory & Event
Vol. 21\]//vikas

During the 1960s and 70s **[a cadre of Black women
activists]{.underline}** and cultural workers **[aligned themselves
with]{.underline}** a form of **[anti-capitalist, anti-racist,
anti-sexist]{.underline}**, at times **[anti-homophobic and decolonial
politics.]{.underline}** Some of **[these Black women were featured
in]{.underline}** the anthology **[The Black Woman]{.underline}**
(1970). It is in her contribution to this volume that **[[Lindsey
deconstructs the family]{.mark} as a violent formation [and calls for
its abolition]{.mark}]{.underline}** as an institution. **[[Lindsey's
critique]{.underline}]{.mark}** of the family departed from white
radical and lesbian feminist critiques in the sense that it
**[[linked]{.mark} the [family to antiblackness and
conquest.]{.mark}]{.underline}** It's **[intersectional analysis
reaches]{.underline}** far **[[beyond]{.underline}]{.mark}** a critique
of **[[patriarchy and capitalism implicating]{.underline}]{.mark}** the
family in everyday forms of **[[imperial, genocidal and antiblack
violence.]{.underline}]{.mark}** Lindsey's **[[abolitionist critique
calls attention to]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[ways]{.mark} violent
processes of [property accumulation]{.mark} [give white heteronormative
gender, sexuality and family]{.mark} formations [coherence and]{.mark}
secure [white ascendency through antiblack racism and Native
genocide]{.mark}]{.underline}**. The American family is far from a
benign and innocent site of existence. Lindsey pens, "**[[The family
and]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[land on which it lived]{.mark} and
cultivated]{.underline}** its **[crops [became the man's
property]{.mark}]{.underline}**, man moved on to the seizure of the land
of others and his prisoners of war became his slaves. Upon this base,
**[the state evolved and empires were created.]{.underline}**"17 For
Lindsey, **[[the family is the core and animating unit of property
creation and]{.mark} of the [imperial state]{.mark}]{.underline}**. In
the essay, Lindsey connects family, land, slaves, property, state and
empire in order to situate the family within various forms of
historical, state and genocidal violence. Lindsey's indictment of the
family invites a spatial analysis of its power. **[The family as a
social formation can be reconcieved as an amalgamation of land, slaves,
property, conquest and the state.]{.underline}** The family could be
constructed **[as geopolitical unit or assemblage]{.underline}**
(family-land-slaves-property-state-empire) **[that creates the American
frontier]{.underline}**, or the time-space coordinates of the human. The
family as the geopolitical unit of women-children-land-slaves is a form
of property that can be accumulated. Specifically, **[[property is
accumulated through]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[conquest]{.mark} of
Native people,]{.underline}** the theft of land **[[and]{.mark} the
[accumulation]{.mark} of slaves as property]{.underline}**. According to
Lindsey, family formation is a Western formation that is anti-Black.
**[[The family]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[as a white
institution]{.underline}**, **[[has been held up to Blacks as]{.mark} a
[desirable but somehow unattainable]{.mark} goal]{.underline}**, at
least not in the pure form that whites have created. **[Witness [the
Black middle class]{.mark}]{.underline}** or pseudo-escapees into the
mainstream. **[This group [has]{.mark} assumed many]{.underline}** of
the **[institutional postures]{.underline}** of the oppressor, including
the so-called intact family, but even here we find **[a fantastically
[high divorce rate and frustration]{.mark} on]{.underline}** this
**[domestic level]{.underline}** has **[increased dissension between
individual Black men and women]{.underline}**, when **[it should instead
be a signal that [something is radically wrong with the model
they]{.mark} have chosen to [imitate.]{.mark}]{.underline}**18 For
Lindsey, **[[the family is a white institution.]{.underline}]{.mark}**
**[The family as a patriarchal unit of property]{.underline}**,
enslavement and conquest is not a priori to Black sociality. In fact,
Black people often fail in their attempts at replicating the formation.
**[[Lindsey calls for the entire model to be destroyed]{.mark} rather
than succumb]{.underline}** to the institution. We have an obligation as
Black women to project ourselves into the revolution to destroy these
institutions which not only oppress Blacks but women as well, for if
these institutions continue to flourish, they will be used against us in
the continuing battle of mind over body.19 **[[The institution of the
family is]{.mark} constructed as [a weapon that will be used in]{.mark}
the "[battle of mind over body]{.mark}."]{.underline}**20 Further,
**[Lindsey theorizes [the family as an organizing logic of]{.mark}
control and [state power.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The family is not a
private space or a private matter for Lindsey. In fact, the family is
intimately connected to the state and animates its power. Lindsey
states, "**[The family has been used by]{.underline}** the **[white
agency to perpetuate the state]{.underline}**, **[and Blacks have been
used as extensions]{.underline}** of the white family,
**[as]{.underline}** the **[prisoners of war enslaved to do the dirty
work of the family]{.underline}**, i.e. the state. If the family as an
institution were destroyed, the state would be destroyed."21 As an
activist, Lindsey's work as an advocate for poor single Black mothers
made her acutely aware of the ways that the state constructed the notion
of the Black AFDC 'family'22 as a site of surveillance and intervention.
Lindsey reminds us that **[the [disciplinary power of the welfare state
has access to]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[internal domestic space of
Black women]{.underline}]{.mark}** (or the Black matriarch's house). The
increase in state sanctioned surveillance prompted by The Report and
administered through a diffuse network of welfare case managers, monthly
reporting and documentation of sexual behavior, children, income and
daily activity was an issue and concern that weighed heavily on Kay
Lindsey. **[In The Report]{.underline}**, **[[Moynihan is
deploying]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[epistemologies]{.mark} and
tools [of sociological surveillance]{.mark}]{.underline}** in order
**[[to make Black households transparent]{.underline}]{.mark}** in order
**[to assess whether they can be assimilated into]{.underline}** the
**[social and spatial formation of the family.]{.underline}** Patricia
Hill Collins (1989) argues that **[Moynihan erroneously conflates the
Black household with the Black family]{.underline}** when he wrote the
report.23 Hill Collins is making an argument for a more expansive notion
of family in Black life that cannot be contained within or reduced to
the empirical unit or data of the household. On the other hand, Lindsey
and contemporary Afropessimists would argue that it cannot be presumed
that there is such a thing as a 'Black family' in 1965 when the report
is drafted.

#### Thus the method: we demand the end of foreign military sales and direct commercial sales of the colonial household.

#### White racial kinship structures the nation state, white supremacist vigilantism, and liberal safety-pin wearing symbolic gestures of false allyship that refuse to put comfort, families, friends, and least of all lives--- on the line. The only thing that can be done is to remake the world through a refusal of white racial kinship.

**Sharpe 16** \[Christina Sharpe, "Lose Your Kin," *The New Inquiry*,
November 16, 2016. <https://thenewinquiry.com/lose-your-kin/> //tjb\]

"**[[SLAVERY is the ghost in the machine of
kinship]{.underline}]{.mark}**." **[Saidiya Hartman's concise
articulation gets to the heart of the ways that chattel slavery
continues to animate the present: transatlantic [chattel slavery's
constitution of domestic relations made kin in one direction, and in the
other, property]{.mark} that could be [passed between]{.mark} and among
[those kin.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[This is the ghost in the machine
of contemporary U.S. life and politics.]{.underline}** We can trace this
through the examples of U.S. Senator James Henry Hammond (1807-1864) and
U.S. Senator Strom Thurmond (1902-2003) and into the present. Hammond
claimed ownership over other people of what he called that "inferior
race," and Thurmond, a Dixiecrat, declared: "All the laws of Washington
and all the bayonets of the army cannot force \[...\] the southern
people to break down segregation and admit the Negro race into our
theaters, into our swimming pools, into our homes, and into our
churches.'' Both were residents of Edgefield, South Carolina and both
"fathers" of Black women, slave and free, whom they never claimed as kin
in the ways that they would claim their white children. **[The laws of
U.S. chattel slavery and Jim Crow made white kinship (legally,
familially, and politically). These modes of [recognizing white kinship
and refusing to recognize Black personhood endure into the present; they
make and unmake persons and families, and assign human beings
value]{.mark} in and of themselves, [or not.]{.mark}]{.underline}**
Thurmond's declaration encompasses not only Black people, but Latinx,
Muslim Americans and more. **[White kin in one direction, "property" in
another. [Whiteness]{.mark}, then, [is a political project.]{.mark}
[It]{.mark} is distinct from, but [often acts in concert with, the
political projects of making and sustaining nation, ethnicity, and
ethnic nationalisms.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[Whiteness is a political
project and it is also a logic, by which I mean it is a calculus, a way
of sorting oneself and others into categories of those who must be
protected and those who are, or soon will be, expendable.]{.underline}**
**[[It is this calculus that allowed Dylan Roof to sit]{.mark}, for one
hour or more [with the]{.mark} welcoming [members of]{.mark} a Bible
study and prayer group at the Emanuel African Methodist Episcopal in
South Carolina ([the oldest AME church in the South]{.mark}), [and
then]{.mark} to show them his gun, to rise from his seat, [to slaughter
most of them]{.mark}, and to spare three so that they could "tell
everyone what happened." [It is this logic that allowed Dylan Roof's
friends to say nothing.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Those friends noted that
he seemed changed and "angry" but they asked him no questions when he
repeatedly broadcast his homicidal intentions in the weeks before he
massacred six Black women and three Black men. **[The logics of
[whiteness allowed him to be captured alive (by kin) and then taken to a
Burger King]{.mark} and fed because he said he was tired and hungry.
That whiteness and white supremacy are logics of making the human also
means that it is expected that the survivors of the massacre will offer
Roof their forgiveness. [White supremacists are
angry;]{.mark}]{.underline}** those who claim that title and those who
do not but who act in the interests of white supremacy. Not only are
they angry, but also "we" are told that their anger must be
understood--that "we" must make room for it. This "we" is across race,
sex, class, gender, and geography. **[[This unmoral, unethical anger has
the full support of the state.]{.underline}]{.mark}** **[[It is the only
anger]{.mark} that the state recognizes; the only anger [not
criminalized or met with deadly brutal force.]{.mark}]{.underline}** It
is the anger of Ryan and Ammon Bundy and the understanding of the jury
of their all-white peers that found them not guilty of the illegal
occupation and armed takeover of the Malheur National Wildlife Refuge.
**[[It is an unmoral anger that hits]{.mark} in the register and the
grammar of violence, [in the logics of law and order, in electoral
victories and the grammar of reconciliation with kin by any
means.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[Many white people are struggling to
figure out if they should speak and what they might say to their white
kin]{.mark} now[,]{.mark} in this moment. (Kin here means, [all of those
recognized by the self--in some fundamental]{.mark}, indelible [way--as
being like the self]{.mark}.)]{.underline}** They are wondering if they
should be silent or if they should broach the election with their
intimates, with the people who are closest to them and who occupy
different points of view, who voted differently, who apprehend the world
in ways they self-report as deeply antithetical or inimical to their
own. **[They acknowledge that [the lives of people, not them, are at
stake.]{.mark}]{.underline}** They take to the streets to protest, and
yet some speak this fear of potential loss of kin into the same ether as
their co-workers, friends, and colleagues who are marked (as
Black/Muslim/refugee/Latinx/immigrant/LGBTQI/differently abled/Asian and
Native American/undocumented) and are in imminent danger. **[[They
equivocate at the thought and reality of losing kin, and at]{.mark}
probable [discomfort and pain;]{.mark} and in that interjacent space of
equivocation, they reconstitute and re-enflesh that ghost of a past that
is not yet past.]{.underline}** White people are searching for ways to
show solidarity to people of color and some have landed on the
performative symbol of wearing a safety pin. Symbols are important and a
safety pin is not enough. A safety pin is a temporary fix for a rend in
the fabric. **[One must be willing to say this is abhorrent. [One must
be willing to be more than uncomfortable. One must be willing to be on
the outside.]{.mark} [One must refuse to repair a familial rift on the
bodies cast out as not kin.]{.mark} Slavery is the ghost in the machine
of kinship. [Kinship relations structure the nation.]{.mark}
Capitulation to their current configurations is the continued
enfleshment of that ghost. [Refuse reconciliation to ongoing
brutality.]{.mark} [Refuse to feast on the corpse of others. Rend the
fabric of the kinship narrative. Imagine otherwise. Remake the world.
Some]{.mark} of us [have never had any other
choice.]{.mark}]{.underline}**

#### Rather than beginning from the House of IR, our advocacy attempts to think and live with Worldism, opening ourselves up to multiple worlds rather than a single and universalizing House -- challenging the reproduction of the global system and allowing us to pose alternative spatio-temporal visions of globality.

Transforming the House of IR Let us return to the House of IR.
**[[Transforming colonialism]{.mark}, Stoler]{.underline}** (2002:217)
**[suggests, [requires recalling \"other kinds of memories \... and
stories]{.mark} to tell.\" [These would begin to pave alternative
visions of]{.mark} and approaches to [the material inequalities that
beset the world]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Agathangelou 2004a). Steve Smith
(2004) concurs. Aus- tralia\'s aborigines, he points out, believe that
they \"sing\" their world into existence. In contrast, **[[IR
scholars]{.mark} seem much less wise, \"rarely reflect\[ing\] on who
wrote the words and the music \[of IR\], and virtually never listening
out for, nor recognizing, voices of worlds than our own until they
occasionally force us into silence\"]{.underline}** (Smith 2004: in
press). To begin our awakening, we need to theorize historically.
**[That is, our [analytical categories need to]{.mark} help us
distinguish and [explain]{.mark} particular historical forms of world
politics and social relations. Included must be \"some conception of
[how \[social relations\] might be implicated in the reproduction of
\[global\] systems]{.mark} in particular\"]{.underline}** (Rosenberg
1994:38). [[**For this reason, we turn to Worldism**.]{.mark} It begins
with five epistemological commitments: (1) intersubjectivity, defined as
institutionalized social structures that emerge through the labor of
agents in relation to other agents; (2) agency, which \"reverberates\"
with Others in a constant process of creating, articulating, and be-
coming; (3) identity, whose abstract subjectivity (e.g., notions of
Self) emerges from materiality (e.g., the body) and the social relations
of production that produce it; (4) critical syncretic engagement, which
fosters exploration and experimentation at the interstices of multiple
worlds where conflict and contestation most pressure the need for
negotiation, adaptation, and reformulation; and (5) accountability,
which stems from the Self\'s inescapability from the Other.]{.underline}
[These commitments differ from the House of IR\'s five main principles:
sovereignty, hierarchy, normality, legitimacy, and power.]{.underline}
Worldism does not deny the historical lineages that found the House of
IR. On the contrary, **[[Worldism historicizes the House]{.mark} itself
[as an effect of]{.mark} larger, structural inequalities in [global
capitalist- patriarchy.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[In response, [Worldism
poses open-ended questions]{.mark}, not answers, [that shift IR from an
organizing principle of \"order versus disorder\" to that of
\"negotiation across difference\" to construct a common yet alternative
universe.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[[Worldism takes seriously Other
worlds by engaging with]{.mark} their [crises and contradictions, not to
convert or conquer them into another version of the
Self.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Worldism relates to the House of IR
accordingly: (1) Intersubjectivity Dissolves Sovereignty. **[Sovereignty
in the House of IR enables the declaration: \"I reside in the center,
you stay at the margins.\" This mode of political organizing silences
the problematic of ideologically territorializing and compart-
mentalizing communities into house versus state versus market versus
order versus disorder, as if these divisions do not sustain structurally
asymmetrical social relations.]{.underline}** \"Cooperation\" and
\"interdependence\" simply delude under these conditions. **[Worldism
asks, instead: \"How can we understand each other, especially our
relationally produced, differentially experienced positions, locations,
interests, and privileges?\" This open-ended query compels us to
recognize and accept multiple sources of knowledge and their worlds. [It
dismantles house boundaries]{.mark} and the constructions [that keep us,
literally and figuratively, in place.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Poetry
offers one means of doing so: Poetry envisions and revisions forms of
political life. Yet its closer emphasis on words sometimes
illuminates-recalls or reinvents-their narrative construction. That is
to say, it can highlight how words are not just narrative material but
\... can themselves have stories to tell \.... \[Poetry\] can help to
expose how the dominant world narratives we are called to accept and act
upon are in turn but a collage of word-stories forgotten, interpreted,
or synchronized to suit the rhe- torical moral of the grand narrative
(Constantinou 2000:287). **[By broadening the routes to voicing and
becoming, Worldism escapes narrow, fixed boundaries and the interests
they sustain. No longer must we tolerate bourgeois, patriarchal
authority passing for \"abstract individualism.\" Instead, [Worldism
foregrounds]{.mark} the fluid, dynamic landscape of mutual
understandings and [constant (re)constructions of who and what we
are.]{.mark} [From intersubjectivity]{.mark}, then, [we may develop
strategies to dissolve the House\'s fixation on and need for
sovereignty.]{.mark}]{.underline}** (2) Agency Obsolesces Hierarchy.
**[Hierarchy stabilizes [the House]{.mark} (\"I live upstairs, you work
\[for me\] downstairs\"). It [relies on an epistemic violence that
denigrates Other sources of knowledge, traditions, and worlds even while
exploiting them for sustenance.]{.mark} [Such violence]{.mark} is not
unilateral. It [afflicts, also, those who luxuriate \"
upstairs.\"]{.mark} They must contend with the agony and anger of
disenfranchised progeny while worrying, at the same time, about access
to power and wealth in a constantly changing and contested
context.]{.underline}** Seen from this perspective, hegemonic stability
theory takes on a self-isolating, self-reifying character. In contrast,
**[Worldism asks: \"How do we affect our worlds?\"]{.underline}** It
focuses attention on the empowering, world-making role of all agents as
they reverberate off each other\'s identities and subjectivities,
desires and interests, power and histories. **[Recognizing that
production and reproduction, public and private, masculine and feminine
together contribute to a more integrated social life may, at last, free
us from an internalized oppression that deracinates and depoliticizes
both knowledge and power. [In helping us recognize that difference is
constituted through social relations,]{.mark} Worldism affirms that
identity is not immutable. [We are enabled]{.mark}, then, [to imagine
and develop different]{.mark} yet [interconnected
communities]{.mark}]{.underline}**[.]{.mark} Such community-building
disturbs and ultimately obsolesces hierarchy. (3) Identity Relativizes
Normality. **[The House of IR decides the norm (\"I set the standard,
you comply with it\"). It needs to keep in line \"rebel sons\" and
\"fallen daughters,\" not to mention \"bastard sons\" and a roaming
\"love child\" here and there. Like the actual colonial household, the
House of IR resorts to this tactic to deny its intimacies with exotic,
destabilizing Others, even when they produce common
progeny.]{.underline}** **[Worldism removes such alienation and
ambivalence by asking: \"How does my identity or self-understanding
emerge from interactions with others?\"]{.underline}** **[In this way,
we may engage with those intimate parts of ourselves, as individuals and
collectivities. Even during moments of fear and insecurity, we may risk
listening to, communicating with, and learning from those who seem so
different but who are really part of us and just as committed to making
our worlds better.]{.underline}** By sustaining healthy (nonparanoid,
noncovetous) communities that benefit the world\'s majority, Worldism
dynamizes and eventually relativizes the norm. (4) Engagement Supercedes
Legitimacy. **[[The House of IR needs to enforce legitimacy]{.mark} (\"I
am genuine, you are false at worst or an incomplete imitation at best\")
to uphold all its previous properties: [sovereignty, hierarchy, and
normality.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Indeed, legitimacy draws clear
boundaries around those who \"belong\" and those who don\'t, separat-
ing \"gentry\" from \"riffraff,\" \"high politics\" from \"low
politics,\" \"concepts\" from \"data,\" \"theorist\" from \"activist.\"
**[[What results]{.mark}, as we have seen, [is a]{.mark} camouflaged
[complex of erasures, violences, and desires]{.mark} that, nonetheless,
percolate to the public, especially when crises break the
routine[.]{.mark} [Relatively easy access to technologies of cruelty
through a globalized and commodified militarization further intensifies
the need for Worldism]{.mark}\'s next pair of questions: \"How can I
engage with Others-and myself-through critique?\"]{.underline}**24 And
equally important, \"how do I re- construct syncretically from this
critical engagement?\" **[[Critical, syncretic engagement across
borders, categories, and generations allows]{.mark} agents from
[multiple worlds]{.mark}-whether marked by tradition, race, class,
gender, or sexuality-[to build communities together.]{.mark} No longer
would we suffer from arbitrary exclusions, such as who needs education
(\"developing world\") and who does not (\"developed world\"). Nor would
we prevent the fruitful syncretism of laws, rules, and ideas from
multiple traditions and worlds. [The sheer joy of creative exploration
and experimentation derived from Worldism\'s critical, syncretic
engagement would help us supercede the banal legitimacy of house
rules.]{.mark}]{.underline}** (5) Accountability Reframes Power.
**[[Power determines the House of IR]{.mark} (\"I impose, you
consent\"). Without power, the house reminds us, we would tremble in a
scary state of nature where there is no shelter, no pater, no mater, and
definitely no servants. [This rationale loses its force when we consider
the insecurity that it produces despite a constant claim to the
opposite]{.mark}.]{.underline}** As the house reinforces its ramparts,
even \"inside\" members like postmodern feminism or
constructivism-pragmatism begin to seem threatening, not to mention
\"wards\" like Asian capitalism and pe- ripheral-transitional economies.
**[Here, [Worldism holds accountable those who erect, maintain, and
insist on such an establishment and]{.mark} ask: \"What are we think-
ing/doing/being, at whose cost, and why?\"]{.underline}** **[We are
moved to [interrogate the meaning and purpose of
power.]{.mark}]{.underline}** \"Whom does it benefit? Whom does it
sacrifice? Why should we mirror imperialism?\" From these queries, a
more meaningful and sus- tainable democracy may arise. It could involve
another conception of the \"house,\" its role and significance: If I
were to name the chief benefit of the house, I should say: the house
shelters daydreaming, the house protects the dreamer, the house allows
one to dream in peace \.... We can perhaps tell everything about the
present, but about the past! The first, the oneirically definitive
house, must retain its shadows. (Bachelard 1964:6, 13) Table 3 compares
these differences between the House of IR and Worldism. **[Many may
question our optimism. How could Worldism retain its intellectual and
political integrity given the power politics of the discipline as
outlined above?]{.underline}** Cynthia Weber (1999b:443) has noted that
mainstream IR tends to \"incorporate\" alien theories by turning them
into lesser, domesticated venues for \"enhanc\[ing\] scientific
disciplinary knowledges of IR rather than in any way transform\[ing\]
them.\" **[We cannot predict or control the course of an idea.
Contestations, contradictions, and crises will always follow.
Nonetheless, we suggest that Worldism offers a significant departure
from previous \"alien\" theories explored in IR.]{.underline}** First,
**[[Worldism does not simply talk about including Others but directly
integrates non-Western epistemologies and ontologies into
theory-building and political activism.]{.underline}]{.mark}** Else-
where (Agathangelou and Ling 2004b), we discuss how poisies from ancient
Greece resonates with Buddhism\'s pratitya samutpada (co-dependent
arising). **[[When the Other voices itself, neither Self nor
Other]{.mark}, theory-building nor practical politics, [can remain the
same.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[Even Osama bin Laden, whose
imperialistic rhetoric mirrors George Bush\'s, has changed IR by his
authorship of another voice, another vision, and another
politics.]{.underline}** **[Furthermore, Worldism aims to redress
structural and material asymmetries. It is not captured by elite
politics whether in the discipline, as a source of knowledge production,
or as a field of practical politics.]{.underline}** **[Rather, [Worldism
\"vivifies\" the perspectives, interests, and actions of those who are
usually marginalized, exploited, or exiled by the House of
IR.]{.mark}]{.underline}** In so doing, Worldism is alert to the
seductive yet ultimately self-delusional \"hyperreal\" politics
identified by some postmodernists (cf. Baudrillard 2002).
**[Furthermore, open-ended queries rather than declarative statements
drive Worldism. This orientation keeps [the House of IR]{.mark} in
perspective. It [becomes one of many sites, not the only one, at which
knowledge or politics is produced.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Lastly, as
shown by our tripartite analysis, IR is not limited to one arena, that
is, the discipline. Although consistencies align IR as a discipline with
its production of knowledge and implementation in practical politics,
change could occur at one site (e.g., knowledge production) with lagged
effects in the other two (e.g., the discipline, practical politics).
Indeed, Weber\'s observation of main- stream IR\'s supposed resilience
may be accurate for now but overly pessimistic in the long run. Our time
frame for making such judgments may be unnecessarily short. **[Worldism,
in brief, unleashes the poisies of IR.]{.underline}** **[[Worldism
openly declares its desire and interest to realize another world where
wealth would not accrue to a privileged few at the expense of the
many]{.mark}.]{.underline}** It would be a world, moreover, that
reflects col- laboration at all levels, \"out there\" and \"in here,\"
to sustain an open, inviting, and profoundly democratic environment that
is free of necessity and rich with poisies \"everywhere.\"

## 1AC -- REFUSE YOUR KIN (BLACK MATRIARCH) 

#### US foreign military sales and direct commercial sales of arms are premised on relational ties -- these bonds serve US self interests both economically and geopolitically, and contribute to a geospatial imaginary premised on territorial egoism.

**Perkins & Neumayer 10** \[R. Perkins & E. Neumayer. "The organized
hypocrisy of ethical foreign policy: Human rights, democracy and Western
arms sales." *Geoforum*, 42(2), 254-256 //tjb\]

**[Ideas of new beginnings, agendas and [spatial horizons have long been
a feature of]{.mark} the [geopolitical
imagination]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Agnew and Corbridge, 1995). Since
the early-1990s, one such imaginary (re-) scripted by Western political
elites is that they have moved away from the state-centric agenda of the
Cold War era, dominated by purely self-interested concerns for domestic
economy, security and power. **[[According to the rhetoric, a more
ethically-grounded foreign policy has taken]{.mark} its [place,
underpinned by universal moral principles]{.mark} and a commitment to
promote the protection of rights and freedoms of extra-territorial
citizens.]{.underline}** **[Critical geographers have been quick to
criticize [these]{.mark} and similar [socio-spatial imaginaries]{.mark}.
Amongst others, they have argued that such scripts [have]{.mark} often
[performed covert, instrumental roles.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[That
is, [rather than marking a more progressive]{.mark} moral [turn]{.mark}
in foreign policy action, [they are]{.mark} part-and-parcel of [a
discursive strategy]{.mark} which seeks [to legitimize existing security
agendas, economic interests and]{.mark} patterns of [domination over
space]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Flint and Falah, 2004; O'Loughlin and
Kolossov, 2002; ÓTuathail, 2000; Power, 2003; Roberts et al., 2003;
Sparke, 2007). In the present paper, we set-out to scrutinize the
alleged gap between political imaginaries and material outcomes, using
the example of Western arms exports to developing countries. **[Our
basic thesis is that [states' official commitment to a value-based arms
controls policy is]{.mark} largely a matter of [symbolic]{.mark}
politics [-- with countries reluctant to sacrifice national interests of
economy and security]{.mark} in name of []{.mark}protecting or advancing
the rights and freedoms of extra-territorial citizens.]{.underline}**
Using quantitative data on large weapons transfers from France, Germany,
the UK and the US over the period 1992--2004, we find evidence to
support our arguments. **[According to our statistical estimations,
[neither human rights abuses nor autocratic polity]{.mark} would appear
to [reduce the likelihood of countries receiving Western arms,]{.mark}
or reduce the relative share of a particular exporter's weapons they
receive. In fact, [human rights abusing countries are]{.mark} actually
[more likely to receive weapons from the US,]{.mark}]{.underline}**
while autocratic regimes emerge as more likely recipients of weaponry
from France and the UK. Reinforcing the impression that
extra-territorial ethical considerations are far from para- mount, **[we
show that [the geography of Western arms exports is]{.mark} closely
[aligned with self-interested, economically and geopolitically-important
relational ties.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[Hence developing countries
which are more important trading partners exhibit a greater probability
of qualifying for arms, as generally do countries which fulfill the
domestic interests of the supplying state extra-territorially as
military allies. [Western countries have not]{.mark}, it seems,
systematically [acted counter to]{.mark} more [conventional, materialist
interests]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Our statistical estimations say
nothing about underlying causal- ity, but it would be difficult to
square the above findings with behavioural explanations based solely on
hegemony. If power were indeed the decisive variable, then the US should
logically be ex- pected to have shown a greater propensity to defy
international normative roles than the three European states, both in
the case of human rights and democracy. **[For sure, [the US hardly acts
in the spirit of its self-declared ethical
principles]{.mark},]{.underline}** but nor does France or the UK. Both
sets of countries have systematically contravened one principle or
another. Germany fares only slightly better with respect to democracy,
but not human rights protection. What seems to matter more, at least in
defining what states can or cannot get away with, are domestic
institutions (Donnelly and Liang-Fenton, 2004). That the US is more
willing to overtly ignore human rights abuses than autocratic government
is broadly consis- tent with the idea that the country is, first and
foremost, founded on democratic ideals rather than humanitarian ones
(Moss, 1995). Although far from irrelevant, the domestic appropriateness
of defending or promoting democracy in non-domestic spaces would appear
to be less deeply institutionalized in Britain and France than in the
US. **[Instead, [partly as a consequence of their historic role in
colonialism, norms of extra-territorial responsibility are]{.mark}
centrally [defined around human rights.]{.mark}]{.underline}**13 The
idea that domes- tic institutions matter is also supported by the German
case -- the country with the ''least bad" record regarding arms
transfers. Ger- many's foreign policy identity, which was formed in the
shadow of an excessively autocratic, militaristic and human rights
violating past, is one where economic or geostrategic concerns are least
likely to trump the promotion of Western values (Erb, 2003). What are
the implications of our findings? **[Most obviously, by
[exposing]{.mark} the [hypocrisy underlying]{.mark} geopolitical
imaginaries of [ethical foreign policy]{.mark}, they [remind us of the
importance of interrogating]{.mark} the [discursive constructions of
political leaders]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Flint and Falah, 2004;
ÓTuathail, 1992; Roberts et al., 2003). **[[While]{.mark} a [more
ethically-grounded foreign policy may script a geopolitics of
hope]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Sparke, 2007), **[[underpinned by]{.mark} a
deterritorialised [concern for the well-being of]{.mark} a more
spatially inclusive, [cosmopolitan humanity, our findings suggest
that]{.mark} such [socio-spatial narratives]{.mark} (potentially)
[conceal a geopolitics of territorial egoism.]{.mark}]{.underline}** In
an important sense, therefore, our study speaks to wider debates about
the changing boundaries of the modern state. Although it has become
popular to talk about a blurring of domestic and foreign, and the
emergence of a post-sovereign state identity, the findings of the
present study suggest that it is important not to take this argument too
far. Aspirationally and rhetorically, Western countries may subscribe to
a non-territorial conception of rights and duties, and envision a state
whose interests lie in promoting universal values across borders. Yet if
the case of arms control is anything to go by, these universals have not
generally dislodged the primacy of national economic and geopolitical
interests. Far from the borderless world envisaged by hyperglobalists,
territorial borders would appear to continue to enframe economic and
geostrategic interests, and centrally guide Western states' foreign
policy actions (Yeung, 1998).

#### Normative political calls for arms control frequently take for granted the who, why, and how of arms control, securitizing space through an us/them dichotomy that frames the United States as a domestic homeland vulnerable to foreign threats. It is critical that we investigate the representational economy of arms control discourses, as taken for granted presuppositions about the foreign and domestic follow patterns of geopolitical thought that evacuate the global South from both policy and scholarly analysis and form the moral basis of political campaigns for and against arms control.

**Dalby 11 \[Simon Dalby; Critical Geopolitics and the Control of Arms
in the 21^st^ Century; April 2011; Date accessed 3 July\] DG**

**[[Who controls which arms and why is essential to the]{.mark} future
[geopolitical order]{.mark} of the planet in so far as the threat of the
use of military force is part of the political calculus in coming
decades.]{.underline}** **[[The question of who controls arms is related
to where]{.mark} that ['who' resides.]{.mark} [The world, and how it
might be changed, does not look the same from Washington as]{.mark} it
does in [Tehran]{.mark}, nor [Cairo]{.mark}, [Moscow or Delhi]{.mark},
and [the]{.mark} common [assumption]{.mark}s [that]{.mark} the [American
dominance]{.mark} in geopolitical matters [is]{.mark} necessarily
[permanent isn't one that can be taken for granted]{.mark} in any
discussion of geopolitics for the 21st century.]{.underline}** A world
of sovereign equals, or one with a multiplicity of great powers would
apparently have a rather different set of arms control priorities than
one dominated by one superpower. The assumptions about a desirable world
order in the 21st century are not universally shared, but they matter
greatly as the premises of political conduct and the rationale for
foreign policy making in particular. The logic of American preeminence
was the key theme in the Bush Doctrine that dominated the first decade
of the century.1 There was a very distinctive notion of controlling arms
in its strategies of preeminence. Crucial in its innovations in American
thinking were the unavoidable necessities of dealing with globalization
and the clear recognition that the geopolitics of the 20th century had
been bypassed by new developments. And yet, despite the explicit
formulation of the geopolitical context as a matter of global war, some
very old-fashioned state rivalries persisted, not least the apparent
attempt to gain some form of nuclear strategic superiority over Russia,
and an explicit containment attempt on Iran ostensibly designed to stop
the regime gaining nuclear weapons. Arms control in that context was
about controlling arms that might challenge American hegemony. From the
beginning of the Obama administration in Washington in 2009 there was a
more obvious inclination to weapons reduction, and non-proliferation and
arms reduction themes reemerged in serious diplomatic discussion. An
ambitious agenda of comprehensive nuclear weapons test ban treaty
ratification in the Senate, a new strategic arms reduction treaty with
Russia, and the goal of eliminating nuclear weapons by strengthening the
nuclear non-proliferation treaty were all spelled out in a speech in
April 2009 in Prague.2 But less clear is how these policies will deal
with the apparently recalcitrant peripheral states in the world system
much less the 'nuclear outlaws' of India, Pakistan and Israel who more
or less operate outside the international regimes on these matters.
**[How all this fits into a reshaped geopolitical order in the longer
term is key to the question of what kind of arms control policies are
sought by whom and where.]{.underline}** Nor is it clear that the locus
of arms control initiatives is necessarily driven only by state
priorities. The most interesting point about President Obama's speech in
Prague is in the part that invoked his own political themes of change,
and change driven by popular demand. Invoking the experiences of both
the Prague Spring of 1968 and the Velvet Revolution of 1989, Obama
suggested that popular demand for nuclear disarmament was crucial, that
the refusal to accept nuclear armed rivalries were key to the future of
geopolitics; [**[resistance is not useless]{.underline}**.]{.mark} It
is, he once again argued, essential to political change. **[But [change
to what kind of a world]{.mark} for the 21st century [is the question
that hangs over discussions of the technical practices of arms
control]{.mark} and the finer points of diplomatic
agreements[.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[The other point that is [implicit
in calls for arms control]{.mark}, in President Obama's speeches, and in
very many other places, [is the]{.mark} crucial [specification of the
world as a dangerous place,]{.mark} and hence in need of arms
control[.]{.mark} But [precisely who is threatening to whom
where]{.mark} is frequently less clearly specified not least because it
[is]{.mark} frequently [taken for granted in political rhetoric.]{.mark}
But as the rest of this paper suggests, [focussing]{.mark} on those
taken for granted contextualizations, [on the implicit]{.mark}, and
sometimes explicit, [geographical framing of the world]{.mark} as
needing certain forms of arms control, [is a useful analytical way of
clarifying how the politics of arms control
works.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Many of the new innovations in Washington
retain the geopolitical assumptions of American hegemony. In Andrew
Bacevich's terms they follow the longheld foreign policy credo in which
American thinking has 'an abiding conviction that the minimum essentials
of international peace and order require the United States to maintain a
global military presence, to configure its forces for global power
projection, and to counter existing or anticipated threats by relying on
a policy of global interventionism'.3 Nonetheless, and this is the key
point in the rest of this paper, the geography of violence, and the
technological means of its application have changed very substantially
since the Cold War.4 **[Thinking through how these new circumstances,
only loosely caught in the terms 'globalization' on the one hand and
'human security' on the other, are tied into campaigns both by civil
society and states to control arms matters both as a matter of policy
and a matter of scholarly analysis of the new geopolitical circumstances
of the 21st century.]{.underline}** What is clear is that a combination
of the inter-related phenomena of urbanization, climate change,
migration, financial crises, technological innovation and the rapidly
shifting geography of global production is changing how geopolitics
works. The rise of multilateral institutions emphasizes how inadequate
nationalist political visions are in these circumstances, however
tempting they are for politicians mustering domestic support for various
policies. CR IT ICAL GEO POL IT IC S AND THE CONTROL O F ARM S 41

seeking unilateral advantage by the control of arms cooperate uneasily
with social movement campaigners who frequently advocate the total
abolition of whole classes of weapons. Where arms are campaigned against
and why is the obvious first question that arises. Who organizes
protests, and who runs which political organizations is a related
question.11 Which weapons are owned and used by whom where and for what
ostensible reason matters too. Likewise, clearly in the case of land
mines and the recent agreement on cluster bombs, the questions of
victims, and the obvious point that weapons that violate the 'laws of
war' in particular by not discriminating between combatants and
civilians, are especially worthy of condemnation and hence being
controlled. Which states sign on, and which ones don't, and where
matters greatly. Critical geopolitics is about these contextualisations
of security, danger, fear and violence, and as such it should have much
to say because the details of arms control negotiations are so
frequently fraught with the overlapping problems that individual states
see in terms of complex threat perceptions in particular regions, only
most obviously in the case of the Middle East.12 **[This is not only a
matter of challenging the obviously self serving strategies of
attempting to accomplish unilateral disarmament by coercion as in the
cases of Iran and North Korea, but also a matter of the larger
discourses that assume the inevitability of eternal rivalry between
territorial states, part of why arms control appears to be a necessity
in the first place.]{.underline}** **[In quizzing the taken for granted
specifications of violence and its purported
geographies,]{.underline}**13 **[to tackle the implicit geopolitics that
structures the discussion of arms control, and in particular the fears
of proliferation that have preoccupied Washington so intensely in the
last few years, [a critical geopolitics]{.mark} should be able to
[suggest some lines of criticism that unsettle the dominant terms of the
arms control discussion.]{.mark}]{.underline}** But, with the notable
exception of Spiegel and Le Billon's recent paper, little has been done
explicitly on arms control.14 The rest of this paper is thus a
preliminary exploration that suggests that explicitly thinking about the
contextual dimensions of arms control adds a critical edge to any
analysis of likely trends by bluntly asking the geographical questions
of precisely whom these policies benefit where. Looking to the
geopolitical contextualizations implicit, and sometimes explicit, in the
contemporary discussions of such things as strategic arms limitation,
the current angst over Tehran and nuclear technology, the question of
nuclear weapons free zones, the South Asia situation, and such things as
the international humanitarian campaigns against land mines and, more
recently, cluster munitions, emphasizes the importance of those
contextualizations in arms control discussions. CR IT ICAL GEO POL IT IC
S AND THE CONTROL O F ARM S 43 Downloaded By: \[Dalby, Simon\] At: 14:51
26 May 2011 Arms Control Clearly arms control is a political process,
and a technical one too. After all it should never be forgotten that
states are very keen to control weapons they don't own or use but which
might be used against them, and not so keen on agreeing to binding
agreements to limit weapons that they obviously gain some perceived
advantage from deploying. Activists campaign for nuclear free zones, and
such things as international bans on the production and deployment of
land mines and cluster bombs, not least because they violate
humanitarian norms and the differentiation between combatants and
civilians. States seek the control of chemical and bacteriological
weapons among other things, but have a decidedly selective attention
span to weapons abolition. Nowhere more so than in the case of nuclear
weapons where comprehensive disarmament is the avowed goal of many
states but not a practical matter for most of those who have the
weapons.15 Geopolitical context explains much but not all of these
matters; threat perceptions and national pride matter too it seems and
as Ken Booth warned long ago, ethnocentrism continues to haunt strategic
matters. 16 **[[How weapons are represented in the discourses of
international politics,]{.mark} [how the contexts in which they are used
are portrayed,]{.mark} both by states using the weapons, and by
campaigners wishing to ban them, [matter in the practical politics of
weapons control.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[So too do the mundane
practicalities of international trade and the logics of states
supporting national industries; where trade trumps humanitarian concern
the logics of protest, and norm construction are made more complex, but
nonetheless they matter in the politics of all this.]{.underline}**17
**[They do so in part because [representational politics is part of the
politics of norm construction]{.mark} and crucially to the norm cascades
[that turn moral concerns into political campaigns]{.mark} that
formulate such things as the treaty to ban
landmines[.]{.mark}]{.underline}**18 **[The politics of this case was
driven by a reframing of landmines from weapons of war to indiscriminate
killers of the innocent.]{.underline}** Once formulated in these terms
their legitimacy was undermined and an international agreement to
prohibit their production and use gathered momentum. Such popular
protests and international campaigns get very difficult where
international campaigns run into the foreign policy priorities of
particular states, and where international campaigns are understood by
particular governments to be the foreign policy of other states by other
means. The classic example from the Cold War was the accusations from
both sides of the divide that peace movements on the other side were
concerned to undermine security.19 Our weapons, being purely defensive,
could never be a cause that sincere campaigners would tackle after all!
Drawing a line around a territorial designation to limit or restrict the
use or transport of particular weapons, usually nuclear ones, is a mode
of arms control. States have attempted to use these international
agreements to restrict the spread of nuclear weapons in many parts of
the world.20 Implicit in the spread of international nuclear weapons
free zones is the failure of the existing non-proliferation regime to
constrain the spread of nuclear weapons.21 In the 1980s the hope of
nuclear weapons free campaigners was that this territorial strategy
would gradually restrict the use and transport of nuclear weapons, and
in the process extend the international norms condemning these devices
in a manner that would bring political pressure to bear on the 44
CONTEMPORARY SECUR ITY POL ICY Downloaded By: \[Dalby, Simon\] At: 14:51
26 May 2011 superpowers to get serious about disarmament. While the
numbers of weapons in Russian and American inventories have decreased
dramatically from their Cold War levels, at least so far, the nuclear
non-proliferation treaty still seems to be more about maintaining the
political status quo than eliminating weapons. [What caused greatest
official concern in the United States in the period of the 1980s wasn't
the spread of nuclear weapons, but concerns that states would follow New
Zealand in declaring themselves nuclear free zones. This might offer
political constraints on the operation of US military forces, and this
mode of arms control was obviously a threat to superpower political
freedom of action in so far as international basing rights and rights of
passage were to be followed.]{.underline} The New Zealand arguments
suggested that the greater danger to New Zealand came from the existence
of these weapons and the potential use of them in major war.22 As such
the anti-nuclear campaign in the 1980s explicitly tackled the implicit
geopolitical rationalizations of nuclear strategy and the assumption
that American extended nuclear deterrence actually provided security.
Thus the clashing geopolitical premises of the debate suggest that the
geographical understandings of security that structure attempts to
control arms are far from innocent in this discussion. Indeed they seem
to be key to understanding which international agreements are negotiated
and why many apparently sensible moves that might reduce the risks of
conflict, or at least limit the casualties and damage should hostilities
occur, aren't made. **[[Such discussions focus attention on]{.mark} the
most obvious and taken for granted [assumptions about the geopolitical
order]{.mark} of our times, about [how the world political map is
structured and]{.mark} about [who is using violence for what
ends.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[In the aftermath of the discussions in
December 2009 in Copenhagen concerning climate change where promises of
a comprehensive agreement on limiting emissions of greenhouse gases so
spectacularly failed, and the continuous low level violence related to
the military actions of what President Obama refuses to call the War on
Terror, the big questions of the locus of political authority and what
kind of political order is emerging in the 21st century, are the
essential backdrop for any discussion of attempts to limit
arms.]{.underline}** Much of the discussion in the 20th century was
about limiting the proliferation of certain kinds of weapons, only most
obviously nuclear, chemical and biological ones. While there were
obvious humanitarian impulses to reduce the potential for violence there
was an overarching priority on the part of the superpowers to manoeuvre
in such a way that arms control didn't undermine their dominance in
international rivalries. Preventing weapons proliferating meant
maintaining strategic dominance during the Cold War. This crucial theme
has been perpetuated since in the military strategies of the remaining
superpower; the United States spends somewhere close to half of all the
total of the military budgets on the planet, and its military is
designed to be preeminent.

#### The House of International Relations dictates who ought to possess US arms, acquired through the legitimate means of sale, calculating legitimacy through a regime of white racial kinship that requires aspirations to the existing international order in order to become adopted in. Thus the House of IR is the foundation of the topic -- state and non-state actors alike must funnel their limited prescriptions of agency through the proper channels or else risk losing their kin. This process is gendered and racialized through the lens of the Western, white nuclear family.

**[[IR]{.mark}, we argue, [operates like a colonial

**[[Staking out "civilization" in a space that's already crowded with

regardless of empire's devastations and/or secret

The House of IR exhibits a similar politics of exclusion and
violence.**[3 [It]{.mark} clearly [identiﬁes who's "in," who's "out,"
and who's precariously "on the border." It]{.mark} also [stratiﬁes who's
"upstairs" and who's "downstairs."]{.mark} [This hierarchical division
of space reﬂects]{.mark} the House's [participation in and complicity
with material relations of production and its uneven distribution of
social wealth.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[As its "founding father,"
[realism heads the House]{.mark} of IR.]{.underline}**4 Though labeled a

intellectual lineage that dates to ancient Greek history, especially
that recorded by Thucydides (5 bc) (cf. Lebow and Strauss 1991; Garst
1989; Jansson 1997; Monten 2006).5 Subsequently, realists have added
luminaries of power politics like Machiavelli and Hobbes to their
roster. But it was a generation of scholars from post-World War II
Europe, Britain, and the US that gave realism its distinctive voice.
**[Power, they asserted, reﬂects objective laws of calculated (sometimes
amoral) self-interest for all states across time and
space.]{.underline}** John Herz, George F. Kennan, Walter Lippmann, and
Hans J. Morgenthau each saw "political realism" as the cure to what they
considered the fateful cause of war among nations: "legalism,"
"utopianism," and "idealism" (Keohane 1986: 9). **[[Abstract
individualism pivots realism's understanding of the world.]{.mark}
Primordial individual units ([states]{.mark}) [struggle in eternal
competition over resources]{.mark} that confer power and wealth. [In
this way, realists justify instrumental reasoning and
behavior.]{.mark}]{.underline}** But then how do realists explain the
existence of an interdependent international political economy in a
world of conﬂicting nation-states? Hegemonic stability theory comes to
the rescue (Kindleberger 1973). **[It posits a dominant state or hegemon
that willingly bears the asymmetrical cost of global leadership so the
inter-state system may survive peacefully and prosperously despite
anarchy and competition. [Game theoretic models explain]{.mark} such

philosophical traditions, including Confucianism.11 Nor does Nussbaum

what she claims to be Aristotelian logic. **[Because Nussbaum sees
"third-world traditions," particularly in their patriarchal form, as
obstacles to the full realization of personhood through choice, she
implicitly rationalizes their dismissal (to put it mildly) or extinction
(to put it more harshly)]{.underline}**. **[This "civilizing mission,"

policed as \[the Other\]]{.underline}**" (Stoler 2002: 60). **[For this
reason, while neoliberalism, liberal feminism, and standpoint feminism
may ﬁnd social and political emancipation appealing and a seeming basis
for solidarity with the Other, they are bound, nonetheless, to the

Other refers to non-Western, non-white sources of knowledge, traditions,

"wards" of the House but]{.underline}**]{.mark} **[otherwise [not

#1 intrigues the most. Heir apparent to the House of IR, neorealism

Waltz (1979), for instance, constructs the international system as a
