tensions between neorealism and the other progeny of the House of IR
remain severe.

#### The House of IR is premised on the existence of the sovereign subject, thereby reproducing the subject of politics as ontologically European, masculine, and propertied. The existence of this subject demands the construction of colonized zones and zones of gratuitous violence. Because only propertied subjects can come into political being within this House, its internationality presumes a universality that is not truly global in reach. In other words, the House of IR requires a segregated asymmetrical World order, licensing permanent war.

**Agathangelou 11** \[Anna Agathangelou, "Bodies to the Slaughter:
Global Racial Reconstructions, Fanon's Combat Breath, and Wrestling for
Life." *Somatechnics* 1 (1), 209-248 //tjb\]

When Foucault began to write and joined the French communist party,
anti-colonial struggles and revolutionary projects were brutally evident
worldwide including Algeria. Fanon's famous book Black Skin, White Masks
(1967a) was published in 1952, two years before Foucault published his
dissertation (1954). But before this, Fanon fought as a Frenchman
against the Nazis. Macey (2000) writes that he was cited for
'distinguished conduct' and awarded the Croix de Guerre with a bronze
star. [Fanon's experience of French republicanism in Martinique, France
and Algeria convinced him that seeking to include/integrate slaves,
blacks, or the colonised within the historical strictures of imperial
political (universal) orders and their contingent humanist projects was
not viable because of their slave-racio-colonial underpinnings, or what
Achille Mbembe]{.underline} (2001: 24) [calls the imperial
commandment.]{.underline} Foucault published Maladie mentale et
personnalite ́ (1954) two years after the two major American political
parties put aside their differing geopolitical interests to support NATO
and other international institutions, recognising that such structures
could link the United States to the prevailing world order and expand
its influence worldwide (Kupchan 2009).3 In this work, Foucault
recognises that modernity (and its liberal humanist foundations) is a
problem. Even so, he is ambivalent about undertaking a genealogy
(Muppidi 2009; Grovogui 2007; Trouillot 2005)4 of the emergence of
liberal sovereignty to demonstrate that, historically, capitalism is a
zero-sum game in the exploitation of territories and peoples.
**[[Critical theorists in]{.mark} International Relations ([IR]{.mark})
such as Ashley (1988) and Weber (1999) recruit Foucault to understand
global power but remain mostly within the deconstruction of the liberal
interstate structure. IR Marxists problematise these analyses and others
that articulate the emerging global sovereignty as 'an international
state dominated by the US', not guided by a single biopolitical 'logic
of rule']{.underline}** (Kiersey 2009: 35). These **[IR Marxist
critiques are, thus, more concerned with the imperial genealogy of
globalisation and the American state's articulation and ordering of the
world.]{.underline}**5 **[While Foucault is critiqued by IR Marxists
regarding his theorisation of the formation of the imperial, he
demonstrated its connections with political economy, biopolitics, and
power in a series of lectures between 1975 and 1979: 'Society Must be
Defended', 'Security, Territory, Population' and 'The Birth of
Biopolitics']{.underline}** (Foucault 2004, 2008). **[Nevertheless, his
theorisations are embedded in the French liberal and a white supremacy
project, so that his analyses [are delimited by a universalisation that
is not truly global]{.mark}]{.underline}**6 **[[because it presumes the
structuring and subject of politics to be ontologically European,
masculine, and propertied.]{.mark} For instance, he does not explicitly
address French colonialism.]{.underline}** How do those who read his
texts today feel this silence? Said, for one, compares him to Fanon to
critique his narrowly French experience: 'Fanon represents the interests
of a double constituency, native and Western, moving from confinement to
liberation; ignoring the imperial context of his own theories, Foucault
seems actually to represent an irresistible colonizing movement that
paradoxically fortifies the prestige of both the lonely individual
scholar and the system that contains him' (Said 1994: 278). In
re-reading Foucault with Fanon,7 Said gestures to the limits of his and
other Western writers' work whose contributions are at once
indispensable and inadequate in their understanding of the realities of
(neo) colonial and global multiple worlds. Why is Fanon 'contestatory',
whereas Foucault is not? How are these contestations crucial for today's
struggles, specifically the crises of governance in the postwar liberal
international order? **[[What if our vantage]{.mark} point [in
critiquing practices, theories, and normative orders began with the
violence of the wretched,]{.mark} 'laying the groundwork for a theory of
antagonism']{.underline}** (Wilderson 2010: 7) **[[where demands and
claims cannot be satisfied by simply transferring ownership]{.mark} of
an organisation [within existing strictures]{.mark}]{.underline}**
(Wilderson 2005: 7**[)[?]{.mark} [Fanon]{.mark} scales colonisation to
the level of the slave and colonised body. He illustrates the
incommensurability of the intimate encounter of black flesh with the
body of the coloniser and focuses on the structuring processes required
to make it possible. He [begins his critique with the]{.mark} normative
[imperial order of slavery and colonisation and]{.mark} those [humanist
interventions claiming to protect the sovereign subject.]{.mark} He
tells us that [the constitution of this]{.mark} sovereign [subject
depends on an asymmetrical segregated-order]{.mark}]{.underline}**: This
world divided into compartments, this world cut in two is inhabited by
two different species \... When you examine at close quarters the
colonial context, it is evident that what parcels out the world is to
begin with the fact of belonging to a given race, a given species . . .
The cause is the consequence; you are rich because you are white, you
are white because you are rich. (Fanon 1967d: 39--40, emphasis in
original) Fanon points out that this order's constitution depends on
direct violence that turns a species into slaves, black, and colonised.
**[[This violence makes it possible for zones to become 'civil' spaces
of]{.mark} 'generalized trust' and [security for]{.mark} the
[sovereigns; the species occupying them]{.mark} possess 'generalized
trust' and [are]{.mark} racially [white.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[This
relation ends up being taken for granted: [belonging to a given race of
property relations is the precondition for any 'civil'
encounter.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Indeed, as Wilderson argues, 'Fanon
makes clear how some are zoned, a priori, beyond the borders of
generalized trust' (Wilderson 2010: 33). **[[The establishment of
gratuitous violence zones, positions and constitutes simultaneously the
species and the colonised.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Further, 'the
condition of possibility upon which subjectivity' (Fanon 1967d: 39--40)
is based must be recognised and theorised. **[[The creation of colonised
zones, the interstate state system, racialised whiteness, and property
relations require theorising if we are to disrupt]{.mark} those
relations which unify and entify a [normative 'ethical
order']{.mark}.]{.underline}** Fanon, of course, is clear: without the
vertical existence of breath, that is, giving one's breath as
nourishment for blackness, slavery, and colonisation, there is no such
order. **[[This order, even when it claims inclusion, segregates
subjects of recognition from 'species'.]{.mark} [Subjects are positioned
into the interstate structure of worlds with sovereign protection, able
to take by force and accumulate anything, from things to life
itself.]{.mark}]{.underline}** Fanon seems to have anticipated Foucault
who argues: 'Power is employed and exercised through a net-like
organisation. And not only do individuals circulate between its threads;
they are always in the position of simultaneously undergoing and
exercising power \... The individual \... is not the vis-a\`-vis of
power; it is I believe, one of its prime effects' (Foucault 1980: 98).
However, Fanon does not begin with this prime effect of power, as he
wants us to learn to read social relations, racism, and economies of
violence as if experiencing our own gratuitous violence, in an attempt
to think the impossible place of the slave, the black body, and the
colonised -- in other words, the living being whose existence is already
assumed as structurally impossible and, hence, as breath which can never
be synonymous with life. **[[The basis of the (inter) state
structure]{.mark}, Fanon recognises, [is]{.mark} already [the juristic
sovereign person who]{.mark}se essence, or what Goodrich calls the
sovereign that the state has a right to kill, [is already secured
from]{.mark} the threat of [mutilation.]{.mark}]{.underline}** On the
one hand, Foucault (1990: 138) asks this about state power: 'How could
power exercise its highest prerogative by putting people to death, when
its main role was to ensure, sustain, and multiply life, to put this
life in order?' On the other hand, Fanon makes explicit the matrix of
violence which requires and makes sure that species are zoned as black
and colonised: 'Individualism is the first to disappear \... the
colonialist bourgeoisie had hammered into the native's mind the idea of
a society of individuals where each person shuts himself up in his own
subjectivity, and whose only wealth is individual' (Fanon 1967d: 47):
Their first encounter was marked by violence and their existence
together -- that is to say the exploitation of the native by the settler
-- was carried on by a dint of a great array of bayonets and cannons.
**[The settler and the native are old acquaintances. In fact, the
settler is right when he speaks of knowing 'them' well. For it is the
settler who has brought the native into existence and who perpetuates
his existence. The settler owes the fact of his very existence, that is
to say, his property, to the colonial system.]{.underline}** (Fanon
1967d: 36) But why such insistence? What tension does Fanon want to
foreground? Fanon actually has a different 'locus of enunciation' and
insists on a long trajectory of the effects of the imperial, colonial,
and slave order and vertical relations of what he calls 'combat breath'
(Fanon 1967c: 65). **[By drawing out Fanon's idea of 'combat breath' and
articulating it as struggles that disrupt the practices of violence and
the final destruction of countries and people, we see that [enforcing
the right to life of the radical individual]{.mark} (the propertied man
of a structure of white supremacy which depends on slavery and
colonisation) [will authorise thanatopolitics and
necroeconomics,]{.mark} not by suspending a right to life but rather [by
enforcing a right to that 'liberal' life.]{.mark} But [this]{.mark}
minimalist right to life [could preclude crucial relations in the
everyday continuum-spaces of the human and the non- human]{.mark},
including ecologies [and it does by deploying practices of disfigurement
and destruction.]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[Fanon exposes the imperial
European re-assemblage of power and demonstrates that [state power
shifts are connected to the emergence of an 'international' order and
apparatuses that make possible a particular sovereign-master-colonising
subject.]{.mark}]{.underline}** In his view, colonial power says: 'Since
you want independence, take it and starve \... A regime of austerity is
imposed on these starving men; a disproportionate amount of work is
required for their atrophied muscles' (Fanon 1967d: 96). **[Fanon notes
the prevalence of suffocation and starvation in world politics, the
devouring of the flesh and the subsequent redistribution of its
existential vital energy that is turned into wealth]{.underline}**.
Amelioration requires more than changing working conditions and setting
up less exploitative structures (such as socialism and communism).
Rather, it requires 'regime\[s\] which \[are\] completely oriented
toward the people as a whole' which prioritise the principle 'that man
is the most precious of all possessions'.8 Such a locus will preclude
'that caricature of society where all economic and political power is
held in the hands of the few who regard the nation as whole with scorn
and contempt' (Fanon 1967d: 98). **[[War]{.mark}, for both Fanon and
Foucault, [is part of the violent process that makes it possible to
'rule over the ordering of the colonial world']{.mark}]{.underline}**
(Fanon 1967d: 44). **[Fanon posits that war involves
politico-ontological claims whereas Foucault wonders about the war that
gave birth to the state. For him, '[peace itself is a coded war. We
are]{.mark} therefore [at war with one another; a battlefront runs
through]{.mark} the whole of [society, continuously and permanently,
and]{.mark} it is this battlefront that [puts us all on one side or the
other']{.mark}]{.underline}** (Foucault 2003: 50). He adds, 'war is both
the web and the secret of institutions and systems of powers' (2003:
110--11). For his part, Fanon knows that war's effects on the species
are horrendous. Fanon discusses the case of two Algerian boys, 13 and
14, who stab a European classmate to death, in order to make the point
that, like the European colonialist, the black slave may shirk his/her
humanity by pursuing violence to establish political equality. The
13-year-old describes the dead child as 'our best friend', targeted
simply because he was a friend and thus easily lured to his death. The
pair is remorseless. Fanon speaks of the racial tensions in this case
and recounts 'long conversations' with the 14-year-old, who describes
the murder as an act of revenge: 'I wanted to take to the mountains, but
I'm too young. So \[the other boy\] and I said . . . we would kill a
European'. 'Why'? 'In your opinion, what do you think we should have
done'? 'I don't know. But you are a child and the things that are going
on are for grown-ups'. 'But they kill children, too'. 'But that's no
reason for killing your friend'. 'Well, I killed him. Now you can do
what you like'. 'Did this friend do anything to you'? 'No. He didn't do
anything'. 'Well'? 'That's all there is to it'. (Fanon 1967d: 201) Many
theorists read these passages as confirmation that Fanon is a theorist
who incites violence and proposes it as the only option against
colonisation. Consequently, they underrate the extent to which Fanon, a
'dissident', considers it important to be present completely to the
slave-colonial order and its traumatic effects which are not easily
disentangled, good from bad. For him, this allows a nuanced
understanding of its genesis, violence, and offers the possibility of
transforming systems of valuation ranging from one's breath to universal
orders. In fact, in telling this story, Fanon makes visible the multiple
costs of slavery and colonialism. For him, imperialism is buried in the
bones of the native (Fanon 1967d: 40). He notes the costs of an imperial
and sovereign-democratic form of life, while insisting on the timing and
limitations of alternatives (such as national cultures): 'the violence
which has ruled over the ordering of the colonial world \... will be
claimed and taken over by the native at the moment when, deciding to
embody history in his own person, he surges into the forbidden quarters'
(Fanon 1967d: 40). He says that violence does not necessarily replace a
degraded slave with a self-actualised man. In essence, **[[Fanon invites
us to take a hard look at the myriad ways we avoid the inter-
existentially onto-politically-structured violence in our everyday
lives.]{.mark} Fanon begins his analysis of war by analysing slave and
colonial (structural) violence. For him, war is crucial, but which war?
Is it the distinction between enmity and war between brothers (war
between liberal and democratic states) and war against strangers, the
object of polemos?]{.underline}** **[Or is it the 'rational' war
calculated to enable a 'kind of stabilisation' in the interstate system
(among the colonial sovereign states themselves), thereby evading
questions about the doctrine of imperialism, its forced universal order
(currently led by the US), its reason, and the freedom of the
individual?]{.underline}** **[Fanon unequivocally answers by demanding
an inquiry into a fundamental process of world politics (including
imperial relations, colonial relations, and slave relations) ignored by
those who want to keep this order in place by violence]{.underline}**.
**[For Fanon, [it is not enough to talk of war and the subjects who
fight these wars,]{.mark} as [both war and racial bodies are already
technologies, inscribed in the economy of slave and colonial violence
through which]{.mark} the 'I' ([the subject]{.mark}) [attains
individuality within a politico-juridical order that enforces its
actuality antagonistically]{.mark}; the human body (and not sentient
flesh) and war are already vertically and horizontally positioned as
legitimate forms of human formation.]{.underline}** Fanon concisely
says: '**[Colonialism is not a thinking machine, nor a body endowed with
reasoning faculties. It is violence in its natural state']{.underline}**
(Fanon 1967d: 48). This requires more than biopolitical functions such
as managing bodies and things, life and wealth. Its fundamental grounds
are inter-antagonistically and inter-existentially set up. It is a
relationship that depends on the slave-colonial-racist dynamics that
create and systematically dehumanise and black(en) the person. Fanon
writes: 'the Black man has no ontological resistance in the eyes of the
white man' (Fanon 1952: 33). He adds: Colonialism . . . is violence in
its natural state, and it will only yield when confronted with greater
violence. **[The policeman and the soldier, by their immediate presence
and their frequent and direct action, maintain contact with the native
and advise him by means of rifle butts and napalm not to budge. It is
obvious here that government speaks the language of pure force. The
intermediary does not lighten the oppression nor seek to hide the
domination; he shows them up and puts them into practice with the clear
conscience of an upholder of peace; yet he is the bringer of violence
into the home and into the mind of the native.]{.underline}** (Fanon
1952: 91) Fanon repeatedly articulates this antagonistic relationship as
if he wants to split asunder the dominant and normative belief that
slavery and colonialism consolidate politics to fit all desires and
serve the interests of all.

#### Pro-family discourses deployed by the US against the global South institutionalize colonial rule. The family has been weaponized as an arm in the arsenal of universality, denying the possibility of alternative social arrangements and privileging Western knowledge above all others.

**McEwen 17**. Haley McEwen is a lecturer in the Centre for Law and
Justice, Australian Graduate School of Policing and Security at Charles
Sturt University. She lectures in criminal justice and law. Haley holds
a Bachelor of Arts/Law from the University of Sydney, with a triple
major in English, Psychology and Education, First Class Honours in Law,
and a Masters in Social Work from UNSW \["Nuclear power: The family in
decolonial perspective and 'pro-family' politics in Africa," 2017,
*Development Southern Africa*, DOI:
10.1080/0376835X.2017.1318700\]//vikas

The international pro-family movement

A **[US-driven theo-political]{.underline}** movement calling itself
**[['pro-family' is working to advance policy agendas in Africa, Eastern
Europe, India and Asia]{.mark} through]{.underline}** the establishment
of non-governmental organisations (**[NGOs]{.underline}**)
**[and]{.underline}** increasing advocacy work at **[the]{.underline}**
United Nations (**[UN]{.underline}**). As Croft (2007) and Martin (1999)
have revealed, **[[pro-family foreign policy involves measures
to]{.mark} '[strengthen the family]{.mark}' through opposition to birth
control]{.underline}**, the use of **[condoms]{.underline}** (even in
the fight against HIV/AIDS) **[and denial of]{.underline}** the
**[rights and dignity of sexual minorities.]{.underline}** Furthermore,
'Religious activists have consistently opposed any foreign-policy
initiative that might weaken parental control over children, facilitate
abortion, expand the rights of homosexuals, or devalue the role of the
conventional homemaker and mother' (Martin, 1999:74). The **[growing
influence of an international 'profamily' movement has been noted by
scholars who]{.underline}** have **[studied the [rise of discourses of
the 'natural' or 'traditional' family]{.mark} within domestic contexts
in the West at first]{.underline}**, and more recently their advances
within the UN **[and [across Africa and the Global
South]{.mark}]{.underline}** (Buss & Herman, 2003). Drawing on Christian
beliefs about the sanctity of marriage, pro-family organisations work to
actively 'preserve and promote' the nuclear family model, which is
positioned as the ideal kinship structure. For instance, **[the
[US-based]{.mark} organisation [Family Watch International works with
the UN and internationally to]{.mark}:]{.underline}**
**[[Preserve]{.mark} and promote [the family]{.mark}]{.underline}**,
**[[based on marriage]{.mark} between a man and a woman as the societal
unit that provides the best outcome]{.underline}** for men, women and
children.1 Similarly, Focus on the Family -- which is US based but which
has associate offices in 13 other countries across Asia, Latin America,
Europe, the Middle East and Africa -- sets itself out as: a global
Christian ministry dedicated to helping families thrive. We provide help
and resources for couples to build healthy marriages that reflect God's
design, and for parents to raise their children according to morals and
values grounded in biblical principles.2 **[The
[WCF]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[a project of]{.mark} the
[Illinois-based Howard Centre]{.mark}]{.underline}** for Family,
Religion and Society, **[was established]{.underline}** in 1997 as an
initiative **[to consolidate]{.underline}** various **[pro-life and
anti-gay advocacy organisations]{.underline}**, and to bring together
global conservatives (and elites) who can advance 'family values' at the
levels of national and international policy. The **[WCF [gives global
expression to]{.mark}]{.underline}** the notion that **[the [nuclear
family]{.mark}]{.underline}** is **[[universal to all of humanity and
history]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[[arguing that the family
is]{.underline}]{.mark}** '**[[based on]{.mark} the [marital union of a
man and a woman]{.mark}]{.underline}**, is **[the bedrock of
society]{.underline}**, the **[strength of our nations]{.underline}**,
**[and]{.underline}** the **[hope of humanity]{.underline}** ... the
ultimate foundation of every civilization known to history'. 3 In 2016,
the WCF launched the International Organisation for the Family in Cape
Town, South Africa, where delegates signed the Cape Town Declaration,
confirming their efforts to 'firmly resis\[t\] every push to redefine
marriage: to include same-sex or group bonds, or sexually open or
temporary ones'. Prominent African signatories to the declaration
include the head of the African Christian Democratic Party, Kevin
Meshoe, and the Nigerian ambassador to South Africa, Uche Ajulu-Okeke. A
few months prior to this gathering, **[the WCF collaborated with the
Africa Organisation for Families]{.underline}**, the **[Kenya Conference
of Catholic Bishops]{.underline}**, the **[Kenyan Ministry of Labour and
Social Protection]{.underline}**, the **[Catholic Archdiocese of
Nairobi]{.underline}**, the **[Cameroon-based Global Institute for
Family Integrity and]{.underline}** the **[Nigerian-based Foundation for
African Cultural Heritage]{.underline}** (FACH) **[to host the African
Regional Conference on Families.]{.underline}** Anne Kioko, President of
the African Organisation for Families, explained that **[comprehensive
sex education was amongst the concerns of Kenyan pro-family
advocates]{.underline}**, arguing that such 'programs go way beyond
regular sex education and are designed to change all the sexual and
gender norms of society' (Nyambura, 2016). **[['Pro-family' discourses
advocating for]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[nuclear family
model]{.mark} to be protected]{.underline}** in national and
international policy **[as the only legally recognised form of family
[are]{.mark}]{.underline}** **[becoming [increasingly
influential]{.mark} with[in international policy debates]{.mark} and
imaginaries.]{.underline}** The growing influence of the pro-family
movement has been enabled not only by advocacy work in civil society and
politics, but also in academia. In the United States, for instance, W.
Bradford Wilcox (cited later in this article) is the Director of the
National Marriage Project, which is based at the highly ranked
University of Virginia. Other pro-family advocates who are based in
academic departments include WCF Founder Allan Carlson (Professor of
History, Hillsdale College), Mark Regnerus (Professor of Sociology,
University of Texas-Austin), Everett Piper (Oklahoma Wesleyan
University) and Monique Chireau (Duke University). Beyond universities,
a number of pro-family advocates work from US-based think tanks that are
notoriously conservative, including the Sutherland Institute, the
Witherspoon Institute, the Population Research Institute and the
Heritage Foundation. Increasingly, popular discourse across the
continent construes people who do not subscribe to the heterosexual
nuclear family model not only as 'unAfrican', but also as socially and
economically threatening to the developing state. **[Within these
discourses]{.underline}**, non-heterosexuals, single mothers and
childfree women, those who engage in pre-marital and extra-marital sex
and reproduction, and **[alternative family forms are
scapegoated]{.underline}** for various social challenges, and therefore
experience forms of oppression, namely marginalisation, victimisation
and exclusion (Young, 2009).

Decolonising the family

As Oyěwùmí (2002:1) writes, the **[enforcement and institutionalisation
of the European concept of the 'family' formed]{.underline}** the
**[ideological basis of gender and race]{.underline}** 'as two
fundamental axes along which people became oppressed' **[in colonised
African societies]{.underline}**. As one of the 'scientific designs' of
modernity, **[the nuclear family ideal was]{.underline}** not created
**[by]{.underline}** Africans but by Western **[Europeans and
Americans]{.underline}**, responding to the needs and visions of these
places, not those of Africa (Mignolo, 2011:130). **[As decolonial
scholars]{.underline}** (Smith, 2006; Lugones, 2007; Quijano, 2007;
Mignolo, 2011) **[reveal]{.underline}**, **[there is scope to decolonise
the family]{.underline}**, **[given that [this]{.mark}]{.underline}**
Christian and bourgeois **[[kinship model was an instrumental axis
through which colonial power was]{.mark} implemented
[institutionalised.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The **[control of sexuality
and gender]{.underline}** that this model enabled **[was reinforced by
European control of knowledge]{.underline}** (Quijano, 2007; Grosfoguel,
2013). Within the pro-family movement, the **[['logic of
coloniality']{.underline}]{.mark}** (Quijano, 2007) **[is [reinvigorated
in that]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[normative ways in
which]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[eurocentric nuclear family
model]{.mark} is used [reinforce western
authority]{.mark}]{.underline}** while also protecting systems of
domination that can be legitimated through the nuclear family's
modelling of sexed and gendered hierarchies as natural.
Mid-twentieth-century literature on kinship in Africa (Hunter, 1936;
Krige, 1936; Schapera, 1940; Phillips, 1953; Reuter, 1963) largely
offered descriptive accounts that constructed African kinship systems as
located along a linear path of 'progression' towards 'advanced' family
forms associated with modernisation (Siqwana-Ndulo, 1998:408).
**[[Christonormative European thought constructed the gender binary and
hierarchy as]{.mark} evidence of [enlightened
society]{.mark}]{.underline}**, **[[with]{.underline}]{.mark}** any
other form of **[[indigenous sex and gender]{.underline}]{.mark}**
practice **[constructed [as 'savage']{.mark} and]{.underline}**,
further, **[as an indication of the need to evangelise and modernise
these societies]{.underline}** (Kitch, 2009). Howard Washington Odum's
1910 publication, Social and Mental Traits of the Negro, illustrates the
ways in which racist ideology drew on patriarchal constructions of
'civilization', reporting that 'in his home life, the Negro is filthy,
careless, and indecent ... as destitute of morals as many of the lower
animals \[and with\] little knowledge of the sanctity of home or marital
relations' (Stacey 1994:66). Such **[sociological and anthropological
scholarship on 'family']{.underline}** and kinship **[in Africa
provided]{.underline}** the **[[epistemic resources used to justify
conquest, colonisation]{.mark} and domination]{.underline}** of the
non-European world. Thus, **[colonised men were constructed as being
unable to govern colonised women]{.underline}**, and therefore unable to
govern themselves (Kitch, 2009). Employing the nuclear family model as a
means of measuring 'savagery' or 'civilization', enlightenment ideology
erected a system of racial classification and ranking through a
gender/sex hierarchical order that positioned male dominance as a signal
of cultural advancement (2009:56). African men, fated to become slaves,
were considered to lack fully formed souls, like women, and the
institution of marriage offered a gendered biblical model that could
justify the capture and enslavement of African people (2009:52). What
**[pro-family accounts of the universality of the 'nuclear
family']{.underline}**, such as those quoted earlier, further
**[obscure]{.underline}** is **[the extent to which African kinship
systems were destroyed throughout]{.underline}** the period of
**[colonial conquest.]{.underline}** At the same time that European
scholars were constructing African kinship structures as inferior
through the normative modern ideal of the nuclear family, they were
actively destroying the kinship systems of colonised people. The
**[[idealised western nuclear family model is]{.underline}]{.mark}**
therefore **[[implicated in economic]{.underline}]{.mark}** and
**[[knowledge practices]{.underline}]{.mark}** of modernity that have
justified racism and constructed some human lives as inferior and
dispensable (Mignolo, 2011:6). Mignolo reinforces the salience of
heteropatriarchy within a power relations, arguing that a global
gender/sex hierarchy 'privileged males over females and European
patriarchy over other forms of gender configuration and sexual
relations' through the invention and institutionalisation of sex
(heterosexual/homosexual) and gender (male/female) binaries (Mignolo,
2011:18). Thus, 'a global racial/ethnic hierarchy that privileged
European people over non-European people' secured the privileged status
of European men over all men and colonised people, as well as European
women (2011:18). These **[[systems]{.mark} were held [in place by
privileging of western knowledges]{.mark} over non-western
knowledges]{.underline}** (which were often targeted for eradication)
**[[and]{.mark} a [spiritual]{.mark}]{.underline}**/religious
**[[hierarchy]{.mark} that privileged Christianity]{.underline}** over
non-Christianity and other non-western spiritualities (2011: 18).

#### The institution of the family is itself a weapon bought and sold in the project of conquest, antiblackness, heteropatriarchy, imperialism, and capitalism -- the white nuclear family as a dual processes of clearing and accumulation is sustained by the control and state power exercised through the family as a monopoly on the proper forms of kinship. The family must be abolished.

**King 18**. Tiffany Lethabo King is an Assistant Professor of Women's,
Gender and Sexuality Studies at Georgia State University. Her research
focuses on black gender formation and sexuality in the African diaspora,
critical geographies, and the intersections of slavery and settler
colonialism in the U.S. and Canada \[\"Black \'Feminisms\' and
Pessimism: Abolishing Moynihan\'s Negro Family,\" 2018, Theory & Event
Vol. 21\]//vikas

During the 1960s and 70s **[a cadre of Black women
activists]{.underline}** and cultural workers **[aligned themselves
with]{.underline}** a form of **[anti-capitalist, anti-racist,
anti-sexist]{.underline}**, at times **[anti-homophobic and decolonial
politics.]{.underline}** Some of **[these Black women were featured
in]{.underline}** the anthology **[The Black Woman]{.underline}**
(1970). It is in her contribution to this volume that **[[Lindsey
deconstructs the family]{.mark} as a violent formation [and calls for
its abolition]{.mark}]{.underline}** as an institution. **[[Lindsey's
critique]{.underline}]{.mark}** of the family departed from white
radical and lesbian feminist critiques in the sense that it
**[[linked]{.mark} the [family to antiblackness and
conquest.]{.mark}]{.underline}** It's **[intersectional analysis
reaches]{.underline}** far **[[beyond]{.underline}]{.mark}** a critique
of **[[patriarchy and capitalism implicating]{.underline}]{.mark}** the
family in everyday forms of **[[imperial, genocidal and antiblack
violence.]{.underline}]{.mark}** Lindsey's **[[abolitionist critique
calls attention to]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[ways]{.mark} violent
processes of [property accumulation]{.mark} [give white heteronormative
gender, sexuality and family]{.mark} formations [coherence and]{.mark}
secure [white ascendency through antiblack racism and Native
genocide]{.mark}]{.underline}**. The American family is far from a
benign and innocent site of existence. Lindsey pens, "**[[The family
and]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[land on which it lived]{.mark} and
cultivated]{.underline}** its **[crops [became the man's
property]{.mark}]{.underline}**, man moved on to the seizure of the land
of others and his prisoners of war became his slaves. Upon this base,
**[the state evolved and empires were created.]{.underline}**"17 For
Lindsey, **[[the family is the core and animating unit of property
creation and]{.mark} of the [imperial state]{.mark}]{.underline}**. In
the essay, Lindsey connects family, land, slaves, property, state and
empire in order to situate the family within various forms of
historical, state and genocidal violence. Lindsey's indictment of the
family invites a spatial analysis of its power. **[The family as a
social formation can be reconcieved as an amalgamation of land, slaves,
property, conquest and the state.]{.underline}** The family could be
constructed **[as geopolitical unit or assemblage]{.underline}**
(family-land-slaves-property-state-empire) **[that creates the American
frontier]{.underline}**, or the time-space coordinates of the human. The
family as the geopolitical unit of women-children-land-slaves is a form
of property that can be accumulated. Specifically, **[[property is
accumulated through]{.underline}]{.mark}** the **[[conquest]{.mark} of
Native people,]{.underline}** the theft of land **[[and]{.mark} the
[accumulation]{.mark} of slaves as property]{.underline}**. According to
Lindsey, family formation is a Western formation that is anti-Black.
**[[The family]{.underline}]{.mark}**, **[as a white
institution]{.underline}**, **[[has been held up to Blacks as]{.mark} a
[desirable but somehow unattainable]{.mark} goal]{.underline}**, at
least not in the pure form that whites have created. **[Witness [the
Black middle class]{.mark}]{.underline}** or pseudo-escapees into the
mainstream. **[This group [has]{.mark} assumed many]{.underline}** of
the **[institutional postures]{.underline}** of the oppressor, including
the so-called intact family, but even here we find **[a fantastically
[high divorce rate and frustration]{.mark} on]{.underline}** this
**[domestic level]{.underline}** has **[increased dissension between
individual Black men and women]{.underline}**, when **[it should instead
be a signal that [something is radically wrong with the model
they]{.mark} have chosen to [imitate.]{.mark}]{.underline}**18 For
Lindsey, **[[the family is a white institution.]{.underline}]{.mark}**
**[The family as a patriarchal unit of property]{.underline}**,
enslavement and conquest is not a priori to Black sociality. In fact,
Black people often fail in their attempts at replicating the formation.
**[[Lindsey calls for the entire model to be destroyed]{.mark} rather
than succumb]{.underline}** to the institution. We have an obligation as
Black women to project ourselves into the revolution to destroy these
institutions which not only oppress Blacks but women as well, for if
these institutions continue to flourish, they will be used against us in
the continuing battle of mind over body.19 **[[The institution of the
family is]{.mark} constructed as [a weapon that will be used in]{.mark}
the "[battle of mind over body]{.mark}."]{.underline}**20 Further,
**[Lindsey theorizes [the family as an organizing logic of]{.mark}
control and [state power.]{.mark}]{.underline}** The family is not a
private space or a private matter for Lindsey. In fact, the family is
intimately connected to the state and animates its power. Lindsey

agency to perpetuate the state]{.underline}**, **[and Blacks have been
used as extensions]{.underline}** of the white family,
**[as]{.underline}** the **[prisoners of war enslaved to do the dirty

institution were destroyed, the state would be destroyed."21 As an
activist, Lindsey's work as an advocate for poor single Black mothers
made her acutely aware of the ways that the state constructed the notion
of the Black AFDC 'family'22 as a site of surveillance and intervention.
Lindsey reminds us that **[the [disciplinary power of the welfare state
has access to]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[internal domestic space of
Black women]{.underline}]{.mark}** (or the Black matriarch's house). The
increase in state sanctioned surveillance prompted by The Report and
administered through a diffuse network of welfare case managers, monthly
reporting and documentation of sexual behavior, children, income and
daily activity was an issue and concern that weighed heavily on Kay
Lindsey. **[In The Report]{.underline}**, **[[Moynihan is
deploying]{.mark}]{.underline}** the **[[epistemologies]{.mark} and
tools [of sociological surveillance]{.mark}]{.underline}** in order
**[[to make Black households transparent]{.underline}]{.mark}** in order
**[to assess whether they can be assimilated into]{.underline}** the
**[social and spatial formation of the family.]{.underline}** Patricia
Hill Collins (1989) argues that **[Moynihan erroneously conflates the
Black household with the Black family]{.underline}** when he wrote the
report.23 Hill Collins is making an argument for a more expansive notion
of family in Black life that cannot be contained within or reduced to
the empirical unit or data of the household. On the other hand, Lindsey
and contemporary Afropessimists would argue that it cannot be presumed
that there is such a thing as a 'Black family' in 1965 when the report
is drafted.

#### Thus the method: we demand the end of m ni,foreign military sales and direct commercial sales of the colonial household.

#### Black female sexuality, disorganized by the gender of the Black matriarch, refuses the family's assimilation of Black people into property. She refuses the production of the colonial household by refusing the propertied imperative, and therefore she is queer.

King 13 \[IN THE CLEARING: BLACK FEMALE BODIES, SPACE AND SETTLER
COLONIAL LANDSCAPES Tiffany Jeannette King, Doctor of Philosophy,
Tiffany (Lethabo) King's research focuses on Black gender formation and
sexuality at the intersection of slavery and indigenous genocide in the
U.S. and Canada.\]

[[The Black matriarch head of household confounds and
disorganizes]{.mark} the [gendered landscapes]{.mark} of settler

project. [Property certainly represents a visible
landscape]{.underline}. However, I would like to take up the landscape
of property as one that is always in, what Richard Schein would say, the
process of becoming. Property as a landscape (including people) also
functions as "discourse materialized."84 According to Nicolas Blomley
who provides a more Marxian account of its existence and purpose,
property is an enactment of power. The specific enactment of power is
the power to exclude.

#### Black feminism is situated at the nexus of blackness and transness -- that mode of living ruptures stable, fixed identities, subverting the humanism underlying the violence of the white propertied familial project.

**Green & Bey 17** \[Kai M. Green, Assistant Professor of Women's,

[Often]{.underline} I think [we use Black feminism to create a politic

October--December 2017 abjection and potentially one of redress and
emancipation; [it is a contested figure at the very center of social

been.25 "What is inadequate to" Black feminism "is already given
ontologies," Fred [Moten writes. "The lived experienced of blackness

constant demand for an ontology of disorder]{.underline}]{.mark}, an

## 2AC A2 FW -- FAMILY IS AN ARM

#### Western nation building practices sell heteronormativity as an arm to other nations in order to enforce global legitimacy.

**McEwen 16** Haley McEwen is a lecturer in the Centre for Law and
Justice, Australian Graduate School of Policing and Security at Charles

*Critical Philosophy of Race*, Volume 4, Issue 2, 2016, pp. 239-262,
DOI: 10.5325/critphilrace.4.2.0239\] DTS

Making Sense of Homophobia in Africa Scholarly attention to homophobia
in Africa has largely drawn on intersecting anthropological,
sociological, historical, and political frameworks. These interrogations
have drawn heavily on feminist, gender, and queer theoretical insights
as well as the discourses and logic of human rights and democratic
citizenship. Through these various approaches, scholars have explicitly
located their respective intellectual projects within the struggle for
equal rights and recognition of sexual minorities. In the literature,
the [politics of knowledge and ignorance and the power relations
underlying them have emerged implicitly, but have not yet received
adequate attention.]{.underline} Engagements with knowledge-power and
ignorance in this context have primarily interrogated the increasingly
popular notion that homosexuality is "unAfrican."25 [Acknowledged as a
construction that depends upon the silencing and ignore-ance of the
reality of [same-sex intimacy]{.mark} and [partnerships in African
societies, denial of queer African genders]{.mark} and sexualities are
understood as having a "xenophobic logic"26 that is "[wielded rampantly
as a tactic for]{.mark} [othering]{.mark}]{.underline}."27 As Reddy
shows[,]{.underline} these views have translated into the denial of
citizenship rights on the basis of a particular sexual orientation
through the mobilization of moral panic that has no basis in evidence of
danger or consequence posed to the populace by same-sex practices.28
According to Ndjio, Afrocentric political doctrines "have been
instrumental in the effort of post-colonial nationalist elites to
constitute an exclusive African selfhood."29 [[These constructions have
focused on]{.mark} what he refers to as "[efforts to 'Africanize' the
sexuality]{.mark} of the masses [in a global
context]{.mark}."]{.underline}30 In Cameroon, Ndjio writes that these
discursive efforts have been widely successful in that "both men and
women have come to believe that in order to be 'good' citizens or 'real'
Africans, they have to . . . limit their sexuality solely to
heterosexual desires, but also have a natural aversion to other forms of
sexuality such as same-sex relations."31 [These critiques of [attempts
to essentialize sexualities in Africa]{.mark} provide important insights
into how denial of same-sex practices and partnerships within African
societies and their histories [have been produced **"on a**]{.mark}
**[mass scale for striking enforcements]{.mark},"**32 functioning to
exclude and victimize thousands across the continent. Constituting
sexuality as a structure of exclusion, political [leaders]{.mark}
promoting homophobic attitudes and legislation attempt to permanently
[fasten heteronormative identities to democratic values]{.mark} and
nationhood.]{.underline} These values serve not only heterosexist
privilege, but male privilege as well. According to Pharr, **[homophobia
is fundamentally created by [heterosexism]{.mark} "with its assumption
that the world is and must be heterosexual and its display of power and
privilege as the norm."]{.underline}**33 Heterosexism and homophobia,
she writes, "**[[work]{.mark} together [to enforce]{.mark}
[compulsory]{.mark} heterosexuality and that bastion of patriarchal
power, the [nuclear family," which]{.mark}, in turn, [is used to justify
male dominance]{.mark} over women at all levels of social
organization.3]{.underline}**4 Many African feminist scholars have
documented and theorized **[how heterosexism and patriarchy not only
oppressed women [and]{.mark} sexual minorities in the West, but also
[served as weapons of Western modernizing
projects]{.mark}]{.underline}**. [British colonialism in Nigeria for
instance entrenched a system that explicitly privileged men and
disenfranchised women as "unable others" whose supposed subordinate and
inferior femininity cast male authority as the natural order of
things.]{.underline}35 As Izugbara writes, the colonial government
emphasized "able-bodied men" within criteria for employment as guides,
servants, tax collectors, cleaners, and stewards for the colonizers.36
This system continues in post-independence Nigeria, where
[[heteronormative "forms of]{.mark} sexual identity and [family life
have been]{.mark} discursively [produced as the norm, and]{.mark} made
to function as metanarratives for national identity]{.underline}."37 As
McClintock,38 Tamale,39 and Kitch show,40 heterosexist political values
were also justifications for European conquest. As Jackson demonstrates,
[colonial discourses of racial and gender difference [became fused
within European constructions of sexuality]{.mark}]{.underline}.41
**[The ideologies of race, gender, and sexuality intersected in these
discourses, as sexologists of the late nineteenth century "drew on
fantasies of black female embodiment as their model of sexual deviancy
and gender nonconformity" in constructing "healthy" and "pathological"
sexualities:]{.underline}** Racial comparative-anatomy methods were used
to determine sexual definition, with a presumed similitude between
"deviant" white bodies and the black body. The word homosexual itself
seemed to conjure some anxieties about miscegenation, as the
"barbarously hybrid word" was a mix of Latin and Greek, even referring
to "shades of gender" and "sexual half-breeds."42 Here, Jackson makes
apparent the inextricability of raced, gendered, and sexed ideologies of
difference within colonial knowledge systems. In turn, the construction
of sexual deviance among African people became the constitutive "Other"
within middle-class European performances of respectability in the form
of monogamous, heterosexual marriage.**[43 [Within Western nation
building projects, it was believed that homosexuality weakened the
virility of the nation]{.mark}, while lesbianism compromised women's
ability and desire to produce the next generation of patriots who would
expand the empire further into Africa.]{.underline}**44 [Thus,
homosexuality was not only [constructed as "sick" and]{.mark} "sinful,"
but as [politically and economically dangerous]{.mark} too. As current
Western conquests into African sexual politics reveal, the control,
regulation, and reproduction of a nation's population through sexual
regulation endures as a mechanism of reproducing global inequalities of
power.]{.underline}

#### The white settler family exists in a time and spatial category created out of the natal alienation and genocide of Native and Black people that prevents the "Black family's" existence and therefore opens Black socialities to diagnosis, surveillance, and violence.

constructed for examination.]{.mark} The [Black family]{.mark} as a unit
of pathology [hides its centuries' long production of the white family
through its own negation]{.mark} thus always [producing a diagnosis of
Black impairment.]{.mark} Within the Moynihan Report, the [parasitic
nature of the white Settler family which depends on conquest and slavery
is not untangled and exposed]{.mark}]{.underline} as a constitutive
element of the family as a sociological unit of analysis.

#### Abolition requires the forwarding of Black sociality in the excess of current familial grammars: the agential capacity to transform strangers to loved ones outside of genealogical relationships.

#### Global settler colonialism and conquest instantiates Western supremacy via the sale of the nuclear family and heteropatriarchy to produce a universal social order -- the nuclear family is a colonial determinant of civility which reduces non-Europeans to a position of inhuman and conditions death-dealing on racial and gendered lines.

#### Imperial power relations produce the nuclear family in colonial settlements.

**Phillips 9** \[Richard Phillips, Department of Geography, University
of Liverpool, Liverpool, "Settler colonialism and the nuclear family,"
*The Canadian Geographer,* 6/1/2009\] sg

Debates about gender and sexuality have mushroomed in geography and
postcolonial criticism in recent years, but---particularly in
geography--- these debates have concentrated on the margins rather than
the centres of sexual life. Much of great value has been written about
sex workers (Hubbard 1999; after Walkowitz 1980) and same-sex desire
(Castells 1983; Bell and Valentine 1995), for instance, and much less
about those who occupy the moral and sexual centre ground, quietly
conforming to the often unwritten rules of the times and places in which
they find themselves. David Bell and Gill Valentine observed that '[A
big absence from geographies of sexualities is, ironically, the dominant
sexuality within contemporary sexualities---
heterosexuality']{.underline} (Bell and Valentine 1995, p. 12). [This
echoed]{.underline} Julia [Cream's]{.underline} [call]{.underline} for
researchers interested in sexuality and space [to interrogate the often
hidden sexualization of everyday spaces:]{.underline} We need to know
how space is produced as uncontaminated, and shorn of its associations
with sexuality. Sexuality is so often hidden away in the upstairs of
homes, behind closed doors, or in the upper reaches of the disciplinary
house. [We need to expose the ways in which it has been excluded,
obscured and rendered irrelevant.]{.underline} (Cream 1994, p. 122)
[This means interrogating ordinary sexualities, including those rendered
almost invisible by their constructed normality]{.underline} (Phillips
2006b). To see why **[it is important to denaturalize the nuclear
family]{.underline}**---[as a social institution that hinges on a sexual
contract]{.underline}---it is first necessary to understand [Foucault's
analysis]{.underline} of sexuality as 'an especially dense transfer
point for relations of power' (Foucault 1978, p. 103). Ann Laura Stoler,
in a postcolonial critique of Foucault's History of Sexuality and an
archival study of colonial sexual life, [has shown how sexuality was a
dense transfer point for relations of specifically imperial
power]{.underline} (Stoler 1996, 2002). Stoler argues that **[sexual and
other forms of intimacy both reflected and produced colonial social
relations]{.underline}**; her analysis draws upon the memories of
[domestic servants in the Dutch East Indies,]{.underline}
[and]{.underline} speaks to those of others affected by colonialism,
such as the former [pupils of residential schools in Canada]{.underline}
(De Leeuw 2007). **[Sexualized imperial power was expressed and produced
in relationships between individuals]{.underline}**, [which]{.underline}
in turn [were shaped by]{.underline} what Lenore Manderson calls [the
'moral logic of colonialism']{.underline} (Manderson 1997, p. 373). As
Sally Engle Merry has shown in her work on Hawai'i, this logic was
complex, contested and differentiated. She contrasts the priorities of
overlapping groups of Christians and capitalists, finding the former
interested in the eating habits, cleanliness, sleep and sexuality of
colonial subjects, the latter with their punctuality and discipline
(Merry 2000, p. 16). The often heated arenas of imperial sexuality
politics suggest that, though academic research on the subject is
relatively new, sensitivity to the issues at stake is not. [It was
widely recognized on the imperial shopfloor and in the imperial
home]{.underline}---tacitly or explicitly, by family members, medical
workers, schoolteachers, owners and employees of plantations or mines,
governments--- [that sex was important]{.underline} not only for the
pleasure (for some) but also [for]{.underline} the
[business]{.underline} [and]{.underline} the fundamental social bonds
and everyday routines of [empire]{.underline}. The importance of the
colonial family is supported in the historical record. There is much
evidence that **[nuclear families were an important presence in many
resettlement societies]{.underline}** and that this was due both to the
relocation of families and to the fact that many men and women travelled
with ideas and expectations about families they would like to form.
[**Social and specifically legal frameworks** were also
relocated]{.underline}, [and this **allowed nuclear families to flourish
in colonial societies**]{.underline}. Legal historian Patrick Brode
shows that a series of laws underpinned the institution of marriage in
early Canada by legislating against breaches of marital conduct (such as
sex outside marriage and failure to keep a promise to marry). These were
both 'a major feature of early Canadian jurisprudence' and 'part of the
inherited English system' (Valverde 1991; Brode 2002, p. vii).
Crucially, he adds that English laws were imported selectively. For
instance, [laws that propped up nuclear families were
adopted]{.underline}, whereas those providing poor relief were not.
Colonial law and the production of the nuclear family [To understand the
difference between the transplantation of nuclear families to colonial
societies]{.underline}, and their production in such societies, [it
helps to review]{.underline} a criticism that has been applied to one of
the more influential (and polemical) interventions in the debate about
[imperialism and sexuality]{.underline}. Ronald Hyam's colourfully
illustrated thesis in Empire and Sexuality: The British Experience is
that their active sex lives helped British men through months and years
in penal colonies and resource frontiers, military camps and farming
settlements, and attracted some of them there in the first place. 'The
expansion of Europe', he argued, 'was not only a matter of Christianity
and commerce, it was also a matter of copulation and concubinage' (Hyam
1990, p. 2). The idea that British men travelled with fully formed if
sometimes repressed sexualities, which were then simply liberated
through the 'essentially harmless pleasures' of empire (Hyam 1990, p.
152), does not survive Foucault's powerful critique of the 'repressive
hypothesis' (the 'repressive hypothesis' regards sexualities as innate,
and as such either repressed or liberated in social contexts; Foucault
argued, on the contrary, that sexualities are socially produced).
Recasting sexualities as contextually and discursively produced, through
reciprocal relationships with powerful social institutions such as
medical, religious and legal establishments, Foucault (1978) showed that
[sexual desires were not so much fulfilled as produced in colonial
contexts and encounters]{.underline} (Stoler 1996). Hyam's (1990)
account in Empire and Sexuality: The British Experience of British men,
travelling overseas with fully formed sexualities, is also disrupted by
the more general postcolonial critique of what James Blaut has called
'the colonizer's model of the world': the Eurocentric assumption that
colonial societies and their components---arguably including nuclear
families---were fundamentally derivative. Blaut contests what he calls
'Eurocentric diffusionism': the assumption that 'culture', 'innovation'
and 'human causality' flow from the European to the non-European world
(Blaut 1993, p. 1). Contesting the worldview that casts Europe as the
eternal inside and asserts that 'Inside innovates, Outside imitates',
Blaut (1993, p. 1) articulates some familiar axioms of postcolonial
criticism---most generally, the critique of colonial binaries---in
specifically geographical terms. Theoretically, these points have been
well taken. The challenge is to translate them into more specific and
concrete terms. One way of doing this is to develop and substantiate
claims that [nuclear families were not simply taken to British colonies
but were to some extent made in them]{.underline}, and that these and
other forms of sexualized social life not only reflected but actively
defined the colonial order. **[The nuclear family was transplanted and
produced in settlement societies]{.underline}**, not only by the
individuals who formed families, but **[by the social and cultural
processes]{.underline}**--- [including]{.underline} the [legal
mechanisms and institutional]{.underline} contexts and
[frameworks]{.underline}---[that encouraged them to do so]{.underline}.
This section acknowledges some of the legal processes, and specifically
the laws governing family life and defining the parameters of family
that underpinned the production of colonial families (Blomley 1994;
Cooper 1998; Blomley et al. 2001). [**This**]{.underline} discussion
**[ranges beyond formal legal systems because]{.underline}**, as
Foucault argued and others have amplified, **[sexuality is regulated and
constructed through a wide variety of institutions]{.underline}**
([including]{.underline} the [religious and medical
establishments]{.underline}) **[and practices]{.underline}**
([including]{.underline} the [regulation]{.underline} [of]{.underline}
other family members and of [the self)]{.underline} (Foucault 1978; Hunt
1999). In the interests of focus, emphasis is placed upon laws that
shaped families in a primary sense rather than those that governed
families and discriminated against individuals on the basis of their
membership of such families. Examples of the latter include the
Homestead Act (1862) and Canadian and other colonial equivalents and
derivatives, which generally allocated land to families and single men:
in the United States and Canada, married couples could claim 160 acres,
unmarried men half that much and unmarried women and children nothing at
all. Profoundly and foundationally important though they were, laws such
as these were ultimately secondary because they worked with ideas of
marriage and family that had already been defined through primary
legislation.

## 2AC A2 FW -- DEBATE IS A FAMILY

#### Debate's guise of [family resemblance]{.underline} congeals the most [violent impetus of the university]{.underline}: the [silent intensification]{.underline} of carcerality waged [against.]{.underline} There is no "debate community," because debate is overdetermined by the negligence of the university.
