#### We must theorize place and landscape through an understanding of the Black matriarch and property in order to think settler colonialism and antiblackness alongside each other.

- Note: this evidence is also a response to settler colonialism.

**King 13** \[IN THE CLEARING: BLACK FEMALE BODIES, SPACE AND SETTLER
COLONIAL LANDSCAPES Tiffany Jeannette King, Doctor of Philosophy,
Tiffany (Lethabo) King's research focuses on Black gender formation and
sexuality at the intersection of slavery and indigenous genocide in the
U.S. and Canada.\]

In order to make spatial and temporal links between the space and time
of genocide (which continues) and the space and time of slavery (and
ongoing anti-Black racism), I employ palimpsestic methods which can
attend to a number of different spaces and different historical moments
at once. I use the work of Jacqui Alexander and the work of cultural
landscape scholar Richard Schein to help guide me through this method of
time and space scrambling[. Schein argues that in order to truly
understand how landscape matters [one must theorize not only the "place
of the cultural landscape in the social relations and spatial
arrangements of daily life" but also "how a particular, identifiable
cultural landscape in this place is related and connected to landscapes
and social processes in other places."]{.mark}]{.underline} This call to
understand how landscapes are connected and related to one another gives
me the confidence that [the theoretical risks I am taking in attempting
to [connect "Black" landscapes like the plantation, the Black matriarch
and the disorganized family to property]{.mark},]{.underline} conquest,
and Native genocide are worthwhile, necessary and politically useful. An
important intervention that Schein makes is the development of a
critique of Pierce Lewis's conceptualization of the landscape as a
"series of sedimentary layers of social accretion", or time periods
layered on top of one another[. This [layering creates the effect of
linear time]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Schein argues that it may be
more useful to "begin by viewing the landscape as a palimpsest rather
than a cultural strata" or linear time. By using the palimpsest, [Schein
[enables a conceptualization of landscapes as spaces of "erasure and
overwriting" where different scripts can co-exist.]{.mark} The
palimpsest assumes that there is an intermingling and interplay of
different historical eras as well as historical and contemporary actors.
[The co-existence of settler colonialism and slavery as distinct forms

anothe]{.mark}]{.underline}[r]{.mark} is what I will attempt to reveal

#### Black women as an identity category is most salient when deployed as a refusal of the organizing binary of male and female, and therefore dwells in Black feminism and trans feminism as an undoing of identity categories and opening space for unrepresentable futures.

- Note: this is also an answer to queer theory and trans studies that
  does not account for the unique position of Black women or Black
  feminism.

**Green & Bey 17** \[Kai M. Green, Assistant Professor of Women's,
Gender and Sexuality Studies @ Williams College, & Marquis Bey, Ford
Foundation Dissertation Fellow and PhD candidate in the English
department @ Cornell University, "Where Black Feminist Thought and
Trans\*Feminism Meet: A Conversation," *Souls*, 19:4, 438-454, DOI:
10.1080/10999949.2018.1434365\] sg

[How does the category of woman function]{.underline} here? [Who is it
able to hold and how is it able to hold difference]{.underline}? Those
of us who are interested in Black liberation must be weary of the "and"
in "[trans and cis"]{.underline} as it purports to have reckoned with
the real difference and fissures that rest between trans and cis. It is
a question of relationality. How do we/they belong to one another? The
"and" here is not natural as it [seeks to bring transgender women into
the fold of the category women]{.underline} (read cisgender), a category
that has been critiqued by Black and Women of Color feminists as [a
category that continuously fails]{.underline}, fails [to articulate
clearly what it is that makes some women]{.underline}, who are not
white, who are not middle class, [illegible as the imagined
universalized representative subject]{.underline} [of the
category]{.underline}. So we are left asking (1) Are Black women, women?
(When? How?) (2) If not, why do we hold on to that category "woman" at

assume the answer to Sojourner Truth's question, "ain't I a woman?" is
"Yes!" But what if we instead took the gift of Truth as a proposition to
dwell in the question? Do **[models of inclusion prohibit us from
thinking about or asking for something else]{.underline}**? Even more
challenging: **[what would something else look like without the

bodies. Basically, what I am getting at is that we can and should shift
our grammar and language to be more inclusive, but understand too that

[marginalized folks do often find joy in claiming the category, so the
extermination of it might in fact do a kind of harm]{.underline} (which
is not to the exclusion of the very harm done by the category itself,
known or not). [This is]{.underline} all in part [why I am interested
more in politicized identities,]{.underline} following Cathy Cohen; [why
I am interested in the work that we do as the identities that come to
subjectivate us,]{.underline} rather than presuming that identity is an
immutable possession. The phrase you use, the "place of the demand," is
right where I want to dwell, and it is that demand that I'm interested
in. It seems to me that to do or be a Black or trans feminist, or both,
is to heed that demand. The "identity," of sorts, of one who uptakes the
demand Black feminism and trans feminism bear---and thus the eruptive
volatility and multiplicity simmering beneath and alongside each of
them---concerns less a delimited entity that is a Black and/or trans
feminist and more in the vein of Nathaniel Mackey's "enmity," a
non-substance that describes an "auto-constitutive stress."8 [**The
transness of Black feminism and the Blackness of trans feminism,** as it
were, marks a reverberatory tremor that pervades the rhythm of work done
in service to Black and trans feminism.]{.underline} The vibratory waves
of its stress **[is the fuzzy location in which "identity"
resides]{.underline}**, [unable to be placed or limned and rather **a
haptic disturbance**]{.underline}, a tenor, a worrying, a movement [that
is placed where it cannot be placed]{.underline}. So yes, [the centering
of Black "women"]{.underline} (again, this is a troubled term,
especially rubbing so close to the gender trouble that is Blackness) in
conversations surrounding feminism and the operative gender category of
"woman" [was in many ways meant to critique that very
term]{.underline}---to whom it actually refers in our imaginary, to what
and whose ends is the term deployed, in what situations is the term
deployed. "\[T\]he 'Black woman,'" L.H. Stallings writes in Mutha' Is
Half a Word, a phenomenal examination of the "unnaming" processes of
Black women, radical Black female sexuality, and Black queer desires,
"represents an invented character by cultures not of her own making ...
the term unsuccessfully attempts to join the narratives of woman (white)
with that of Black (man)."9 [In critiquing the notions of "Black" and
specifically]{.underline}, initially ["woman," Black women]{.underline},
in effect, [put a pressure on it that]{.underline} questioned its very
nominal and social status and [sought to alter the very foundations and
assumptions upon which the racially solipsistic category of "woman"
rested]{.underline}. You touch on something really important: [the
simultaneous urge to disrupt and change these limited and limiting
categories we use to identify ourselves, and to hold them close to
us]{.underline} [because it is by virtue of these categories that we
exist as subjects;]{.underline} **[we are subjectivated by these
categories, so to do away with them entirely would be to feel as though
we have lost ourselves.]{.underline}** But I sometimes wonder if this is
the aim, to lose ourselves, to cultivate liveable space in the losing of
ourselves. [Might the nexus of Black feminism and trans feminism be an
enabling provocation for un/racial, un/gendered "thought and existence
otherwise, an otherwise than being,"]{.underline} a shadethrowing
deployable problem for thought that names a force rather than physical
characteristic?10 Could that be a place to live? This is a genuine and
open question that I really want to invite to be interrogated.

#### Black women are ungendering the nexus of Black and woman, meaning that Black woman describes a set of disruptions rather than a bodily occurrence -- thus the aff opens space for new forms of becoming that hold out for impossible possibilities to be and live otherwise.

- This is an answer to the Hartman stuff elsewhere in this file.

**Bey '19** (: Marquis Bey (2019), Ford Foundation Dissertation Fellow
and PhD candidate in the English department at Cornell University, Black
Fugitivity Un/Gendered, The Black Scholar, 49:1, 55-62,
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00064246.2019.1548059)

[[Black women]{.underline}]{.mark}, for example, [[incite the rethinking
of un/gendering through a thoroughgoing interrogation of the processual
becoming (un)human through the subject placed at the nexus of Black and
woman]{.underline}]{.mark}. If "the Black woman" is, contentiously
speaking, an impossible figure, there is a curious kind of
becoming-Black-woman that is not only "read as a twin refusal (of both
purity and subjection)," but more pointedly an evil twin refusal, a
refusal of purification and subjectification.10 Or better yet, a
becoming-[[Blackwoman]{.underline}]{.mark} could be what Spillers has
called, at the literal outset of her seminal text, "a marked woman." The
marked woman: a figure who [[needs no introduction; or, a figure who,
perhaps, cannot be introduced, as the marked woman]{.underline}]{.mark}
is a pervasive, haunting presence of modernity, conditioning modernity's
possibility. Spillers is referencing herself and more broadly the nexus
of Black and woman. I want to maintain the troublesomeness of the
"marked woman," the words serving as indices for the disruption of
un/gendered subjects proximal to Blackness. The "problem for thought,"
the marked woman is a figured figure: not merely, or even at all, a
[[figure that exists as such and unmediated in sociality, but a figure
that is figured]{.underline}]{.mark}, which is to say an assemblage
fashioned to affix to and as subjectivity. To say that the marked woman
is the quintessential "problem for thought" is to think about not
solely, or even primarily, an actually occurring subject. The marked
woman describes not a subject in the world but a disruption that is
corporealized, discursive, and created; [[they]{.underline}]{.mark}11
[[describe a set of disruptions rather than a bodily
occurrence]{.underline}]{.mark} or Ellisonian "matter of a bio-chemical
accident to \[the\] epidermis." Insofar as the marked woman can be
linked to un/gendered fugitivity, they name a deployable disruption of
normativity. They name an inhibition of traniflesh. If the figured
figure of the marked woman is mired in a veritable un/gendered
Blackness, where are we to locate them in the world? [[Black women's
bodies serve]{.underline}]{.mark} here less [[as exemplar
representations of racialized cis womanhood's
discontents]{.underline}]{.mark}---which itself is a troubled and
fractured terminological rendering on the grounds that [[Black women
have long been withheld from "womanhood" and its constitutive
cisness---and instead as an excessive figuration that retains the
possibility of an otherwise ontology]{.underline}]{.mark}, an ontology
[[that refuses the logistical machineries of racial capitalism, gender
normativity, settler colonialism, and other hegemonic
regimes]{.underline}]{.mark}. So I will grudgingly concede that "the
Black," inasmuch as such terminology references Blackness (and, to my
mind, that terminology does not capture Blackness, understood in pre
ontological distinction from ontic "Blacks"),12 is non-being, an
ontology. But to put a finer point on this: this non-being is, in fact,
not an incapacity or absolute abjection and subjection to being defined
by legacies of white supremacy; rather, this non-being is a becoming
that torques the very texture of how we come to exist and opens up
existence to the primary avenue of non-recognition. That is, instead of
existence naming a recognition and addressability based on norms imposed
from without, un/gendered fugitivity, traniflesh, gives existence over
to how we relate to one another on self-determinative, opaque, and
otherwise terms. It is a demand to give primacy to an ever-capacious
unfolding will to become something else. To become someone elsewhere,
somewhere else one; to become in and as and through traniflesh,
fugitivity un/gendered. [[To inhabit the fugitive spirit of this
un/gendering is to incite a radically different way of
living]{.underline}]{.mark}. It is a secretive and shadowy force that
presents new conditions of possibility for possibility. In this sense it
lays bare Spillers' argument that Blackness comes to be figured "as
subject possibility"; it lays bare, and operationalizes, how [[the
fugitive spirit of un/gendering points primarily to "a place where being
undone is simultaneously a space for new forms of
becoming]{.underline}]{.mark}." 13 As possibility for subjectivity,
[[un/gendered fugitivity's tran flesh holds out for impossible
possibilities]{.underline}]{.mark}, shadow possibilities, and thus
unanticipated possibilities that do not necessarily rely on normative
frameworks for their somethingness. In differentiating from
somethingness, a somethingness beholden to logics and optics of
legibility, fugitive un/gendering might present that which we dare not
know will come. ∼ On the increasingly intellectual space of Facebook,
one can find, if one knows where to look (or who to subscribe to),
gender gems. Che Gossett, on June 10, 2017, posted as a status: "trans
as being on the run from gender ... trans as gender's undercommons." 14
And my mind set sail. Yes, trans is on the run from gender, [[gender
understood particularly as that material and]{.underline}]{.mark}
(white) [[symbolic regime of binaristic
categorization]{.underline}]{.mark}. If gender is at least one
substantive node of legalizing oneself as a subject, trans articulates a
self-effacement inasmuch as one's "self" operates upon a fundamental
cohesiveness of which an intact/integral gender is constitutive.
Moseying along in the undercommons, [[trans refuses to be known ahead of
itself, slurring subjective embodiment and blurring subjective
transparency.]{.underline}]{.mark} Trans emerges through its otherwise
to be knownness, its runnin' of its mouf, its lay-low dissemblance.
Effacing the subject it is believed to be stemming from is a fugitive
move away from subjectivity toward subjectivity, a beneath, an
underground, a subversive simmering subterrain where we ain't got no
business doing\' none of yo' business. I feelz you, Nate: I, too,
"confess to a weakness for these amphibious, in-between, both/and
advances into a realm which defies categorization, this way of
trespassing, so to speak, the line which otherwise divides ... "15
Amphibiousness is transness, interstitial and defiant of categorization.
Where it goes, it trespasses; where it leaves, it tears ever so
slightly. In the tearing might be where traniflesh emerges. Inasmuch as
traniflesh has been gifted by Green's and Ellison's tranifest---and it
has been gifted a great deal, an insurmountable debt that is not a
debt---it harbors a profound sense of hope fomented by a Black feminist
genealogy. It is life in the "dissonance" [[Black feminism has made a
quotidian practice, making love and queer futures]{.underline}]{.mark},
effusively.16 That quotidian practice looks to worry and trouble the
lines. The traniflesh that un/gendered fugitivity and fugitive
un/gendering reference upsets the end game, as it were, of linear
frameworks that demand answers by partitioning, and instead refuses an
answer, remains open and, perhaps with a little groove and pep, dances
along the edges. Gettin' funky, the movement here challenges foreseeable
loci that attempt to pin down (one cannot say where something dances as
the dancing is always between places) and, in fact, allows insight into
unlocatable movement as a manner of living. But further, this is not a
dancing that is in opposition to stance; it is a dance that does not
know stance, does not register stance as stillness, because its dancing
is where it stands: standing in movement. Unhappy with "positions," the
work done here is unsure of the utility of positionality as
positionality maintains the legitimacy of scripted positions. Positions,
though increasingly useful in times in which one's given position
dictates so much, are nonetheless given by hegemonic powers, powers that
fugitivity and un/gendering ultimately wish to displace. What is
advocated, then, is entering in and out, a maddened stepping that plays
with multiplicity and subversion. In other words, ain't no half-steppin'
here; if you gon' step, then step. The movement is valorized because
[[there is always more room in movement, always more breath space, a
substance vitally needed for those to whom breath is absolutely sacred
due to its scarcity.]{.underline}]{.mark} There must always be a time in
which the instability of structures comes into bold relief, in which
instance they must be parted from. Structures always show their unstable
colors, so it is therefore necessary to demand a kind of living
unstructured, a living on the move as living as such inasmuch as being
structured and fixed disallows life to proliferate. To situate into a
position is to disallow meaning to erupt from the dance, to stultify the
life occurring. But even more, the dancing, the movement of the
un/gendered, is also an invitation to dance differently in apposition
to, say, race and gender as biologically determined. [[I want to claim
fugitive un/ gendering as a means by which we refuse formalized
stability, by which we differ and defer locatedness, and find the shape
of subjectivity in the shake of subjectivity]{.underline}]{.mark}.17

#### The refusal to be who one is said to be -- to become otherwise -- is mired in the Blackness of transness and the transness of Blackness. Fugitive ungendering violates normative gender regimes and their imposed antiblackness by positing Blackness and transness as on the run from recognition.

**Bey '19** (: Marquis Bey (2019), Ford Foundation Dissertation Fellow
and PhD candidate in the English department at Cornell University, Black
Fugitivity Un/Gendered, The Black Scholar, 49:1, 55-62,
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/00064246.2019.1548059)

Blackness and transness, and their constitutive queerness, as analytic
modalities ---skirting and tangentially related to known congealed
"identities"---are projects for world transformation. These projects
concern [[radical work that interrogates varying
identity]{.underline}]{.mark} vectors and aims not merely for their
hegemonic destruction or a nebulous unspecificity but a kind [[of
sociality in the troublesome terrain of becoming
undone]{.underline}]{.mark} with and through one another. We don't know
where we'll end up, or if we'll end up anywhere, but that
feeling-with---that feeling of dissonance when you're shy about the fact
that now we "goin' together," as my aunties might say---is the onset of
a different way of doing ourselves in coalitional relation. [[We must be
working toward the underthrow of the world, thinking and living in
excess of our present condition because the radical
knows]{.underline}]{.mark}, and must believe, quite sincerely, [[that
things are not wholly contingent upon the context in which they were
sired]{.underline}]{.mark}. The structures under which we live posit
themselves as immutable and unable to be superseded, but if we imbibe
the nutritious nectar of Sweet Honey in the Rock we come to learn that
there is a way to supersede it, to get "outa," not out of, this no
way---and it is through a kind of flesh that we get "outa." This flesh
that has gotten out is what I want to delineate. [[It is the refusal to
be the way we are said to be]{.underline}]{.mark}, a coalitional refusal
marked by "ensemble's para ontological insurgency" [[that does not
straighten, does not disentangle, and does not cohere, all in the name
of an "ungendered plurality.]{.underline}]{.mark}"1 The irruptive work
of Blackness's proximity to trans/gender puts into relief that
[[Blackness and transness are, in the first instance, "political
move\[s\], strategic or tactical move\[s\]]{.underline}]{.mark} ...
movement itself, a displacement between established plateaus ... "2
Black and trans reference movement and cultivate space to live, to
become-as-being, in this movement. [[They reference the process by which
gender is unmoored and unmade as an otherwise way to become a subject in
excess of gender]{.underline}]{.mark}. As we become, both in
unanticipated ways and in ways imposed upon us, [[in addition to ways we
self-determine,]{.underline}]{.mark} there is a displaced and ethereal
movement within us that yearns to breach the bounds of this body that
has been made without our consent. [[Being touched by Blackness and
transness]{.underline}]{.mark} (and they can touch anyone, anywhere,
whenever) [[incites a radically divergent otherwise mode of becoming a
subject, so we come to "be" somewhere different where "being" is
something different.]{.underline}]{.mark} It's the lush fallout of the
touching and being touched: a volatile "viscera that ruptures the
apparent surface," as Rizvana Bradley describes hapticality.3 In this
radical divergence that one, anyone, moves toward ---[[where one
disperses themselves outside the constraints of normative (gendered)
subjectivity]{.underline}]{.mark}---is the processual space in which
gender-that-is-not-gender manifests. A meditation on "ungendering" is of
course indebted to Hortense Spillers, and constituted by an inveterate
Black feminism. [[Gender is that which is made to attach to bodies of a
domesticized space]{.underline}]{.mark}, predicated on the integrity of
an ontology constituted by a white symbolic order. The subjects
contained in the "lab" of the ship's hold, as malleable flesh,
[[ungender subjectivity by way of refusing and being refused by the
necessary symbolic ontology of gender]{.underline}]{.mark}. In the
context of Spillers' other remarks, particularly regarding Blackness's
unfixation from physiognomy, [ungendering might be understood usefully
as a refusal of an "identity," and furthermore of a forced
ontology]{.underline}. Ungendering's undercurrent of Blackness, a
radically feminist Blackness, mobilizes the flesh. To live in the flesh
is not, on my reading, to simply occupy an excoriated, captive
abjection; flesh does not strike me as primarily that which is engulfed
in an ontological state of violation, despite its subjection to searing
and being ripped apart, as Spillers notes. Or, at least not the flesh
with which I am concerned in this essay. [[Flesh is advanced here as an
inexhaustible agility that evades capture and in fact names the constant
eradication of oppressive apparatuses.]{.underline}]{.mark} Moving with
flesh is a generative abolition of normative regimes insofar as it, and
more specifically what I call below [[traniflesh, enacts a subjectivity
that definitionally instantiates the impossibility of (gendered)
normativity.]{.underline}]{.mark} And this, reader, might be a fugitive
un/gendering. (Of note: the slash in un/ gendering marks a necessary
slight departure from Spillers by drawing readers' attention to the
liberatory, uncapturable otherwise of flesh, of traniflesh.
[[Un/gendering's fleshiness is an overflow that spills over violent
categorization and]{.underline}]{.mark}, instead of being generated by
whips and frisks, [[is the unwhippable and unfriskable displacement of
normative violence]{.underline}]{.mark}.) A fugitive un/gendering can be
consolidated into what I want to call, bringing together Spillers, Kai
M. Green, and Treva Ellison: traniflesh. Spillers understands [[flesh as
distinct from the body, the body as embedded in captivity]{.mark} and
flesh as an antecedent "liberated subject-position"]{.underline} that
Alexander Weheliye describes as "nothing less than the ethereal social
(after) life of bare existence." Green and Ellison, in turn, describe
"tranifest"---transformative manifesting---as modalities that
"operat\[e\] across normativizing and violative configurations of race,
gender, class, sex, and sexuality," mobilizing across apparatuses
designed by the state.4 The spirit of these two theorizations is what I
want to put forth in traniflesh. Quintessential to traniflesh is an
[immaterial materiality; a kind of material displacement that lingers in
the vastness and fastness of the flesh, in resentful excess of the
"merely" biological.]{.underline} [[There is an urgent need to
conceptualize an alternative and otherwise mode of living grounded in a
non-exclusionary sociality that escapes captive and captivating logics
of subjectivity. A fugitive un/gendering attempts to vitiate the regime
of gender by way of an unyielding, radically opening
ungendering.]{.underline}]{.mark} We do this through the flesh, through
trans flesh---traniflesh, flesh that throws shade on gender, that erupts
into and out of the force of Blackness, the subversive interrogative
force of Black feminism, and the exuberant that-ness of trans. And that,
with all its [[Black and trans impossibility, provides an impossible
route out of the impossibility of routes]{.underline}]{.mark}.5 This
flesh is a kind of being that refuses being in favor of, or in
apposition to, becoming away from being as being. Traniflesh, then, does
not index matter or form but a general sentient sensorium that exceeds
corporeal capture and is the inaugurative muck that produces
unanticipated relational affinities. Traniflesh intensifies the haptic
and tactile---it's hac (k)tile, in all its connotative touch and
subversive infiltration---and challenges grammar through dehiscence and
fission, [[always coming apart in service of coming together
differently]{.underline}]{.mark}. Moving toward an unsettled
inhabitation of traniflesh is an inhibition that would lead to a transed
subjectivity, a non-normative way of living in, or even beside, oneself.
This is flesh that opposes the systematicity of bodies, which renders
subjects subjugable to the violence of the Law. My articulation of
traniflesh denotes a decidedly un/gendered anti-terrain,
"transorient\[ing\] sex and gender," as C. Riley Snorton provocatively
and poignantly maintains.6 Indeed, flesh is the
gendered-sitethat-is-not-a-gendered-site which welcomes those who were
expunged from traditional symbolics of gender, but also, to advance
Spillers a bit, those who move toward and engage the sociality of the
transgressive posture of fleshiness, engendering a different way of
becoming as that which might have come but for Gender. This "moving
toward" traniflesh indexes what Treva Ellison calls flesh "werq." Werqin
is how we move toward traniflesh, a [[werqing that is understood as a
"performance and praxis of existence that exceed\[s\] the commonsense of
normative categories]{.underline}]{.mark} of social being like gender,
race, class, and sexuality," a surplus and repurposing of the nodes of
our subjectivities.7 Moving toward operates through the [[fugitive
un/gendering occurring here via Black female flesh on the run from
gender, on the run from legibility]{.underline}]{.mark}, by way of a
kind of stepping out of a lineage, worrying the line as it were. The
stepping out of line, the getting out of line, that occurs when one
uptakes fleshiness and its fugitivity ruptures captivity and the social
death it purports to make natural---[[Black flesh's fugitivity is the
onset of a livable life beyond what has qualified as
being]{.underline}]{.mark}. Perhaps it might be necessary, with this
theorization, to distinguish ever so nimbly traniflesh from Spillers'
"hieroglyphic" theorization of flesh. Whereas Spillers links flesh's
activation to violence and criminal wounding, traniflesh, as noted at
the outset of this essay, denotes the getting outa, which should be
construed as an circumscribable evasion of the interpellative call to
violated subjectivity. [[Traniflesh is]{.underline}]{.mark} that which
gets outa Spillersian flesh---[[the excess of the violence, the refusal
of being inaugurated via violence]{.underline}]{.mark}. It is where we
live otherwise outta the necessary violence of eligibilities imposed.
[[Traniflesh emerges as an impossibly possible space where we know not
what will arise because it does not rely on normative
frameworks.]{.underline}]{.mark} As an emergent force and "capacitating
structure for alternative modes of being," traniflesh [[offers different
formations and matterings, different and differing subjectivities, of
material and symbolic life]{.underline}]{.mark}.8 Traniflesh is an
otherwise way of being that exceeds the categorizing logics of race and
gender, that exceeds "identities" and (dis)organizes around subversive
world-building. It is fluid, excessive, a kind of primordial
transitivity that indexes Blackness, Black feminism, and
transness---citing Kimberly Benston's "beginning-as-blackness,"
"primordial blackness";Claire Colebrook's "transitivity"; Kai M. Green's
Blackness in excess of the category of Black; and Spillers' femininity
that evades definition because of its shadowy ubiquity. Traniflesh and
its flitting, flickering inhabitation is a rigorous commitment to
Green's and Ellison's "flexible new collectivities" that, on my reading,
do not conform to the mechanistic templates we understand as legible
identities: race, gender, sex, sexuality. [[It renames and reconfigures
how we become, and continue becoming,]{.underline}]{.mark} subjects that
breach hegemonic logics; traniflesh names that breach, names it as a
place to dwell and, more importantly, fashions another way of
living---indeed, a way that too many of us who are not permitted to live
simply can. [[Gender as a regime cannot do what it purports to do:
control and discipline.]{.underline}]{.mark} The supremacies of
whiteness, cisness, or maleness (and their various imbrications) are
perennially troubled by their leakages. Always is there something that
escapes, something that can't be caught, and these are the instances
that are fertile for making life here. My insistent belief in the impact
of these fleeting instances sometimes presents itself as timid
insecurity, as it seems that such belief is naïve considering the
pervasive oppressiveness that betides Black and trans life. And yet I
still, with the ardor of the Black womenfolk who raised me, believe in
those miniscule moments that give us that sinister grin of persistence.
I love that grin because I see it now as a small flash of being taken
over by the fugitive spirit, of dipping one's toe in traniflesh.
Traniflesh illegible, traniflesh outside this body, traniflesh and lose
yourself beside yourself. Gender won't control you there. [[Traniflesh
is the space that opens up amid gender
disruptions]{.underline}]{.mark}---a habitable space begat by
rupture---and, though difficult to go there for some, stretches out its
arms to let us live finally. I want to get too close to see, so close
you gotta feel through someone elsewhere or somewhere else one has never
one. We arrive together in continuous dislocation. These arrivals
constitute a field---a field of consent---where we consent not to be
unreasonably withheld, and [[consent not to be a single being through
our completely unreasonable entanglement of need and
enjoyment,]{.underline}]{.mark} our joy of missing out, of planning
without splaining. ... What is laid bare in the ungendering of gender
that Hortense Spillers describes is the specifically speculative fiction
and fraction of co-sent---an open field sequestered, a color field
abstracted. To never arrive alone, to render incomplete, to go home, to
never go home, to go back while moving forward in time, is to stick and
move, to strand without landing, in recess doubled down in struggle and
held by all those stranded.9 What if a feeling through another
elsewhere, or Park McArthur's and Constantina Zavitsanos' feeling
through somewhere else one, is a feeling in and as and through
traniflesh? Traniflesh is a visceral vocabulary enlivened through the
fugitive breakdown of gender. Even as one might be enclosed by the
prohibitions of other grammars, [[traniflesh acts as the unpunctuated
and anagrammatical]{.underline}]{.mark} (per Christina Sharpe)
[[subjectivity in which we can escape.]{.underline}]{.mark}

## Method Extension -- Subjugated Knowledges

#### Only through embracing subjugated knowledge and illicit social practices are we able to resist oppressive IR theory and undermine structural violence.

**Richmond 11** Oliver Richmond is the Research Professor in
International Relations and Peace and Conflict Studies at the University
of Manchester \["Critical agency, resistance and a post-colonial civil
society," *Cooperation and Conflict,* Vol 46, Issue 4, 2011, DOI:
<https://doi.org/10.1177/0010836711422416>\] DTS, tag via lilith

Critical agency and resistance In order not to be seen as an exercise of
disciplinary power---and as more critical theorizing in IR has long
recognized (Cox, 1981; Held, 1980)---[[IR needs a theory of resistance
if it is]{.mark} [to overcome its current]{.mark} methodological and
ethical crisis over its conceptual and epistemic approaches and their
liberal and bureaucratic biases]{.underline}.1 As with the discovery
that anthropology was once a colonially constructed discipline in the
employ of colonial governance (Asad, 1973), [IR is shaped by the power
structures it is embedded in (i.e. the 'international community', the
developed north, [conceptions of the liberal/neoliberal state]{.mark},
international organization, its norms and law, [and the global
economy]{.mark}]{.underline}). **[So far there has been little space for
an understanding of the potential legitimacy of resistance, which has
troubling implications.]{.underline}** It might be said that variants of
liberalism, pluralism, normative theory and constructivism represent
theoretical resistance to variants of realism. [However, [they]{.mark}
also [accept the inevitability of structural power over discourses,
social practices, norms and contingency]{.mark}. Such theories offer an
immanent critique which may ultimately be compliant with, rather than
resistant to, power, whether power is represented by military force,
material capacity or normative superiority]{.underline}. Critical and
post-structural theories may represent more radical resistance not
merely on theoretical grounds, but also in methodological and epistemic
terms (Agathangelou and Ling, 2004; Foucault 1984 \[1976\]). However,
[the insight that power always entails resistance and such critical
agency can be constructive not just negative and may carry its own
legitimacy has not yet been fully engaged with.]{.underline} The path
these dynamics indicate diverges: to criticize within the mainstream
paradigms in order to renew governmentality via a broader universalism
(or via 'securitization'); or [the discovery and adoption of new sites
of knowledge that are more contextually representative where
agency]{.underline} (or in Foucaultian terms, 'techniques of the self')
[can prosper]{.underline} (Foucault, 1999: 162). Of course these sites
are not new, but merely shielded from mainstream theories' view by the
inherent bias against resistance. Resistance in either framework is not
as a revolutionary or nationalist form of violence against the liberal
good life for all, as Marxists or nationalists of various early
post-colonialist persuasions might have it (Cabral, 1979). [It is also
not about the exertion of power as realists of various persuasions
envisage, or in the gathering of a broad but thin agreement on norms,
values or discourses, as implied by liberalism, constructivism or
critical theory]{.underline} (Richmond, 2008). It may also not represent
public and mass socio-political mobilization, given the obvious danger
of recrimination. Instead it indicates that government or governance,
whether through power/structure, institutions and norm transfer, is a
'... versatile equilibrium, with complementarity and conflicts ...'
(Foucault, 1999: 162). Power shapes, and the subject also acts within
its field---or possibly beyond (Foucault, 1983: 221). Such a Foucaultian
notion of resistance has very significant implications[. Foucault's
early work identified the subtleties of power and the ways in which
governance came to represent governmentality.]{.underline} It was often
criticized for allowing for little resistance, such was the perceived
disciplinary effectiveness and reach of governmentality. It was also
argued that as he had not explained a clear normative project (or
critique) the motivations of resistance where unclear, thus leading
agency into a paralysis. Worse, it is argued that he was complicit with
'dubious' forms of resistance, including revolutionary violence
(Habermas, 1987; Pickett, 1996: 445--7). This is a mistaken view.
[[Foucault's]{.mark} entire [project of resistance]{.mark}]{.underline},
which spanned much of his career, [[sought to identify]{.mark} and
understand [the effects of power]{.mark} and then later [to
prevent]{.mark} contestation, transgression, struggle and [resistance
from being co-opted into power]{.mark} itself.]{.underline} Resistance
is always possible, little understood, and should not result in new
patterns or institutions of exclusion according to this phase (Foucault,
in Pickett, 1996: 450). For this reason, [resistance should be allied to
a desire not to create new orders or metanarratives, but instead aimed
towards local forms of agency and their international
effects.]{.underline} Later Foucault shifted away from discourses of
power towards social practices of resistance that were in some way
related to power[**. He thought that epistemological understandings of
[resistance should arise from]{.mark} an [engagement with social
practices]{.mark} of resistance, meaning [from subjugated
knowledge]{.mark}**[. This uncovers the technologies of power,
including]{.mark} hierarchies and [structural]{.mark} forms of
[violence]{.mark}, which are unfortunately reinstalled in every
institutional evolution of mainstream, problem-solving theory and
policy]{.underline}. Genealogical method and an interrogation of
institutions, the economy, ideology (including Marxism), was in
discursive and practical terms to disrupt or unsettle power through both
agitation and critique (of an ideological nature) (Foucault, 1980:
151--4). However, power itself often becomes hidden in order to avoid
provoking resistance. [Yet inevitably, power fails to hide completely
because it is hierarchical, unequal and requires material mobilization.
**[Subjugated knowledge is diffuse and]{.mark} eventually [leads to a
framework for politics without hierarchy]{.mark}**]{.underline},
representative of a variety of resistances. It would be egalitarian and
there would be '... a radical pluralism bordering on anarchy' (Pickett,
1996: 456). [Thus, [resistance is not]{.mark} [merely an
antidote]{.mark} to power [but reconstitutes politics]{.mark} according
to those who express agency, however diffuse]{.underline}. **[The point
of resistance is [to elude power]{.mark} [rather than to
confront]{.mark} it head on---a form of 'strategic
knowledge']{.underline}** (Foucault, 1979: 219, 1980: 145, 1984
\[1976\]: 95). Resistance to a theory, state or policy for not meeting
its own normative or technical standards may not necessarily represent
critical agency, in that it merely holds power up to its own standards
which are already biased in favour of the hegemon (Foucault, 1984
\[1976\]: 107--8). [Transformation requires overcoming disciplinary
power rather than accepting its shaping of the subject]{.underline} (a
form of biopower) even if it does so via discourses of rights,
institutions or governance. Such meta-narratives on a global scale
inevitably follow elite consensus or the subtleties of hidden (meaning
subtle) power that the structures which have emerged over history
construct to maintain themselves. As a result, [for Foucault, a 'daily
ethico-political struggle' ensues to create the conditions in which
resistance can emerge in opposition to domination]{.underline}. New
forms of subjectivity can emerge aimed at autonomy and equality (these
are liberal objectives, even though Foucault was critical of
liberalism's attempts at normalizing and domination) (Pickett, 1996:
463). [[This]{.underline}]{.mark} illustrates how Foucault, with his
later reference to agonism as a process whereby new forms of
subjectivity might emerge though a tense struggle with power, [[was
constructing the grounds for a reconceptualization of agency]{.mark},
legitimacy and of peace.]{.underline} This represents a post-liberal
peace, where the local and the liberal interact creating hybrid
processes and outcomes through the social practices and discourses
associated with both compliance with liberal peace frameworks and
resistance, representing a critical agency (Richmond, 2011). [This
argument does not refer to violent resistance]{.underline} (which
Foucault may have indeed tolerated in some instances) [or even
necessarily to notions of civil resistance]{.underline} (which carries a
whole set of implications about how 'civil' is defined by limitations
set by power on acceptable forms of resistance) (Randle, 1994: 198;
Roberts and Ash, 2009). Passive resistance also implies that agency can
only really be exerted politically through public mobilization on a

[emerges from forms of hidden, everyday]{.mark} forms of [resistance,
and their circulation and engagements with power]{.mark}]{.underline}**,

[agency]{.mark} and capacity [of diffuse]{.mark} and disaggregated

critical comments about how power constructs actors and groups in such a
way as to make them malleable to power and its related disciplinary
analysis) (Latour, 2005: 29). Different theoretical and methodological
frameworks---from sociology, anthropology for example---present
different pictures and agency when compared to IR's rejection of
resistance. This agency represents a form of 'dark matter'---a term
describing the 'missing' matter only recently discovered which makes up
a substantial majority of the physical universe. [In the light of
Foucault's contribution, in IR this may indicate grass roots
agency---and the most numerous of actors---disguised from the eyes of
power and its structures, not coordinated or mobilized on a large scale,
nor violent, but aimed at peaceful change and
transformation]{.underline}. Such agency can be seen in a discursive
manner, as a form of critical agency**[. [It acts in a fragmented and

legitimacy or [consensi]{.mark} and its engagement with, or construction

of its ideological baggage and allusions to national liberation. This
has made it difficult to debate about liberalism and neo-liberalism
without seeming to be retrogressive (especially in North America)
(Rorty, 1998: 228). Social forms of democracy as a legitimate part of IR
and its approach to peace have almost become that 'which dare not speak
its name', in the face of the neo-liberal state and global economy now
so dominant a partner for democracy.2 Rorty's solution to this vacuum of
debate is to shift the discussion from solidarity and emancipation
versus the bourgeoisie to greed and selfishness. This can be juxtaposed

internationalism, institutionalism and neo-liberal zeal are often
focused on forms of social democracy, rights, identity, needs and
justice, but with a far broader-reaching inclusiveness of contextual
frameworks and identities than Western rationalism or liberalism allows
for, while borrowing from both. This potentially represents more than
the bourgeoisie social democracies that Rorty regrets may be the
inevitable outcome of this process (2005: 229). It would be hybrid and
far more mixed that the old terminology of a 'mixed mode' (i.e. mainly
economic) suggests. [[It would be]{.mark} politically, socially and

## IMPACT -- SETTLER COLONIALISM

#### The concept of a nuclear family is fundamental to the colonial order -- legal, social, and cultural practices conspire to make the family an adaptable and formidable social institution to enhance colonial hegemony

**Phillips 9** \[Richard Phillips, Department of Geography, University
of Liverpool, Liverpool, "Settler colonialism and the nuclear family,"
*The Canadian Geographer,* 6/1/2009\] sg

[**Colonial societies revolved around nuclear families**.]{.underline}
Colonists did not stand equally before the law or the state: [only
certain relationships were recognized as marriage]{.underline}, [only
certain groupings as families]{.underline} and only these [were accorded
the rights and privileges that went with family membership]{.underline}.
[These]{.underline} rights and [privileges were fundamental to the
colonial order,]{.underline} [conferring social status and also material
wealth]{.underline}. The hegemony of the heterosexual nuclear family was
most pronounced in agricultural colonies that adopted the family farm as
the building block for settlement (Harris 1977). There, it might have
seemed that colonialism needed families, as its units of production and
consumption. Arguably, however, agricultural settlement needed families
less than families needed farms, and family farms were often poorly
adapted to the new environments. Certainly, the story of the
agricultural colonization of South Australia, presented in Don Meinig's
classic tragedy, The Margins of the Good Earth (1963), is one of
families awkwardly inhabiting and in many cases being defeated by an
area better suited to cowboys, wine growers and Aborigines. [**The
colonial family, though broadly hegemonic, was
differentiated**.]{.underline} The form and importance of families
varied both between and within colonies. This paper has illustrated how
even the most closely related colonies---such as New South Wales and
South Australia or Ontario---regulated marriage and sexuality
differently. Within jurisdictions, the same was true. Informal 'country
marriages', as their name suggests, tended to be tolerated in some areas
more than others, while other regulatory systems such as the Contagious
Diseases Acts and Ordinances had their own intra-colonial geographies.
The differentiated historical geography of colonial families can be
explained, in part, with reference to 'big picture' histories of family,
sexuality and imperialism. The families that resettled in Saskatchewan
or Western Australia in the last decades of the nineteenth century were
different, in degree if not in kind, from their earlier counterparts in
the Cape Colony or New England, and this was because the world had moved
on in the intervening years. The systems that underpinned the family and
regulated non-familial sexualities had changed, with innovations in one
place setting precedents that were followed or adapted, sooner or later,
in others. Taking a longer view of these apparently eclectic
developments, Miles Ogborn (1998) argues that [the changing regulation
of sexuality moved with the unfolding historical]{.underline} [geography
of modernity.]{.underline} [It also moved with the historical geography
of imperialism]{.underline}, which was periodically restructured, for
example, through the introduction of self-government in selected British
colonies and the initiation of mass emigration/immigration schemes
during the middle decades of the nineteenth century. **[The colonial
nuclear family reflected]{.underline} [and adapted to these and other
changes, displaying flexibility that only enhanced its hegemonic
position]{.underline}.** [Differences between colonial family formations
can]{.underline} also [be attributed to more local circumstances and
agency]{.underline}. This paper has illustrated how farming communities
tended to be more family oriented than mining districts, ports, cities
or garrisons, where there were more single people, prostitutes and
same-sex liaisons and partnerships. Families were also more common in
established colonies, where sex ratios were relatively balanced, than
they were in male-dominated settlement frontiers. But colonists also
came to different decisions about the importance of family, and the
kinds of family they valued. This was partly a matter of their
backgrounds: of differences between English settlers in South Australia,
Scots in Ontario and emancipated Africans in Sierra Leone. But it also
reflected the choices they made in the new society. Colonial families
were produced and reproduced by colonists, both institutionally and
physically. Families may have been more portable than communities, but
their portability was complex and contested. As Edward Said argued,
ideas (in the broadest sense) do not travel intact but shift according
to 'conditions of acceptance or, as an inevitable part of acceptance,
resistances--- which then confronts the transplanted theory or idea,
making possible its introduction or toleration, however alien it might
appear to be' (Said 1984, p. 227). So, [while the nuclear family
travelled well]{.underline} [and assumed great significance in most
resettlement colonies]{.underline}, [it was also differentiated,
adaptable and flexible, constantly being reinvented]{.underline}
[for]{.underline} and within [particular colonial circumstances and
unfolding imperial histories]{.underline}. My argument that [colonial
nuclear families were produced in, rather than simply exported
to]{.underline}, [colonies]{.underline} resonates with the postcolonial
critique of what Blaut called the 'colonizer's model of the world': the
ultimate attribution of colonial cultural formations to ideas exported
from Europe, which 'seems like' the 'point of origin' (Said 1984, p.
227). Europe only seems like the origin because ideas are changed as
they travel and by the places they travel to. In Dipesh Chakrabarty's
terms, European ideas are 'provincialized'---their meanings renewed and
changed---in the nonEuropean world. [The nuclear family]{.underline},
[like]{.underline} other, sometimes [grander 'European']{.underline}
ideas and institutions including [conceptions of modernity]{.underline}
(Rabinow 1989, p. 289) and Europeanness itself (Stoler 2002, p. 16),
'[may be renewed from and for the margins']{.underline} (Chakrabarty
2000, p. 16). Ann Laura Stoler argues that broad swathes of ostensibly
European political, social, cultural and economic life were generated in
colonial spheres 'and only then brought "home" ' (Stoler 2002, p. 16).
This challenges us to think differently about colonial societies, but at
the same time it brings us back to Turner's insistence on the generative
possibilities of the frontier, and to Harris's on what might
metaphorically be seen as the unprecedented fertility of colonial soil.

#### Colonial world ordering exists within a frame of white racial kinship that naturalizes racial categories of identity into the lexicon of global governance.

**Agathangelou & Ling 4** \[Anna M. Agathangelou, associate professor
who teaches in the history of international relations and women and
politics @ York University, and L. H. M. Ling, professor of
international affairs @ the New School, "The House of IR: From Family
Power Politics to the Poisies of Worldism," *International Studies
Review*, Vol. 6, No. 4, The Construction and Cumulation of Knowledge in
International Relations (Dec., 2004), pp. 21-23\] sg

Specifically, **[IR comes to resemble a colonial
household]{.underline}**. [Its singular, oppositional
perspective]{.underline} (\"I versus You\") [stakes out]{.underline} an
establishment of [\"civilization\" in a space that is
already]{.underline} crowded with local traditions of [thinking, doing,
and being]{.underline} [but proclaimed]{.underline}, in willful
arrogance, as [a \"state of nature\" plagued by fearful
\"anarchy\"]{.underline} and its murderous power politics. **[The House
seeks to stave off such \"disorder\" by imposing
\"order.\"]{.underline}** But the House does so by [appropriating the
knowledge, resources, and labor of racialized, sexualized
Others]{.underline} for its own benefit and pleasure [while announcing
itself the sole producer-the father-of our world]{.underline}. [**Others
qualify as \"**innocent\" children]{.underline}, [wards, or **servants
at best, or \"unteachable\" barbarians at worst**]{.underline}. In
either case, **[Others]{.underline} [must wait]{.underline}** faithfully
**[for]{.underline}** their [**admittance**, if ever, into the House of
IR.]{.underline} Directing Others with declarative statements, [the
House assumes that it knows both the problem (\"power\") and its
solution (\"more power\").]{.underline} [Such suffocation]{.underline}
of Self and Other [leaves a multigenerational legacy similar to the
actual colonial household\'s]{.underline}: that is, erasures and
violences that flip the household\'s original intent. [Order turns into
disorder]{.underline}, repulsion into desire, [purity into
hybridity]{.underline}. In [the House of IR,]{.underline} especially,
another practice [rules]{.underline}: [to treat power as
empire,]{.underline} regardless of public devastations or secret
indulgences. Poisies helps us to imagine a future beyond colonial
capitalist-patriarchy. We call our theoretical framework Worldism.2
Instead of detailing it upfront, we would like Worldism to emerge,
poitically, from our critical review of conventional IR. This approach
demonstrates why we need Worldism, where it fits, and how it comes
about. Nonetheless, we offer a preview here to give an idea of its
orientation. Worldism opens with poisies\' collective, relational
premise (\"you and me\"3). [Rooted in structural social
relations]{.underline} of production, [Worldism acknowledges the
existence of multiple worlds while registering]{.underline}, at the same
time, [their constant]{.underline}, mutual
\"[reverberation]{.underline}\" [and (re)construction]{.underline}. [In
contrast to the House of IR\'s obsessive fear of disorder]{.underline},
[Worldism aims to allow negotiation across difference in the building of
communities]{.underline}. Toward this end, Worldism asks: What is the
problem, according to whom, and why? [Only then can a solution arise
that will serve the greatest good]{.underline} for the greatest number.
Not a house but Gandhi\'s \"oceanic circle\" guides Worldism. Its
\"outermost circumference \[does\] not wield power to crush the inner
circle but give\[s\] strength to all within and derive its strength from
it\" (Ghandhi 1990:348). [Realizing our oceanic
connectedness]{.underline}, we may \"wake up from our personal and
collective delusions to realize who we are, where we are going, and how
we are living \[thereby preventing further violence through
reactionary\] crusades to rid the world of evil\" (Pigem 2002:10-11).
Table 1 compares these key features of Worldism with the House of IR.
Our argument proceeds in three parts. Part 1 identifies
[IR\'s]{.underline} foremost problematic: its [constitution of
boundaries]{.underline} that [fence off a majority of the
world]{.underline}. [Identifying IR as a colonial household highlights
its structural intimacy with capitalist-patriarchy]{.underline}.
[This]{.underline} recognition [accounts for how this house has emerged
in world politics]{.underline} and what it means for unequal social
relations. Part 2 examines the consequences to colonizing IR as an
academic discipline, a source of knowledge production, and a field of
practical politics. We find that elite gestures toward \"fairness\" and
\"representation\" still collude with power ideologically,
institutionally, and practically. [International relation\'s colonial
desires surface when cracks in the system expose acts of racialized,
sexualized brutality by seemingly normal protectors of the status
quo,]{.underline} as seen recently at Abu Ghraib. Part 3 ends with
suggestions for transforming this outdated, (self-)destructive colonial
set-up for an emancipatory, democratic IR. IR Household Relations: In
and Out, Up and Down Our understanding of the colonial household comes
from Ann Laura Stoler (2002). Her study of colonial management in
Indonesia and Indochina under Dutch and French rule, respectively,
tracks their \"genealogies of the intimate.\" She asks: How do
[racialized categories of identity emerge and become naturalized into
the lexicon of colonial governance]{.underline}? Given the potent
politics of categorizing identities, Stoler (2002:8) examines \"the
histories of their making, the exclusions they enabled, and violences
they condoned\" (2002:8). Stoler notes, for example, a legal case in
French-controlled Indochina where a French father sought clemency for
his halfVietnamese son but was refused on the grounds of the son\'s
insufficient \"Frenchness.\" Yet other mixed-blood progeny were granted
European status if they could demonstrate due \"cultural competence,\"
such as feeling alienated when placed among \"natives.\" [The House of
IR exhibits a]{.underline} similar [politics of exclusion and
violence]{.underline}. [It]{.underline} clearly **[identifies who\'s
\"in,\" who\'s \"out,\" and who\'s precariously \"on the
border.\"]{.underline}** [It]{.underline} also [stratifies who\'s
\"upstairs\" and who\'s \"downstairs.\"]{.underline} [This]{.underline}
hierarchical division of space [reflects the house\'s participation
in]{.underline} and complicity with [material relations of production
and its uneven distribution of social wealth]{.underline}.4

## IMPACT -- PROPERTY OWNERSHIP

#### The landscape of property operates under a logic that incentivizes a constricted black female sexuality because the queerness of the black woman's sexuality produces chaos and dismantles the logic of ownership.

**King 13** \[IN THE CLEARING: BLACK FEMALE BODIES, SPACE AND SETTLER
COLONIAL LANDSCAPES Tiffany Jeannette King, Doctor of Philosophy,
Tiffany (Lethabo) King's research focuses on Black gender formation and
sexuality at the intersection of slavery and indigenous genocide in the
U.S. and Canada.\]

[[The Black matriarch head of household confounds and disorganizes the

gender for settler colonial spatial practices]{.mark}]{.underline}. The

property.]{.mark} [Property is required to]{.mark}]{.underline} occupy
and formally own or [[settle]{.underline}]{.mark} a space according to
the terms of what Carole Pateman calls the settler contract.
Heteronormative gender relations produce and enable property in multiple
forms. [[Non heteronormative gender relations confound and prevent the
production and accumulation of property. Black matriarchs disorganize
and upset the settler colonial and heteronormative thrust toward the
production of property.]{.underline}]{.mark} The unruly female [[gender
of the Black matriarch no longer functions as property or produces more
property]{.underline}.]{.mark} The sexuality of the Black female unwed
matriarch is unrestrained Black female sexuality. Black [[female
sexuality that is not restrained and disciplined produces an
undisciplined and unruly Black surplus. The children of Black
matriarchs, as surplus populations, no longer function as property as
they did under enslavement.]{.mark}]{.underline} Not only do [[Black
women's children not function as or make property (clear plantation
lan]{.mark}d]{.underline}) they represent the antithesis of property
during the Black Power era. [[Black children of Black matriarchs are
anti-property as they will likely destroy it.]{.underline}]{.mark} The
Kerner Commission as well as other sociological studies produced during
the 1960s hypothesized that urban unrest was due to poor parenting on
the part of Black matriarchs. [[Black women's sexuality and reproduction
work outside of the normative linear progression]{.underline}]{.mark} of

are queer/ed in this historical moment because [[their gender and

distinguished from other racialized populations for analytical
precision, [[is]{.underline}]{.mark} wholly [[wrapped up in the

forms.]{.underline}]{.mark} Queerness or non normative gender and

alongside of and within the logics of property. Normative settler gender
and sexuality make property---as people, commodities, land, and
possession of land---possible]{.underline}]{.mark}. Property should be
understood as material, social, ideological, utopic and a site of
desire[. [Property is as much about the future as it is the

particularly the conditions of possibility that made it possible.
Property required the elimination of the Native people and enslavement
of Black people. I want to make sure that property is not understood as
inert in the context of this project. [[Property certainly represents a

the landscape of property as one that is always in, what Richard Schein
would say, the process of becoming. Property as a landscape (including
people) also functions as "discourse materialized."84 According to
Nicolas Blomley who provides a more Marxian account of its existence and
