reshaped. According to Barbaras, "the other from the start informs the
prescription of the perceived world"15 such that it "manifests itself to
me as what is immediately accessible to others."16 However, our
comportments in these perceived worlds are not identical with others',
even if inescapably affected or guided by them. Unlike the external
viewpoint framing behaviorist theories of action, there exists "an
internal viewpoint, that of the observer who lives and understands the
behavior."17 David Morris, for whom confrontation with others cannot be
divorced from the spaces in which those confrontations take place,
characterizes perception as "inherently situational and active," wherein
"perceiver and perceived cross and infiltrate each other, and this
crossing already constitutes a field of perception in which the
perceived already has a meaning for the perceiver."18 Building on the
above conceptualizations, I want to suggest that what can be said of
intersubjective interactions can also be said of our interactions with
spaces themselves. For phenomenologist Matthew Ratcliff, "interaction
with \[a\] person makes one feel a certain way,"19 indicating that our
intersubjective experiences with others shape the modal possibilities we
perceive in these interpersonal contexts: the way I interact with an
employer (formal, polite), for example, is likely distinct from the way
I interact with my sister (casual, unselfconscious). Similarly, our
interactions with particular spatiotemporal situations can and do shape
our psychophysical comportments within these spaces: as a female, the
manner in which I walk downtown during a busy day (headphones in, paying
little mind to my surroundings), will likely be different from the
manner in which I walk downtown at night (more alert, shoulders squared,
attuned to sudden motions and sounds), even if no other bodies occupy by
perceptual field. The spaces in which I live and move, in other words,
are spaces that are already overlain with existential meanings with
which I (and others) constitute them, and my intentional actions and
habits in these spaces are implicit responses and adaptations to these
meanings. Accordingly, my perceptual field---or the indeterminate
horizon which allows objects to come into focus---encompasses a set of
background assumptions that make these objects intelligible in ways
particular to me, reflected in my experiences of and behaviors within
them. Liu Shengli, drawing from the Phenomenology of Perception,
reframes 'space' as the foundation upon which Merleau-Ponty's entire
phenomenological project rests. The "primordial" world, on this account,
is the pre-cognitive, pre-reflective world upon which we have a
'natural' perceptual grasp. We can interpret the 'primordial' world as
the emergent spatial context of Edmund Husserl's epoché---
phenomenological reduction---or that which stands once we bracket all
background assumptions and judgments from embodied experience.20 It is
this primordial world which grounds the unity of lived, human
experiences, insofar as this primordial spatiality "originates from the
pre-conscious hold of our body as a natural subject on a natural
world."21 [Primordial spatiality accordingly underlies individuated,
embodied experiences of inhabited space, and the primordial field we
might understand as the absolute indeterminacy of possibility at the
background of all of our (specific) interactions with and within the
worlds of our lived experience]{.underline}. The lived world,
however---that which Merleau-Ponty sometimes calls (and which I have
been calling) the "perceived world"---brings with it its own range of
existential possibilities, and these are the possibilities that
constitute my being-in-theworld and are concomitantly constituted by it.
As Shengli notes, [the communicative relation between the lived body as
consciousness and the perceived world is not an external relation, but
an internal relation of reciprocal constitution]{.underline}. In other
words, this relation should inhere in the essential nature of the two
related terms so that they are essentially defined in its correlation.22
388 Theory & Event In the same vein, Merleau-Ponty credits the "double
function" of the body for generating the dual manifestations of the
world as primordial (or natural) and as perceived (or lived): Knowledge
and communication sublimate rather than suppress our incarnation, and
the characteristic operation of the mind is in the movement by which we
recapture our corporeal existence and use it to symbolize instead of
merely to co-exist. This metamorphosis lies in the double function of
our body. Through its 'sensory fields' and its whole organization the
body is, so to speak, predestined to model itself on the natural aspects
of the world. But as an active body capable of gestures, of expression,
and finally of language, it turns back on the world to signify it.23
Taking together both Mearleau-Ponty's and Shengli's accounts, I offer a
conceptualization of the body-world relationship as the following:
insofar as I live in the primordial world, I inhabit an existential
world characterized by an "internal relation" of existential reciprocity
that shapes my motricity and experiences therein. Just as there exists a
primordial field that frames my inhabited world, so too does there
exist, then, an existential field from which emerges possibilities of
action that are particular to me. We might consider Matthew Ratcliffe's
characterization of the horizon---as that which "incorporates a
harmonious web of salient possibilities for perception and
action"24---in this regard. My existential horizon, as such, constitutes
background assumptions adopted through my lived experiences and
generates a collection of possibilities for meaningful interpretation
and action unique to my lived world. Crucially, it is this field of
possibilities and my capacity to bring them into actualization that
constitutes my freedom. As Merleau-Ponty states, "freedom must have a
field: that is, it must have some privileged possibilities or realities
that tend to be preserved in being."25 Under this schema, one's degree
of freedom is constituted by the scope of one's existential horizon of
possibility, and likewise, one's existential horizon also indicates
one's level of unfreedom. When framed in the context of concreate
intercorporeal interactions, existentiality may be understood more
determinately as sociopoliticality, with degrees of freedom and
unfreedom predicated on the material possibilities for action available
to different subjects. These, of course, are products of social
construction, normalization, and objectivation---what Peter Berger and
Thomas Luckmann would call 'reification' through which "the world of
institutions appears to merge with the world of nature"26 and in which
'legitimation' "justifies the institutional order by giving a normative
dignity to its practical imperatives"27---and hence variable across time
and space. For example, although I---a South Asian woman---and my
colleague---a Caucasian man---inhabit the same academic space, the
existential horizon of my unique being-in-the-academic-world is
radically distinct from his once we take into account the historic,
sociopolitical elements of the American academy that epistemically and
professionally privilege certain bodies over and against others. We
cannot speak and move in the same ways precisely because we do not
inhabit the same sociopolitical world. We are, as Merleau-Ponty
describes the intercorporeal encounter with the Other, "like two nearly
concentric circles which can be distinguished only by a slight and
mysterious slippage."28 It is through our bodies that "we belong to
relatively different worlds, with different forms of visibility and
invisibility,"29 and the differences between us lies in the differences
of live possibilities that exist in our specific, but interlocking and
sometimes overlapping, sociopolitical horizons. In the phenomenology of
space that I am laying out, [economies of fear work to preserve spaces
as belonging to certain bodies rather than others by creating
determinate threats that must then be contained or eliminated. Objects
of fear---that which I call 'threats'---are intimately connected to the
possibility of spatial disruption insofar as those objects press upon us
"an anticipated pain in the future]{.underline}."30 In the context of
the United States, this future pain can most simply be characterized by
the shattered stability of the white world, insofar as this is a
stability predicated upon a hyperprotected and policed space for the
(white) bodies that fear.31 Just as communities require strangers to
establish their borders, Sara Ahmed notes that [fear concomitantly
"makes these borders by establishing objects from which the subject, in
fearing, can flee]{.underline}."32 Drawing on Ahmed's account, Jasbir
Puar observes how "[the materialization of the feared body occurs
through a visual racial regime as well as the impossibility of the
containment of feared bodies,"33 which in the United States has
historically been applied to the black body and has cultivated a
widespread culture of policing, incarceration, and, increasingly, murder
in the name of 'law enforcement.]{.underline}' However, the terrorist
attacks of September 11 generated a new class of 'threat,' "a new
identity category that groups together persons who appear 'Middle
Eastern, Arab, or Muslim'"34 that has been seamlessly incorporated into
already-existing institutions of racialized subjugation. Here, "Muslim"
is used as a racial signifier rather than one of religious affiliation,
and together with 'Middle Eastern' and 'Arab' reflects what Leti Volpp
calls "a racialization wherein members of this group are identified as
terrorists, and disidentified as citizens."35 **[By invoking the
'terrorist]{.underline}**,' in other words[**, the State is able to
identify which bodies are threats to be surveilled, deported, and/or
interned to ensure the flourishing of the 'white'
community**.]{.underline} 390 The operative value of race in Ahmed's,
Puar's, and Volpp's writings clearly reflect Michel Foucault's
characterization of biopolitics as a structure "bound up with the
workings of a State that is obliged to use race, the elimination of race
and the purification of race, to exercise its sovereign power."36
However, for Foucault race operates not in terms of the "racial
epidermal schema" of Frantz Fanon's writings,37 but instead as a tool by
which the State demarcates certain populations socio-spatially, wherein
technologies of discipline function as "a type of location of bodies in
space, of distribution of individuals in relation to one another, of
hierarchical organization, of disposition of centers and channels of
power, of definition of the instruments and modes of intervention of
power."38 This characterization reappears in Security, Territory,
Population where Foucault writes that discipline structures a space and
addresses the essential problem of a hierarchical and functional
distribution of elements, and security will try to plan a milieu in
terms of events or series of events or possible elements, of series that
will have to be regulated within a multivalent and transformable
framework.39 [The sovereign thus holds "the capacity to define who
matters and who does not, who is disposable and who is
not]{.underline},"40 in way that racializes both bodies and spaces such
that fear itself becomes structurally linked to both bodies and spaces.
In the case of blackness, we find that this is manifested both in terms
of the black body---insofar as, for example, black men are
disproportionately subject to police violence---as well as what are
often interpreted as 'black' spaces---such as institutionally
impoverished urban areas where pervasive police presence is viewed as a
public safety necessity. In this theory of racialized spaces, Frantz
Fanon's analysis remains useful insofar as it dispels the myth of
classical phenomenology's conceptualization of the 'body schema' by
exposing its inability to account for the comportment of the black body
in the 'white world.' Under the sociopolitical and economic dominance of
the white man who "unmercifully imprison\[s\]" him, Fanon observes that
[the sedimentation of racial hierarchy leads to a self-objectification,
a development of his own body schema under the authority of the other:
the existential horizon of the black body, according to Fanon, is
situated in a space of white authority that limits the black subject's
existential possibilities and subsequent degree of freedom.]{.underline}
Amidst the interplay between bodies and spaces, Fanon shows us how
bodies are used to demarcate spaces in such a way that different
existential horizons are generated for different 'types' of bodies. Sara
Ahmed articulates this phenomenon of spaces for bodies by contrasting
the inhabitant (of a space) with the stranger (who is out-of-place in
that space): "[Others are recognized as strangers by those who inhabit a
given space,]{.underline}" Ahmed writes, and "who 'make it' their
own."41 If we take 'space' as a field through which we constitute
ourselves psychologically as well as phenomenologically, then
[disturbances of space directly affect our conscious movements within
them while demarcated spaces produce different horizons of possibility
for different bodies therein.]{.underline} The duality of the body/space
nexus is historically rooted in what Sam Opondo and Michael Shapiro call
"institutionalized or power-invested cartographic practices" which
present regulative ideals predicated on notions of the 'right'
relationships between bodies, spaces and times. They also 'police' and
reproduce regimes for the 'distribution of bodies into functions' and
determine what bodies are recognizable and what they can and cannot do
within the spaces and times they occupy.42 From these cartographic
practices emerged certain moral, economic, and political logics that
historically guided imperialism and colonialism, but which have over
time transformed with the advent of advanced cartographic technologies
(among which the drone, as we will see, is crucial), geopolitical
developments, and changing state interests. On a contemporary global
scale---in a world where the individual sovereignty of nation-states is
protected above all else---the perception of bodies as objects of fear
generates 'threats' which "align bodies with and against others"43 and
simultaneously reinforces what Jürgen Habermas calls the
"ethnonationalist" essence of the nationstate (or the notion of a
perceived 'ethnic community' for whom the State is intended).44 Jasbir
Puar reframes this idea as political exceptionalism reinforced through
the production of certain narratives: [in the United
States,]{.underline} she writes, "[exceptionalism gestures to narratives
of excellence, excellent nationalism, a process whereby a national
population comes to believe in its own superiority and its own
singularity,]{.underline} 'stuck,' as Sara Ahmed would say[, to various
subjects."]{.underline}45 On Ahmed's own account, [the 'nation' is
interpreted as an 'imagined community' which must be "constantly
renegotiated, and that negotiation is crucially dependent on
encountering those who are recognizable as strangers, and who demand a
response from the citizen]{.underline}."46 The negotiations that sustain
the national imaginary often operate in response to cross-cultural or
transnational events---such as war or immigration--- and are, as such,
contingently structured. Post-9/11, a new vision of "America" emerged
which synonymized 'Americanness' with liberal values such as,
principally, 'freedom,' which could operate politically and militarily
only by offering 'terrorism' as its antagonist: '[We' Americans are
defined by our freedom, and terrorism is that which aims to destroy what
'we' are. But just as the 'nation' is contingent upon social and
political developments, so, too, is the body that constitutes the 'we'
of the nation:]{.underline} the production of nations constitutes
individuals as belonging to the nation: the work of 'the nation' is done
as much through the everyday encounters in public life, as it is done
through the political machinery of the nation-state. But [the production
of the nation also involves imagining the nation space]{.underline}...
The construction of the nation space takes place alongside the
production of national character as instances in which 'the nation'
itself is fleshed out as place and person. [47 **Once there exists a
global currency of fear**]{.underline}---such as the one we see
today---**[the biopolitical jurisdiction of the State extends beyond its
own borders: because threats to the 'home' can emerge from afar, the
logic of risk management justifies the State's power to racialize,
categorize, and demarcate the bodies in these faraway spaces of
threat.]{.underline}** As Louis Amoore writes, The question of the
designation of categories of citizen, alien, asylum seeker, refugee,
legal or illegal migrant worker, for example, is not a matter of
excluding what is supposedly alien, illegal, illegitimate per se but of
taking them outside, rendering them as other so that their lives and
bodies can be included within governable space.4 [In the United States'
growing]{.underline} [global, biopolitical regime, the value of
bodies]{.underline} (whether they exist or not)
[is]{.underline}---following [a complex history of indigenous genocide,
slavery, racialized socioeconomic practices, and totalizing
globalization---preemptively determined by their perceived spaces of
origin]{.underline}, reflected by such identifying markers as skin
color, religious affiliation, and even name. It is within the framework
of spatial compartmentalization that the 'space of death' emerges. For
Sam Opondo, "[spaces of death" designate spaces "imbued with moral
values that manifest themselves in the incongruous treatment of
violence"---often grounded in "Christian, colonial, nation-statist or
neo-liberal" logics of statecraft---that concomitantly legitimize
certain uses of force as strategically or ethically
"necessary]{.underline}."49 Under this framework, [the geopolitical
compartmentalization of threatening spatial 'units' serves to justify
violent actions committed therein, deployed in the service of a
"biopolitics of humanity]{.underline}"50 [that aims to 'secure' the
health and safety of non-threatening bodies]{.underline}.51 Opondo
characterizes the "space of death" as the nexus which cultivates and is
[cultivated by a "humanitarian ethos"---disallowing life for some as a
means of preserving life for others, framed always through a discourse
of ethics of global humanity rather than one of onto-theologized
capitalist practices---wherein "some of the 'live-saving' measures
deployed in spaces of death are in themselves imbued with 'forces of
death.]{.underline}'"52 We might [think]{.underline} of the United
States' rationalization of the 2001 invasion of Afghanistan as a
campaign to 'liberate' women oppressed under Taliban rule as an example
of the humanitarian ethos at work, utilizing a narrative which was
distributed with great optimism in the early years of the War on Terror
and helped to [legitimize America's promise "to stay in Afghanistan and
foster security, economic development, and human rights for
all]{.underline}."53 Just as the reification of affect into bodies of
threat is guided by narratives of US exceptionalism, [liberal values are
invoked as a justifying force in military intervention; the pure and
democratic veneer, as always, masks the exploitative and imperialist
reality.]{.underline} For Opondo, the notion of the 'space of death'
helps us to problematize one of the many logics of statecraft that the
US has employed in its imperial project of 'global policing.' His
cartographic analysis wonderfully captures the evolution of Foucauldian
biopolitics---[that which constantly seeks to compartmentalize,
discipline, manage, and control---from a statist technique of governance
to one that disregards traditional geopolitical borders, while still
remaining sensitive to the affective forces of this global biopolitical
logic]{.underline}. Nonetheless, its perspective is predominantly
Western and paints the US as the ontologizing author of geopolitics---
["administering death or life by 'collapsing ontology with
geography'"]{.underline}54---without engaging with particular
technologies of identification and categorization (what Foucault might
call disciplinary technologies) that themselves make possible this
biopolitical management of bodies in space. Such an exploration,
however, is necessary if we are to expand the scope of analysis to these
spatio-situated bodies themselves, and it is to this task that we now
turn.

## 2AC A2 (classical) LIBERALISM -- DEFINITIONS OF WAR

#### Liberal definitions of warfare allow for civilian slaughter. The US creates classifications like "enemy combatant: and "militants" in order to mark groups for death. Only the critical geopolitics of the aff is capable of intervening into these violent imaginaries.

**Ahmed 18** (Sabeen, not Sarah. PhD in Philosophy from Vanderbilt
University. "From Threat To Walking Corpse: Spatial Disruption and the
Phenomonology of 'Living Under Drones'". Theory and Event, Volume 21,
Number 2. //lilith)

[Although Pakistan has been the site of most drone strikes]{.underline},
[Yemen and Somalia]{.underline} have also come under the jurisdiction of
American militarism, and [inhabitants thereof have "become part of a
targeted class simply because they live and move in areas in which
terror suspects may operate,]{.underline}" in line with [the United
States' liberal identification of 'militants' and 'insurgents' in its
self-titled "non-international armed conflict]{.underline}": "the US
counts all killed adult males as 'combatants,' absent exonerating
evidence. In other words, [claims that drones have killed hundreds of
low-level fighters may well mask the deaths of civilians]{.underline}."
[The spatial indeterminacy of the very zone of conflict has thus allowed
for the utilization of subject ambiguity as a means of producing
threatening figures]{.underline}---a rather perverse take on the
'sovereign decision'--- and thereby [fulfilling]{.underline} Amoore's
[criteria for risk management as well as providing subjects upon which
this new biopolitics of war can operate]{.underline}. Much can be said
about the newfound ethical, legal, and political complexities generated
by drone warfare, in light of the topic becoming increasingly relevant
for just war theorists, political philosophers, and ethicists.84 [Even
prior to the use of the drone]{.underline}, however, Sam Opondo notes
that "[humanistic discourse and changing definitions of warfare are
predicated on cultural and geographical assumptions]{.underline}" [that
privilege Western notions of the ethical and unethical, just and unjust,
and secure and insecure.]{.underline}85 **[Space itself has become a
tool for legitimizing State violence, and the visual indeterminacy of
subjects only amplifies the biopolitical logic of risk management by
abstracting the subject from her very body]{.underline}**: all persons
living in drone zones become potential conduits of threat, while their
(blurred) bodies have been transformed into imminent targets, red dots
trapped in the crosshairs. Grégoire Chamayou elegantly chronicles the
history of the drone as one of "an eye turned into a weapon,"86 an
airborne panopticon that has drastically redefined not only military
relation to space, but also the very contours of warfare from a clash
between combatants to "a campaign of what is, quite simply,
slaughter."87 [Accompanying this redefinition is the shattering of
once-traditional norms of warfare]{.underline} (particularly that of
mutual risk) and its subsequent transformation into a new norm of
profound asymmetry which falls both outside of the morality of warfare
and inside the paradigm of policing in which "[the distinction between
combatants and noncombatants loses its value for moral
discrimination]{.underline}."88 The hostile zone, under the model of
policing, "remains a space that is left derelict but which, as a
potentially threatening area, definitely needs to be kept under
surveillance"89---much like the United [States' policing of 'urban'
populations as a means of surveilling black bodies and preserving the
security of the white hegemony.]{.underline}90 The consequence of this
topographic categorization on those living in drone zones, as we will
now see, is that [these subjects become]{.underline}, in the words of
Frantz Fanon, "[overdetermined from without," slaves "not of the 'idea'
that others have of \[them\] but of \[their\] own appearance,"91 and
brings with it a new phenomenology of the walking corpse.]{.underline}

## 2AC -- INTERNATIONAL SOVEREIGNTY BAD

#### Transnational governments function through de/reterritorializing space and capturing thought in order to maintain the monopoly on the proper use of violence. This process is replicated in "petty sovereigns" through American and liberal institutional support.

- Good answer to most disads that people will read against this aff
  (Iran, etc.)

**Al-Bulushi 14** (Samar, University of California President's
Postdoctoral Fellow and assistant professor. PhD from Yale.
"PEACEKEEPING" AS OCCUPATION: MANAGING THE MARKET FOR VIOLENT LABOR IN
SOMALIA. //lilith)

[To understand the nature of transnational governance, we need to study
the daily micropractices of its]{.underline} political, military,
corporate, and administrative [actors]{.underline}---who Butler (2006)
might refer to [as "petty sovereigns."]{.underline} [The United Nations
and regional bodies]{.underline} like the African Union, among others,
[sit at the heart of these practices: the actors employed by these
institutions work to produce information, law, and procedure to channel
attention to particular issues in particular ways, while crafting
imaginaries of global governance as a moral and democratic
sphere]{.underline} (Clarke 2009; Malkki 1994). From security sector
reform trainings, to "standard operating procedures" about the treatment
of detainees, to "civilian casualty tracking and analysis," I suggest
that [the peacekeeping operation in Somalia offers initial insights into
the relationship between transnational governance and the making of
security states]{.underline}. THE MARKET FOR VIOLENCE In The War
Machines: Young Men and Violence in Sierra Leone and Liberia (2011),
Danny Hoffman suggests that [we think of violence as a mode of
work]{.underline}. As he explores the conscription of urban youth into
disposable reserve armies in West Africa, Hoffman turns to Mbembe's
(2003) notion of "necropolitics": [sovereign authority in Africa today
consists of the instrumentalization and material destruction of human
bodies and in the exercise of power outside the law]{.underline}.
[Violent authority on the continent rests not with states alone but with
a range of actors: from transnational mining companies, to private
security, to rag-tag militias, and at times, the state
itself]{.underline}. Military manpower is bought and sold in an
increasingly competitive market permeated by fear and insecurity, both
real and perceived. In this context, politics in Africa today is,
according to Hoffman, "not the rational exercise of collective decision
making through institutions in the public sphere" (Hoffman 2011), but
the power and means to determine who may live or die (Agamben 1998).
Yet[, building on Deleuze and Guattari]{.underline}, Hoffman also points
to the fact that[, as states work to retain sovereignty in the face of
deterritorialized acts of violence, they necessarily draw upon not only
other deterritorializing forces but also upon territorialized actors
like NGOs and international organizations]{.underline} (Deleuze and
Guattari 1987; see also Reid 2005; Duffield 2007). As the "[war on
terror" expands across the African continent, these institutions are
assuming key roles in legitimizing the deployment of newly militarized
sociopolitical formations in the name of peace and
security]{.underline}. A closer look at [the relationship between the
"who" and the "how" of brokering certain forms of violent labor can
therefore shed light on the entanglement of rule of law, state building,
and security sector reform projects in transnational economies of
violence and militarization]{.underline}. The parliamentarian I spoke to
may not have had many details on his military's role in Somalia, but he
was fully aware of the ways in which a range of elite interests worked
to represent their presence as vital to national, regional, and
international security, making possible the movement of troops, arms,
and capital to occupy designated "security vacuums" inside Somalia.
AMISOM In December 2006, 50,000 U.S.-backed Ethiopian troops invaded
Somalia with the declared intention of unseating the Islamic Courts
Union---the first stable government Somalia had seen in years (Hagmann
and Hoehne 2009; Lindley 2010; Williams 2013; Scahill 2013). Despite the
illegality of the Ethiopian intervention and the ensuing loss of life,
the UN Security Council discouraged their immediate withdrawal, amending
a 1992 arms embargo to allow the supply of arms, training, and military
assistance for a planned African-led peacekeeping mission to Somalia.4
In just one document, (Security Council Resolution 1744), the UN
simultaneously affirmed its respect for the sovereignty and territorial
integrity of Somalia and rendered legal the illegal presence of foreign
troops.5 Formally operated by the African Union, AMISOM was launched in
January 2007 and mandated to support dialog and reconciliation; to
protect the Transitional Federal Government (TFG) and assist in the
re-establishment of the national Somali security forces; to provide
security for key infrastructure, the repatriation of refugees and IDPs,
and the delivery of humanitarian assistance. Noticeably absent was a
mandate to protect civilians (Williams 2013). AMISOM is now staffed by
22,000 armed personnel (roughly equal in number to the UN peacekeeping
mission in the Democratic Republic of Congo), but its physical presence
is limited mainly to urban areas. Because the troops come entirely from
African states (Burundi, Djibouti, Ethiopia, Kenya, Sierra Leone,
Uganda), the UN and African Union tout the operation as an example of
enhanced governing capacities on the continent. Yet, the involvement of
a range of external actors complicates this projected imagery to reflect
what is referred to as "international hybridity par excellence." With an
approximate annual budget of \$700 million, the United States, UN,
European Union, NATO, China, Turkey, Qatar, and Pakistan provide funds
for arms, "logistical" materials, trainings, maritime security, and
salaries for troops.6 It is the injection of transnational funds that
makes this market for violent labor especially attractive for African
nationals, as troops are promised monthly wages that dwarf the salaries
of most national armies---including that of Somalia itself.7
Transnational funds also pay the salaries of governance professionals
who remain relatively invisible in contrast to the African troops that
constitute the operation's public face. These petty sovereigns
constitute a class of elite cosmopolitan actors whose liberal
sensibilities ensure continued mobility and access to institutions of
power and capital (Clarke 2009; Englund 2006; Ferguson 2006; Galtung
1986). Operating in securitized enclaves in Mogadishu, Nairobi, and New
York, they are employed by the UN Support Office for AMISOM, the Joint
Security Committee, the Joint Financial Management Board, the Technical
Selection Committee, the National Security and Stabilization Plan, the
Somali Reconstruction and Development Plan, the International Contact
Group on Somalia, the UN Trust Fund for AMISOM, the African Peace
Support Operations, the AU commission for African Peace Facility, the UN
Monitoring Group on Somalia, and the Restructuring and Rebuilding Fund
for Somali Security Sector Reform. The list is endless, but significant:
it is [this dizzying web of committees, commissions, and support offices
that connects the "who" with the "how" of transnational governance and
violent labor, as dozens of bureaucrats work to codify and legitimize
the movement of money, arms, and troops into specific
spaces]{.underline}. BUILDING CAPACITIES, BUILDING STATES One aspect of
this work includes the rationalization and funding of "capacity
building" programs to strengthen and reform the Somali national security
forces. With contracts from troop-contributing states (who obtain the
necessary funds from donor governments), [U.S. military officers and the
private security firm Dyncorp train Ugandan troops in Kampala prior to
their deployment to Somalia]{.underline}; Bancroft Global conducts
further trainings of the Ugandan and Burundian troops in Somalia.
Ugandan troops then train Somalis, but the Somalis also fly to Kigali
and Djibouti City for (re)-training by Rwandan and Djiboutian forces,
who themselves are trained by U.S. or private security actors. [Between
the U.S. State Department's ACOTA program and the Defense Department's
Africa Command (AFIRCOM), the United States has been a key player,
providing over \$355 million in "specialized counterterrorism training
and equipment," including unmanned drones.]{.underline}8 **[Inherent in
the liberal commitment to "strengthen" states is an investment in
building their capacity for violence.]{.underline}** As Ralph (N.d.)
observes, the graduation ceremonies for security actors constitute
ritual acts that "confer privileged moral standing in a Christian
genealogy of sanction" (Ralph, N.d. 204). AMISOM celebrates the
graduates of its training programs accordingly: The 1071 men and women
of the SPF (Somali Police Force) successfully finished the course which
was conducted by AMISOM Police Trainers. The training included Rescue
Operations and dealing with kidnappings, VIP protection exercise, riot
and crowd control, handcuffing, and the use of tear gas. The acquired
training will help fill a key gap in the Somali Police Force
capabilities, after years of civil war and armed conflict, while at the
same time, increase the SPF's ability to enforce the rule of law and
protect the communities they serve.9 With new trainees now back in
Mogadishu and widely deployed across the capital, the Somali Police
Force focus highly on maintaining security and building law and order
building law and order throughout Mogadishu. Night and day police
patrols as well as vigorous stop and search procedures in many parts of
the fragile city have changed the lives of local citizens, letting
people walk freely at night without fear of crime.10 These captions are
[the product of the daily work of bureaucratic officials whose
portfolios require that they articulate a symbiotic relationship between
freedom and the policing of the everyday: they are tasked with crafting
particular understandings of law and order]{.underline} (that it
requires a large police presence), [of security]{.underline} (that it
requires "crowd control" and stop and search procedures), [and of the
Somali people]{.underline} (that they require external tutelage to live
"freely"). At the same time, [these materials illustrate the role of
petty sovereigns in the]{.underline} (often farcical) [production of
state sovereignty, as the Somali Police Force performs for the camera
its budding authority over the use of force]{.underline} (Allen
2013:152). It is to this bureaucratic aspect of the market for violent
labor that we now turn. BUREAUCRACY AND THE MANAGEMENT OF VIOLENCE [As
peacekeepers move from one site to another for trainings and frontline
operations]{.underline}, AMISOM [professionals produce and manage
information about violence, engaging in what Talal Asad (2007) refers to
as "the etiquette of death dealing]{.underline}." A recent job
advertisement offers insights into death dealing as an informational
practice. Posted by an organization called Civilians in Conflict, the
terms of reference outline the responsibilities for a "Civilian Casualty
Tracking, Analysis, and Response Cell" (CCTARC) senior advisor, to be
posted with AMISOM in Mogadishu.11 The incumbent's primary duty would be
to establish a Civilian Casualty Tracking, Analysis, and Response Cell.
For this position, building relationships with "relevant stakeholders"
is key: included in this category are the Somali government, bilateral
and multilateral aid institutions, NGOs, and the UN. The incumbent would
draft "legally compliant" civilian casualty standard operating
procedures; he or she would utilize web-based and traditional media "to
ensure that AMISOM can publicize its response to civilian harm and
counter misinformation or deliberate propaganda." The candidate is
expected to possess expertise "in all aspects of civilian casualty
issues; including investigation and/or response," and to have experience
"with or in a military organization."12 At least four things are
striking about this job description, each of which highlights the
convergence between transnational governance and the making of security
states. First, the prospective employee learns at the outset who he or
she is accountable to ("relevant stakeholders" rather than the Somali
people) and, relatedly, that legitimate political actors operate only in
the realm of bureaucratic institutions, in what Arendt terms
"respectable society" (Arendt 2006).13 Second is the stated preference
for someone with experience in or with a military entity, suggesting
that AMISOM seeks someone capable of prioritizing institutional
directives over political or ethical considerations.14 Third is the
reference to misinformation and "deliberate propaganda," pointing to a
mode of governing in which knowledge is rendered suspect and in which
petty sovereigns are tasked with regulating its consumption (Masco
2010). Finally, the employee will be enmeshed in the disciplinary realm
of biopolitics, tasked with managing understandings of life/death and
civilian/terrorist. While there appear to be no existing procedures for
"handling" the loss of life, the employee will be tasked with producing
them. The broad objective, reflective of AMISOM's overall mission, is
the management of violence not its elimination. In this vein, for
transnational governing organs like AMISOM and the institutions it
cultivates, violence is not an exception but a key organizing principle
of governance. This is evident in a November 2013 UN Security Council
Resolution (2124) requesting that AMISOM establish standard operating
procedures for the handover of detainees who come into its custody
during military operations. [Aware of the use of secret prisons and
black sites by some of its most powerful member states,15 the UN and its
partners are compelled to bureaucratize the use of force through the
production of rules, laws, and procedures, and where necessary, to
replace legal procedures with administrative ones]{.underline} (Agamben
1998, Hussain 2003; Johns 2005; Butler 2006; Goede 2012; Khalili 2012).
[Petty sovereigns are thus tasked with glossing over the uneven power
relationships and political wrangling that animate the crafting and
implementing of policies]{.underline}16 (Clarke 2009; Li 2007;
Subramanian 2009), catering to the desires of AMISOM's state and
intergovernmental backers who expect scrupulous financial reports and
esthetically pleasing images of grateful Somalis. [The information they
produce]{.underline}---and the very process of producing it--- [works to
simplify complexity and to cultivate particular
dispositions]{.underline} (Clarke 2009; Feldman 2012) [that
simultaneously dull the senses to violence while creating the
technological and administrative architectures for its replication
elsewhere]{.underline} (Benjamin 2009 (1936), Arendt 1969; Masco 2006).
To produce a more complex narrative is to risk one's job or, more
likely, to invite more work. As Feldman illustrates in his ethnography
of the European Union's migration apparatus, the mundane rituals of
report, speech, and guideline writing become an "agreed upon set of
lenses" (Feldman 2012) designed to "make everything palatable," (Arendt
2006) in much the same way Adolf Eichmann characterized his profession
in WWII Germany. CONCLUSION While the machinations of transnational
governance work to legitimate the movement of bureaucrats, troops and
arms into Somalia in the name of security and the rule of law, competing
interests and actors make for a messy reality. According to the UN
Monitoring Group on Somalia and Eritrea, some of the private security
contractors tasked with training the Somali security forces are not
registered as legal businesses in Somalia and regularly flaunt UN
requests for cargo manifests. Others, like the Dubai-based Australian
private security company Tacforce International, offer their protection
to UN, AU, and Somali officials while violating the terms of the arms
embargo.17 Among troop-contributing states, the Kenyan Defense Forces
(KDF) have reportedly struck deals with local militias to circumvent the
UN banned lucrative export of charcoal from the port of Kismayo: "with
the changeover of power in Kismayo, the shareholding of the charcoal
trade at the port was divided into three between Al-Shabaab, Ras Kamboni
and Somali Kenyan businessmen cooperating with the KDF."18 Fellow troops
from Uganda have protested delayed payments of their salaries, in some
cases by selling their arms to militias and private buyers. Their
commanders, just slightly higher up the food chain, have exercised what
leverage they have to restrict the use of armored vehicles, knowing that
the less frequently they are driven, the more fuel is available to
siphon off and sell.19 In 2008, the UN Monitoring Group estimated that
as much as 80% of the arms, ammunition, and other material supplied to
support the TFG had been diverted for private purposes, to the Somali
arms market, or to opposition groups, including Al-Shabaab (Wezeman
2010). Human Rights Watch reports of indiscriminate mortar and rocket
attacks by AMISOM in civilian areas, leading not only to loss of life
but also repeated displacement.20 [If and when these cracks are exposed,
it is the job of petty sovereigns to frame them as evidence of the need
for more trainings, more arms, and more troops]{.underline}. [In sum,
their job is to ensure that violence, in the public imaginary, occupies
a realm distinct from that of politics]{.underline} (Asad 2007; Clarke
2009; Mamdani 2009; Mullin 2014). To understand the dynamics as they are
unfolding requires consideration of the political economy of state
building, the histories of intervention in Somalia, and the politics
generated by the influx of a range of external actors with competing
political and economic interests. Further still, it requires attention
to, and analysis of, the ways in which the troops negotiate the
uncertainties and vulnerabilities created by their location in this
particular market for violent labor. [The entanglement of state-building
projects in counterterrorism strategies across the continent ensures
that multilateral governing bodies like the UN and AMISOM assume a
prominent role not only in the temporary contracting of violence but
also in building the infrastructure of security states, all the while
reaffirming their right "to grant or deny formal sovereignty," and to
"expand the informal zone where their own sovereign powers can be
exercised"]{.underline} (Cubukc u 2013: 49). As the transnational elites
who manage these operations are more often the beneficiaries (Pingeot
2012) [rather than the targets of securitized spaces and policies, they
become invested in particular understandings of law, peace, and
security]{.underline}. Indeed, the rationalization of AMISOM's work
seems to serve not only the purpose of public legitimation but also a
kind of self-hypnosis though which transnationally mobile elites
persuade themselves of their high moral purpose (Scott 1992). In this
sense, [the contracting and management of violent labor is as much about
crafting the sensibilities of petty sovereigns as it is about the armed
occupation of a given territory.]{.underline} [While it is on the
streets of Kismayo that violence is made real, it is in the bureaucratic
offices of Mogadishu, Nairobi, and New York that it is imagined---as
necessary, as legal, as civilizing, and as peace keeping.]{.underline}

#### The colonial logics of sovereignty and racism are mimicked within anti-racist and independence struggles---forms of colonial mimicry merely reproduce the same violences through which order and being are currently hegemonically overwritten. The desire for recognition motivates mimicking the terms of power, which only extends colonial violence. 

**Agathangelou 15** (Maya, Fanon on Decolonization and Revolution:
Bodies and Dialectics. You already know who she is. //lilith)

[Even as African countries gained 'independence' or 'nominal
independence']{.underline} (Nkrumah, 1965; Ce´saire, 1956) 2 [and
secured greater access to resources and civil rights, they continued to
turn to Europe.]{.underline} In the Wretched of the Earth, Fanon
[challenges mimicry of the European model: European achievements,
European techniques, and the European style ought no longer to tempt us
.]{.underline}.. When I search for Man in the technique and the style of
Europe, I see only a succession of negations of man, and an avalanche of
murders[. The human condition plans for mankind, and collaboration
between men in those tasks, which increase the sum total of humanity,
are new problems, which demand true inventions]{.underline}. (1961, p.
12) For Fanon, [racism and colonialism, and imperialism and slavery, are
contemporary problems, demanding contemporary (non-European)
approaches]{.underline}. [Modern practice of politics and
theorizing,]{.underline} Fanon argues, [presupposes a reconfiguration of
African sites as anarchical and African bodies into black flesh, or the
slave's 'fungibility', before any political or economic contracts can
begin.]{.underline} Though a fiction, these dichotomies of flesh/body
and body/mind still play a role in the disorder of the world[. Slavery
is a 'condition that anyone can be subjected to, to a world which
reconfigures the African body into Black flesh as the African's access
to (or, more correctly banishment, from) ontology']{.underline}
(Wilderson, 2011, p. 18). While Fanon would be the first to admit that
the slave 'body' as sentient flesh is not the only locus of imagining
and materializing global politics, it seems that such an engagement
though is crucial for him for a politics otherwise. An analytical
reading exposes how the slave has accumulated as an 'unacknowledged
debt' in political theory's repertoire since the late fifteenth century
despite our claims to civilization and modernity. Africana and
postcolonial theorists have acknowledged that much 'advancing' the
debate. However, Fanon's problematization of the human from the locus of
the species allows us to go further analytically by providing us the
conceptual repertoire to engage the 'frictions' within the binaries
mind/body, the human and the non-human all the while problematizing the
co-production of history and sovereign law. For a 'fiction,' of more
importantly, that presumes the material life of (the African or the
'Negro') or a 'destiny' which 'is not itself historical' a story about a
site and a body that 'fall\[s\] outside of time' (Marriott, 2012, p. 51)
there seems to be no place. For Fanon, this 'history' and its
naturalization is the problem. It masks its social production as well as
the life that goes into making possible the 'body' and the 'order' anew
with the (white) subject and his/her social contract a reality. [Theft
and property accumulation turn into productive technologies of a
'global' through which social distribution and order are being imagined:
']{.underline}Below the corporeal schema I had sketched [a
historico-social schema]{.underline} \... \[it was\] [the other, the
white man, who had woven me out of a thousand details, anecdotes,
stories']{.underline} (Fanon, 1961, p. 111). Conventional [theories that
argue in support of structures and anti-human projects are]{.underline}
either [unwilling or unable to grasp disciplinary representation of the
origins and evolution of the international order as constituted and
constitutive of the darker side of modernity]{.underline} (Mignolo,
2000) [and imperial adventures past and present.]{.underline} Fanon
problematizes the explanatory power of dialectics and political
philosophy whose dominant understandings feature the possibility of a
transformative human nature, as bankrupt in the face of the black and
the colonized subject. Following Fanon, at [the 'crux of modernity's
crisis is the dilemma of 'how to represent the Negro as being
demonstrably human within the terms of the law']{.underline} and the
dilemma of how to articulate that body' (Agathangelou, 2012, p. 452;
Wilderson, 2011, p. 45) as bodies, selves, and structures mutually
constitute one another, albeit in a disjunctive manner. Fanon states:
'[If we want humanity to advance a step further, if we want to bring it
up to a different level than that which Europe has shown it, then we
must invent and we must make discoveries]{.underline}' (1961, p. 136).
In the Wretched of the Earth, [Fanon contrasts Europe and history to the
invention of nature and the creation anew of 'all corners of the
globe']{.underline} (Fanon, 1961, p. 311). In short, he moves away from
a geometric or mere empirical understanding of the world. [Instead of
merely presuming what this global world means and what kind of
institutional arrangements and practices it might refer to, he asks what
kind of world is constituted, and what kind of ideas, institutions,
practices, and relations are made possible]{.underline}. This is what I
do here as well. In asking what work is being done with dialectics, by
dialectics in the co-production of the international structures
(contingent civil society and secular state) and subjects
(species/body/mind), I highlight the ways Fanon calls attention to 'real
leaps' to invent the new 'man' itself a (socio-technical imaginary), and
the new earth by focusing on the black--white relation/Master--Slave
Dialectic. Engaging simultaneously two important thematics central to
any conversation of dialectics today on the black and white
relationship, Fanon pushes for [articulating invention as a conceptual
resource that can contribute to the rupture of history and
politics]{.underline} (Marriott, 2011). He argues that for invention to
generate a humanity that is not degraded 'either in the face of its
finitude or against the backdrop of the great outdoors' (Negarestani,
2014, p. 1), suffering and resistance are not enough. For him,
[invention that has as its premise a creative leap without
self-destruction is a rupture irreducible to capture or
valuation]{.underline} (Marriott, 2011). Fanon begins by engaging with
the structure of this commitment; specifically, practicing these
relations may entail a violence that cannot be removed by sublation,
thus, demanding that we read critical works and their notions with
attention to their effects on and production of bodies. In Black Skin
White Masks, he considers whether the relationship of violence between
blacks and whites can be reversed. In 'The Negro and Language,' he
writes: 'On the philosophical level---that is, the plane of the basic
needs of human reality---I am willing to work on the psychoanalytical
level---in other words, the level of the 'failures,' in the sense in
which one speaks of engine failures' (Fanon, 1967, p. 23). For him,
failures are a result of the privileging of ontogenetic and phylogenetic
analyses of the black experience, recentralizing the need for the black
person to look to white people for recognition (Fanon, 1967, p. 1).
While he acknowledges that not all blacks will 'find themselves in what
follows,' he also says: 'This book is a clinical study. Those who
recognize themselves in it, I think, will have made a step forward. I
seriously hope to persuade my brother, whether black or white, to tear
off with all of his strength the shameful livery put together by
centuries of incomprehension' (Fanon, 1967, p. 12). For Fanon, '[To
speak, means to be in a position to use a certain syntax, to grasp the
morphology of this or that language, but it means above all to assume a
culture, to support the weight of a civilization]{.underline}' (1967,
pp. 17--18). [Many black men are learning to speak the language of the
master to be recognized and to move up socio-economically and
politically]{.underline}: 'The black man is supposed to be a good
nigger' (1967, p. 35). Yet 'when a Negro talks of Marx, the first
reaction is always the same: "We have brought you up to our level and
now you turn against your benefactors. Ingrates!"' (1967, p. 35). For
Fanon, [learning the language of the master to sublate the negativity
that the black body is inscribed with in the black-white relationship,
this mediation is a fiction.]{.underline} Learning [the language of the
master presupposes and reasserts the hegemony of whiteness, whereby
white decision-makers are already legitimate and do not require
approval; doing so presupposes the humanity of whites and the
fundamental inferiority and even non-existence of blacks]{.underline}.
Fanon elaborates another aspect of the colonial relation, the erotic,
foregrounding that the black--white relationship is also about sexual
power. The alternatives supplied under the rubric of freedom re-orient
one to believe love as a form of recognition able to sublate the
master/black violence. Yet, those blacks attempting to gain white
recognition by pursuing erotic love are participating in a neurotic
phenomenon. Fanon writes, describing Capecia: Mayotte loves a white man
to whom she submits in everything. He is her lord. All I know is that he
had blue eyes, blond hair, and a light skin, and that I loved him. It is
not difficult to see that a rearrangement of these elements in their
proper hierarchy would produce something of this order: 'I loved him
because he had blue eyes, blond hair, and a light skin'. (1967, pp.
42--43) 'What Mayotte wants,' he explains later, 'is a kind of
lactification. For, in a word, the race must be whitened' (1967, p. 47).
For Fanon, pursuing erotic love as a form of recognition is bound to
fail. Through an analysis of Mennoni's work on Caliban and Prospero, he
argues that [the psychology of colonization is equally problematic in
the ways it explains the 'dependency complex' and is not going to
transform and change the relation even if it is sexual and
intimate.]{.underline} He cites Mennoni as saying 'Not all peoples can
be colonized; only those who experience this need' (1967, pp. 88--89).
Fanon rejects this and asks: Why \... does he try to make the
inferiority complex something that antedates colonization? \... The
appearance of varicose veins in a patient does not arise out of him
being compelled to spend ten hours a day on his feet, but rather out of
the constitutional weakness of his vein walls; his working conditions
are only a complicating factor. And the insurance compensation expert to
whom the case is submitted. (1967, p. 393) If the architecture of the
body politic is unstable, with a weak somatic constitution, Fanon
argues, institutions, authority, and legitimacy are already weakened,
questioning whether intimacy and universality can be thought within
these historically given frames, let alone attained. In the final
chapter of Black Skin White Masks where he engages psychoanalysis as an
approach to disalienation, dehumanization, and repression and as an
effort to develop an understanding of the black--white relationship as
one of disorder, Fanon says, 'In response to the requirements of
dialectic one should investigate the extent to which the conclusions of
Freud or of Adler can be applied to the effort to understand the man of
color's view of the world' (1967, p. 141). These tools are not useful to
explain the experience of black people, many of whom become neurotic,
not because of 'certain infantile elements' (1967, p. 141) but because
of anti-black racism, white supremacy, sadistic and fascistic
institutions, all of which are the everyday of the modern world disorder
(1967, p. 149). Many psychological phenomena, according to Fanon, can be
traced to anti-black racism, white supremacy, and the profound fictions
that characterize them, but not in the abstract. This is true for black
people, whose existence and humanity are produced as structurally
impossible (Agathangelou, 2010, 2011; Wilderson, 2011) and it is true
for white people, many of whom suffer from negrophobia and
negrophilia---an ironic psychosexual phenomenon that cannot, Fanon
argues, be understood without appealing to sociogenesis: 'For the
majority of white men \... .the Negro represents the sexual instinct (in
its raw state). The Negro is the incarnation of a genital potency beyond
all moralities and prohibitions \... We have shown that reality destroys
all these beliefs' (Fanon, 1967, p. 177). In this final chapter, Fanon
argues that [the psychoanalytic approach does not 'dialectically' lead
to the disalienation and humanization of the human in
general]{.underline}. He [exposes the limits of classical
psychoanalysis; its ontogenetic and phylogenetic tools are
constitutionally incapable of understanding the effects of
socio-political structures on the individual psyche, especially the
psyche of a subject not constituted as human but as mere
flesh]{.underline}: 'The Negro problem \... does not resolve itself into
the problem of Negroes living among white men but rather of Negroes
being exploited, enslaved, \[and\] despised by a colonialist, capitalist
society that is only accidentally white' (1967, p. 202). [The Negro
'problem' cannot be resolved by having Negroes live with white men. It
is a question of recognizing, first, the problem]{.underline}. The
Negroes are 'exploited, enslaved and despised by a colonialist and
capitalist society that is accidentally white' (1967, p. 202). If this
is the Negro 'problem,' [it suggests the soma (body politic) itself is
in question; in other words, how do society and subjects come to
co-imagine themselves as 'white', propertied, and 'global' and with what
implications on the production of bodies?]{.underline}

## 2AC -- NATION STATE SUX

#### Nations are constructed through our affective investment into the logic of the strange and familial. Through the creation of "strangers" domestically the nation is able to mark others as "strangers" globally in order to preserve its coherence

**Ahmed 2k** (Sara, "Strange Encounters: Embodied Others in
Post-coloniality" pages 97-102. Former Professor of Race and Culural
Studies at Lancaster University and quit to protest. We stan. //lilith)

[How are nations invented and imagined]{.underline}? The work of
Benedict Anderson is crucial for an understanding of how 'the nation' is
imaginary or fantastic. According to Anderson, [both nationalism and
nationhood are 'cultural artefacts' (1983: 13), with their own
particular 'style in which they are imagined']{.underline} (1983: 15).
Such an approach allows us to recognise that [the boundaries of nations
are not simply geographical or geopolitical]{.underline} (though they
take both these forms), [but also discursive. The nation does not refer
to something that simply exists: nations are produced and constructed as
places and communities in which 'a people' might belong]{.underline}. Of
course, to say that nations are imagined is not to say that they are not
real. The question of nationhood cannot be properly addressed without a
recognition of the political economy of modern nation-states. An entity
can be imagined and real at the same time: in some sense, the opposition
between imaginary and real must be suspended if we are to understand how
the nation comes to be lived as an 'organic community'[. The imagining
of the nation as a space in which 'we' belong is not independent of the
material deployment of force, and the forms of governmentality which
control, not only the boundaries between nation states, and the
movements of citizens and aliens within the state, but also the
repertoire of images which allows the concept of the nation to come into
being in the first place.]{.underline} To think of the nation as
simultaneously imaginary and real (the nation as both fantasy and
material effect) is [also to think of the processes of identification
which allow the nation to be secured as one of the organising
assumptions of 'public life']{.underline} (Goffman 1972: ix). [What
forms of identification are produced with the nation? How does 'the
nation' come to be produced as a form of identification?]{.underline} In
other words, we need to examine how the invention of the nation as a
bounded space requires the production of a national identity which can
be claimed by the individual ('I am ... ') through reference to the
apparent transparency and coherence of the nation itself. [The
investment of the individual in being or having a nation suggests that
the discourse of nationhood operates at both a psychic and social level.
The individual]{.underline}, who encounters others in daily life, [comes
to identify as not only having but being a nationality,]{.underline}
through referring to public symbols and expressions which themselves
tell stories of what it means to be that nationality (the fleshing out
of the 'national character'), [and also through identifying with other
individuals with whom such stories can be shared]{.underline}. The
production of the nation involves not only image and myth-making -- the
telling of 'official' stories of origin -- but also the everyday
negotiations of what it means 'to be' that nation(ality). [The
production of the nation involves processes of self-identification in
which the nation comes to be realised as belonging to the
individual]{.underline} (the construction of the 'we' as utterable by
the individual). Crucially, then, [the production of nations constitutes
individuals as belonging to the nation: the work of 'the nation' is done
as much through the everyday encounters in public life, as it is done
through the political machinery of the nation-state]{.underline}. But
the production of the nation also involves imagining the nation space:
[it involves the projection of boundaries]{.underline} (nationhood as
cartography), [and the telling of stories about the authentic
landscape]{.underline} (for example, in travel writing and tourism), and
the production of interiority (imagining 'the heart of the nation').
[The construction of the nation space takes place alongside the
production of national character as instances in which 'the nation'
itself is fleshed out as place and person]{.underline}. The nation
becomes imagined as a body in which personhood and place are
precariously collapsed. [Through a metonymic elision, the individual can
claim to embody a nation, or the nation can take the shape of the body
of an individual ('bodyscape').]{.underline} But how do [the complex and
multiple sites of the production of nation- hood involve the reification
of the figure of the stranger]{.underline}? As I pointed out in my
introduction[, the formation of a dwelling or place of residence
involves a definition of who or what does not belong]{.underline}
(estrangement). At one level, such [distinctions between the familiar
and the strange take place in everyday encounters]{.underline}. [The
self-identification with the nation also involves the recognition of
others as belonging to the 'same' community]{.underline}. If we return
to my discussion of recognition in Chapter 1, we can consider how the
act of hailing or recognising some-body as a shared member of a
community serves to produce or flesh out that community through or
against the bodies of strangers. For example, [the recognition of
another as belonging to the same nation when travelling abroad relies on
techniques for reading and telling the difference between that which is
familiar and strange]{.underline}. The recognition of the other as from
a shared nation requires a rehearsal of a public discourse of nationhood
(shared memories of the nation space, the use of jokes, reference to
sporting events or national achievements). The recognition of others as
being from the same nation, or as sharing a nationality, hence involves
an everyday and much rehearsed distinction between who does and does not
belong within the nation space. [The production of the
nation]{.underline}, in such a model[, requires some-body or some-where
to not-be in order for it to be. This demarcation of spaces of belonging
through estrangement is central to Said's now classical theory of
Orientalism: the Occident comes into being as a material place through
the creation of an ontological distinction between it and the
Orient.]{.underline} [The Orient comes to embody that which the Occident
is not.]{.underline} That is, the Orient creates Europe (or the
Occident) as a bounded space in the very event of being positioned as
its Other. The fascination with the Orient within Western imperial
culture is a fascination which, in Said's terms, creates an idea of
Europe, 'a notion collectively identifying "us against the
non-Europeans" ' (1978: 7). [Orientalism creates an imaginary
geographical divide based on the binarism of
Occident/Orient]{.underline}. In this sense, [the creation of a space of
belonging]{.underline} (the 'we' that remains unspoken, or is spoken
only through the claiming of the right to speak) [requires that which is
strange in order to be. The nation is one such space of
belonging.]{.underline} Robin Cohen's reflection on the discourse of
nationhood emphasises how national identity is produced through the
differentiation between familiar and strange: 'a [complex national and
social identity is continuously constructed and reshaped in
its]{.underline} (often antipathetic) [interaction with outsiders,
strangers, foreigners and the "others]{.underline}". [You know who you
are, only by knowing who you are not]{.underline}' (1994: 1). In Cohen's
model, strangers are not external to the formation of national identity:
the nation requires strangers in order to exist. Despite this, the
formation of identity is defined in terms of frontiers: strangers are
the ones who are encountered at the border, and whose proximity
threatens the coherence of national identity. Féher and Heller's
consideration of nations poses an analogy between 'the nation' and the
'house': 'a country has a certain number of inhabitants who are supposed
to close the doors behind them, not to let in any casual stranger
without a preliminary agreement; however it can also open its doors'
(1994: 143). Here, the question of the stranger in the nation is a
question of opening or closing the doors: the stranger can either be let
in or kept out. What I want to suggest in contrast is that [the
definition of the nation as a space, body, or house requires the
proximity of 'strangers' within that space, whether or not that
proximity is deemed threatening]{.underline} (monoculturalism) [or is
welcomed]{.underline} (multiculturalism). A key problem in this
literature on nationhood is the use of the model of 'generalisable
other', in which national identity is simply defined through and against
an other, as such (Cohen 1994: 1). What is demanded is a much more
contextual and nuanced understanding of the role of differentiated
others in the demarcation of national identity. In Chapter 2, I examined
how bodily integrity is produced, not simply through or against a
generalisable other, but by differentiating between others, who have a
different function in establishing the permeability of bodily space.
[The very habits and gestures of marking out bodily space involve
differentiating 'others' into familiar]{.underline} (assimilable,
touchable) [and strange]{.underline} (unassimilable, untouchable). While
I would not want to imply that we can make a simple analogy between
bodies and nations, I think we can understand how [bodily and social
spaces leak into each other, or inhabit each other]{.underline} (see
also Chapter 4). The nation becomes imagined and embodied as a space,
not simply by being defined against other spaces, but by being defined
as close to some others (friends), and further away from other others
(strangers). In this sense, only some others are read as strangers
within the nation space. [The proximity of strangers within the nation
space]{.underline} -- that is, the proximity of that which cannot be
assimilated into a national body -- [is a mechanism for the demarcation
of the national body, a way of defining borders within it, rather than
just between it and an imagined and exterior other]{.underline}. As
Christine Inglis demonstrates, [the encountering of cultural difference
within the fantastic nation space allows the work of nation formation to
be sustained: strangers, those who are not recognised as 'typical' of a
nation, might allow the question of what it means to be a nation to be
posed (again and again).]{.underline} Inglis argues that, 'Many nations
are having to confront the issue of how they respond to these
"strangers" in their midst and how they incorporate them within their
existing society and institutions. In so doing, the issue of national
identity as a symbolic and legal issue inevitably comes to the fore'
(1997: 204). The proximity of strangers requires the nation to respond:
there is a constant process of adjustment and transformation. The nation
is not secured in the process of keeping strangers out: the stranger's
proximity is required if the stranger is to be known as the limit of
'the nation'. National identity emerges as a site of social conflict:
there is a constant redefinition of who 'we' are through the very
necessity of encoun- tering strangers within the nation space. As
Zygmunt Bauman puts it, the question is not, 'how to get rid of
strangers and the strange, but how to live with them -- daily and
permanently' (1997: 55). [National identity is unstable, and emerges
through multiple encounters between those who assume themselves to be
natives and those recognised as strangers, as out of place, in
this]{.underline} place. The response to strangerness in the discourse
of nationhood is hence built around the question of what it means to be
'in place'. The assumption that strangers only populate the borders of
the nation is in danger of reifying those borders: rather we need to
understand the process of negotiation between identity and strangerness
as ongoing, and as moving across different spatial formations (the body,
home, the neighbourhood, the city, the country, the region, the nation,
the globe). Immigration policy and border controls are not the only
places in which the question of 'the stranger' is posed for and by the
nation. National identity is metonymically related to other sites of
identity formation: [through marking out spaces of dwelling, spaces
which are familiar and inhabitable, subjects are interpellated into
multiple regimes. Like other forms of identity, nationhood is constantly
renegotiated, and that negotiation is crucially dependent on
encountering those who are recognisable as strangers, and who demand a
response from the citizen:]{.underline} who are they? do they belong
here? who am I? who are we? The distinction between native and stranger
within a nation is not simply enforced at the border: rather, that
distinction determines different ways in which subjects inhabit -- which
involves both dwelling and movement (see Chapter 1) -- the space of the
nation. [The figure of the stranger is also constructed at the level of
governmentality as the 'origin' of the very question of national
identity:]{.underline} how do we live together, as one or many (= the
strategic question of monocultural or multicultural government policy)?
Or even, who is the 'we' of the nation if 'they' are here to stay?
[Thinking about multiculturalism must begin, therefore, with an under-
standing that the coherence of the 'we' of the nation is always
imaginary and that, given this, such a 'we' does not abolish cultural
difference, but emerges through it]{.underline}. Multiculturalism is
always contested, whether at the level of government policy, or whether
in critical scholarship and resistant political activity.
Multiculturalism is one of many historically specific negotiations of
'the nation'. There are key national and regional differences in how a
discourse of multiculturalism emerges and is contested (see Rystad
1997). As Theo Goldberg argues, 'Multiculturalism and commitments to
cultural diversity emerged out of the conflictual history of resistance,
accommodation, integration and transformation' (1994: 7).
Multiculturalism can name a range of activities in education, as well as
in grass roots political campaigns, which are based on the revaluing of
minority cultures, and hence on the promotion of differences that are
not accommodated within the official discourses of nationhood (Gunew
1994: 5). But, in other instances, [multiculturalism works as an
'official' discourse: that is, it involves the setting of governmental
agendas on what it should mean to be 'a nation]{.underline}'.

#### The nation is made into a place where 'a people' and 'a family' may belong. This process intertwines our affective investment into the nation state -- therefore the nation-state is not an inherent fact of belonging but is reproduced on a social level.

#### State theory formulates infrastructures of tolerance that suggest that there must be an original understanding of the other while forefronting the ideology that inclusion is both necessary and possible by prescribing a belief of what progress appears.

**Rodriguez 19**. Dylan Rodríguez is Professor of the Department of
Ethnic Studies at UC Riverside. He received his Ph.D. and M.A. degrees
in Ethnic Studies from the University of California, Berkeley (2001),
and earned two B.A. degrees and a Concentration degree from Cornell
University (1995) \["Insult/Internal Debate/Echo," 2019, *Propter Nos
Vol. 3*\]

always necessarily [[a theater of racial nationbuilding with vacillating

infrastructures of tolerance and repression are never finally
separable**.]{.underline}]{.mark} There is [[a deadly coherence to the
historical variations of this statecraft]{.underline}]{.mark} and

counter-positions within their fields correspond with redemptive
possibilities. [[The historical record shows]{.mark} what the allegation
of redemption entails when waged and executed by [the subjects of

vindication]{.mark}]{.underline}: formed in the crucible of the

unbroken [chain of racist casualties produced through systemic]{.mark}
and scene specific [assaults on both collective physiology and
the]{.mark} mere [possibility of shared, symbolic integrity. The deepest
discrepancy is in the pretense that there is any possibility
for]{.mark}]{.underline} categorical rather than piecemeal (temporary,
fleeting, flimsy) [[inclusion in this]{.underline}]{.mark} apartheid
[[speciation of "humanity," an apartheid that cannot be dismantled in
law]{.underline}]{.mark} or even nominally abolished, [[unless
Mankind---]{.underline}]{.mark}and crucially, the material and symbolic
grounds of [Mankind's global integrity as such---[becomes the subject of
a creative disintegration (some will call this terrorism, others call it
their hope for tomorrow).]{.mark}]{.underline} The speciation draws its

but not limited to genes, spirit, blood, epidermis, and cranial capacity

fullest distensions of its [colonial and chattel formation, as an
internal debate over the relative violence, vulgarity, sophistication,
and political capaciousness of white
humanit]{.mark}**[y]{.mark}]{.underline}. The latter, you will recall,
is [[the position that offers humanitarian favors against the wish and
urge to violently decline them.]{.underline}]{.mark} There is a claim
that persists beneath the din of civil accommodations, that changes in
tenor but not in premise. Accusations of [[backwardness, savagery,
tribalism, and even fundamentalism become self-replicating when such
humanitarianism is rejected by the ungrateful, suspicious antagonists of
white humanity's creeping universals. The language of urban-to-rural

It is in times like this that the white human creates the groundwork of
