#### In order to disengage with violent relations of power we must refuse the call to be included within the universal of the state. Only then can we find new ways to relate and be in the world.

**Sebastian 18** \[J Sebastian is a graduate student in Ethnic Studies
at the University of California, Riverside and holds a Juris Doctor from
the City University of New York School of Law; "Already Something More:
Heteropatriarchy and the Limitations of Rights, Inclusion, And the
Universal"; 6/1/2018;
https://abolitionjournal.org/already-something-more/\] sg

The concept of the universal is one borne out of inherently limited
access---of those who are considered civil within a system that
predetermines which bodies will have an easier time accessing the
protections, privileges, and benefits of the state. I have argued that
**[the concept of inclusion into the universal is always already limited
by its colonial construction---its framework is conditioned by white
supremacy, heteropatriarchy, and capitalism]{.underline}**. This affects
all people, but queer and trans people of color occupy a pronounced
positionality within the intersectional spaces between these three
systems of power. [If the goal of resistance work is to abolish systems
of power, and in turn to abolish colonialism, then as long as
colonialism and its ongoing instantiations of settler colonialism and
neocolonialism continue]{.underline}, [rights will never bring about the
change queer and trans people are seeking]{.underline}. So we must ask
ourselves, when we seek justice, is it rights that we seek? Or is it the
"something more" we have always already inhabited? Mainstream [LGBT
people, white people, and **people** with]{.underline} access to [wealth
**must all disengage from the benefits of these systemic power relations
to consider something different**]{.underline}**,** something that will
not bring more people further up in the hierarchy so that the structure
grows, but rather **[something that will seek to dismantle it
altogether]{.underline}**. For, in the words of Audre Lorde, **[the
master's tools will never dismantle the master's
house.\[]{.underline}**68\] [We already practice this critical work
within our everyday lives]{.underline}. **[Our communities are invested
in practices of care;]{.underline}** **[in resisting the confines of
"expected" behavior; questioning]{.underline}** [logics of
heteropatriarchy in our lives; working through our own understandings of
gender]{.underline} outside a strict gender binary; [offering support,
healing, and harm reduction practices outside what the state
condones;]{.underline} [working through]{.underline} our own
constructions of [conflict resolution and community
accountability]{.underline}; creating chosen families, practicing
polyamorous relations, engaging in friendships that center love, and
working beyond heteronormative constructions of jealousy and
property-based relations that found heteronormative dyadic expectations.
[From the moment colonialism extended its]{.underline} violent
[reaches]{.underline} [through]{.underline} projects of
[Western]{.underline} [imperial expansion]{.underline}, Indigenous
people and people living under colonialism worldwide have been resisting
colonial heteropatriarchy. W[e are engaging in everyday practices of
building and rebuilding, practicing, learning, and relearning how to
live and love in the face of these systems]{.underline}, resisting calls
for conscription into the interrelated and violent disciplinary projects
of heteropatriarchy, capitalism, white supremacy, and colonialism. This
work demonstrates that [**in these practices, we are already something
more than what we are told the universal space of inclusion will
offer.** Ongoing histories]{.underline} and practices of struggle and
resistance [have demonstrated what is at stake when our focus is solely
on inclusion into the universal]{.underline}. [Moving from a space that
questions universal inclusion while fighting for a redistribution of
resources through many tactics allows for a consideration of what
possibilities for relationality are foreclosed when engaging primarily
in rights-based redress,]{.underline} [as well as what other
possibilities exist for offering other ways of being in the
world]{.underline}. In order to fully account for the realm of the
universal, framing the development of universality as occurring within
the rise of colonial-modernity can expose the dynamic between rights and
the legitimations for colonialism as based on heteropatriarchy. [To see
these power relations not as separate, but in fact as constitutive of
the formation of the "universal" of modernity, exposes the limitations
of rights-based incorporation as a fundamental reaffirmation of
settler-colonial state power]{.underline} used to negotiate the freedom
and legitimacy for some as constantly conditioned on the unfreedom and
illegitimacy of others. As critical queer and trans movement work has
articulated, [resistance to these power relations is the work that
involves everyone]{.underline}, [which must comprise leaving behind our
investments in inclusion]{.underline} under the façade of universal
equality [and open our eyes to the ongoing work that is already
happening]{.underline} through engaging with communities in resistance.
[The lives we live are complicated lives]{.underline}, with complex and
intertwined relationships to colonial heteropatriarchal logics. Our
histories are multifaceted, and live through us in our ongoing
struggles. Let us remember to tell these histories as they are---in
their complications, in their difficulties, and in the hope of what it
means when we work through and against them in building together. And in
holding this, [we must also hold the awareness that the institutions and
projects of the universal]{.underline} [will always seek to co-opt
us]{.underline}, to bring us in so that we may have the privileges [to
fight their wars and marry in their churches and extract their profits
and expand their diversity;]{.underline} [so that we might
have]{.underline} a coveted set of rights promised to all deserving
citizenry, [the right to carry on the neocolonial and imperial violence
that is the project of the United States]{.underline}, [the right to
settle and perpetuate investment in this stolen land, the right to the
pursuit of multicultural white supremacist happiness.]{.underline} And
so **[we must]{.underline} also [carry]{.underline} with us [the
knowledge that their prisons will never make us safer]{.underline},
[that their laws will never fully include all of us, and that the
ability for only some to climb over that wall and blend into
cookie-cutter streets at the expense of those continually pushed out,
murdered, and ignored will never be enough.]{.underline}** Part of
[**this ongoing work**, built]{.underline} through the resistance and
struggle [over the past five hundred years as methodologies of
abolitionism]{.underline}, **[is to look]{.underline}** for those
convergences, to seek the layers upon layers of consolidation, the
concentric genealogies, not necessarily as purely linear or
chronological but **[through a framework that can support the move to
resist inclusion into a universal that has always maintained a limited
access into its exclusive realm]{.underline}**. We may hold within us
the understanding that these logics run deep, but so too, do we. [In
this we work, learn, love and fight toward something more]{.underline}.
We move toward a sense, deep and across many realms, that there is
always something more, and in our resistance, in our survival, and in
our remembered names, [we are already something more.]{.underline}

## 2AC a2 CAP

#### Gender is not a product of production, but is production. The organization of kinship sets the stage for capitalist and desire production -- the aff link turns the criticism.

**baedan 14** (a queer journal of heresy, "Against the Gendered
Nightmare". capital letters are so passé. //lilith)

This limit---[[the conflation of the exploitation]{.mark} of subjects
[by capitalism with evidence that capitalism is the origin]{.mark} of
those subjects---[is a flaw of all]{.mark} self-proclaimed ['scientific'
disciplines which]{.mark} aim to [generalize one story into a
materialist theory that locates economics as the cause of all
woes]{.mark}]{.underline}. Following from this, she identifies a wide
range of non-capitalist cultures which are vehemently patriarchal,
including pre-capitalist feudal Europe. She then details several
practices of gender domination (foot binding, chastity belts, and other
fetishized indignities) which cannot be accounted for by a Marxist
analysis of the reproduction of labor power. She argues that at most,
Marxist Feminism can explain the way capitalism seized upon and tinkered
with already existing forces of social control. '[The analysis of the
reproduction of labor power does not even explain why it is usually
women rather than men who do domestic work in the home]{.underline}.'
She argues that [[economics cannot account for the moral element which
determines that a wife is among the commodities needed by a man]{.mark},
that only men can talk to God, and that women are the ones who perform
domestic labor]{.underline}. To her, [[this]{.mark} moral element [is
the]{.mark} massive and [unexplored terrain from which gendered violence
emerges]{.mark} and [that it is the basis of the femininity]{.mark} and
masculinity [that capitalism]{.mark} later
[inherited]{.mark}]{.underline}. It is into this element that she'll
direct the rest of her study. She concludes her critique of [Marxist
feminism by illustrating the silliness of reducing the vastness of the
sex/gender system to being simply 'the reproductive' sphere. For her,
[there is]{.mark} far [too much excess]{.mark} in that system [to be
solely the reproductive aspect of industrial production]{.mark}. Not to
mention that it is also productive in its own way: producing gendered
subjects, for example. [The origins of gender domination]{.mark}, she
claims, [must be located outside the 'mode of
production]{.mark}.']{.underline} Her attempt to find this outside is to
first look at the writings of Levi-Strauss in his explorations of early
kinship structures. His writing places gender and sexuality at the
center of these structures; he develops a theory that links their
essence to the exchange of women between men of various social groups.
In doing so, Rubin believes he has sketched an implicit theory of
gendered oppression. He primarily comes to this conclusion after
studying the role of gift exchange in pre-state arrangements. He finds
that the exchange of gifts was the first measure taken in the long road
toward the development of 'civil society' and the state. For him,
marriage is one of the most significant forms of gift exchange, with
women themselves being the gifts given from one man to another. From
here, he analyzes the incest taboo as a means of policing and enforcing
this exchange of women as gifts. The taboo is less about preventing
endogamous sexual relations, and much more about obliging the exchange
of sisters and daughters into exogamous relations; it is an early
expression of commodity society. [The exchange of human beings is more
powerful than other gifts because it is not simply an arrangement of
reciprocity, but one of kinship. This results in a more long-lasting and
expansive relationship which orders all other types of exchange through
the established kinship network.]{.underline} The marriage ceremonies
recorded in the ethnographic literature are moments in a ceaseless and
ordered procession in which women, children, shells, words, cattle,
names, fish, ancestors, whale's teeth, pigs, yams, spells, dances, mats
and so on, pass from hand to hand, leaving as their tracks the ties that
bind[. [Kinship is organization]{.mark}, and organization gives power.
Organization, then, is [an original structure of power between those who
exchange others]{.mark}. This difference between the exchanged and the
exchangers is a primary split in the system we'll call
gender.]{.underline} For Rubin, the split is between men as organizers,
and women as conduits to organization; men as exchange partners and
women as gifts. The circulation of women provides the mystical powers of
kinship to the men who exchange them; the men benefit from the
subsequent social organization. [The vast permutations of gendered
organization today will not deviate from this unending exchange of
bodies.]{.underline} Women are given in marriage, taken in battle,
exchanged for favors, sent as tribute, traded, bought, and sold. Far
from being confined to the "primitive world," these practices seem only
to become more pronounced and commercialized in more "civilized"
societies. Rubin finds [this concept useful because it locates gender's
emergence in social structures, rather than in biology. Further, it
understands [gender domination to be more rooted in the exchange of
bodies than in the exchange of merchandise.]{.mark} Here, [gender is
not]{.mark} explained away as [a function of reproduction, but is
production itself.]{.mark}]{.underline} [It is an entire system where
individual bodies are produced as gendered subjects and exchanged in the
production of kinship structures]{.underline}. [This system does not
just exchange women, but ancestry, lineage names, social power,
children. [The inauguration of gender violence emerges from this system
within which]{.mark} sex and [gender are organized; the economic
exploitation]{.mark} of this or that gender [is secondary]{.mark} to
this.]{.underline} This story is relevant to the larger one we're trying
to weave because it features gender as inextricably bound to a monster
which is Rubin euphemistically calls social organization. We would call
the monster [domestication, and from this story we can determine a lot
about its character and tendencies]{.underline}. Rubin of course, in
typical academic fashion, shies away from the totality of these
conclusions. She says that, since Levi-Strauss located this exchange as
the beginning of the culture of civilization ("his analysis implies that
the world-historical defeat of women occurred with the origin of
culture, and is a prerequisite of culture"), holding to a firm
interpretation of the theory would also imply that her "feminist task"
would require the destruction of that culture. This destruction remains
unthinkable in her system of thought. Again, we'll choose to go where
others will not. That an argument points to a necessary destruction of
everything is precisely why we'd follow it. The second story that Gayle
Rubin recites is one more common: psychoanalysis and its Oedipus
complex. [Rubin correctly berates psychoanalysis for its tendency to
become more than a theory of the mechanisms which reproduces gender and
sexuality; she argues it has largely become one of those
mechanisms]{.underline}. She follows that a revolt against the
mechanisms of gender must then also be a critique of psychoanalysis.
This critique isn't new for us; Hocquenghem's [queer refusal of
civilization is predicated on this very refusal of
psychoanalysis.]{.underline} Rubin looks at the same concepts as
Hocquenghem in an attempt to flesh out her theory of gender's emergence.
Primarily, she concerns herself with how [psychoanalysis can hint toward
the way children are forced into the categories of boys and
girls]{.underline}. Her exegesis of psychoanalysis mostly centers around
Lacan, who views his efforts as an attempt to identify the traces left
in the individual's psyche by their conscription into kinship
structures, as well as the transformation of their sexuality as they are
integrated into civilized culture. For Rubin this is a nice complement
to Levi-Strauss; whereas the she had already examined the exchange of
individuals within a gender system, she now turns to the interior
realities of those exchanged. She begins from Oedipus: Oedipal crisis
occurs when a child learns of the sexual rules embedded in the terms for
family and relatives. [The crisis begins when the child comprehends the
system and his or her place in it; Before the Oedipal phase, the
sexuality of the child is\... unstructured.]{.underline} Each child
contains all the sexual possibilities available to human expression. But
in any given society, [only some of these possibilities will be
expressed, while others will be constrained]{.underline}. Upon leaving
the Oedipal phase, the child's libido and [gender identity have been
organized in conformity with the rules of the culture which is
domesticating it]{.underline}\... Oedipal complex is an apparatus for
the production of sexual personality. Societies will inculcate in their
young the character traits appropriate to carry on the business of
society\... such as the transformation of the working class into good
industrial workers. [Just as the social forms of labor demand certain
kinds of personality, the social forms of sex and gender demand certain
kinds of people.]{.underline} In the most general terms, the Oedipal
complex is a machine which fashions the appropriate forms of sexual
individuals. Psychoanalysis largely concerns itself with how a child can
properly adapt to this machine. Rubin would say that the machine needs
to be changed. [We'll assert that the machine must be
destroyed.]{.underline} Rubin details how the machine functions along
with an equally familiar concept, the phallus. She emphasizes that
rather than being a biological object, the phallus is primarily a symbol
of belonging to a gendered social order. The father possesses it, and so
he can exchange it for a woman; if a boy behaves and is properly
domesticated, he can one day have the phallus too. The girl is denied
it, and thus has nothing with which to bargain for it. The phallus is
transmitted through particularly gendered bodies and rests upon others.
In the same way as the kinship system detailed by Levi-Strauss [gives
certain people the ability to exchange others as a commodity, the
phallus is the mystical dimension of belonging which is traded for these
bodies in turn.]{.underline} For Rubin, [these systems cohere into a
mutually reinforcing dynamic where women are dispossessed of their very
being, and are possessed and exchanged by men. The linkage of these men
through their exchange of the woman and phallus creates the social bonds
upon which organized civilization is based]{.underline}.

#### The permutation inserts a [mode of hapticality]{.underline} into the process of [anti-capitalist organizing]{.underline} -- they [need to explain]{.underline} how the alternative solves us giving [a shit about each]{.underline} in the [here and now]{.underline}

HAPTICALITY, OR LOVE

**[Never being on the right side]{.underline}** of the Atlantic **[is an
unsettled feeling]{.underline}**, **[the feeling of a thing that
unsettles with others]{.underline}**. It's a feeling, if you ride with
it, **[that produces a certain distance from the settled]{.underline}**,
from those who determine themselves in space and time, who locate
themselves in a determined history. To have been shipped is to have been
moved by others, with others. It is to feel at home with the homeless,
at ease with the fugitive, at peace with the pursued, at rest with the
ones who consent not to be one. Outlawed, interdicted, intimate things
of the hold, containerized contagion, logistics externalises logic
itself to reach you, **[but this is not enough to get at the social
logics]{.underline}**, **[the social poesis]{.underline}**, **[running
through logisticality]{.underline}**. Because while certain abilities --
to connect, to translate, to adapt, to travel -- were forged in the
experiment of hold, they were not the point. As David Rudder sings,
"**[how we vote is not how we party]{.underline}**." Te hold's
**[terrible gift was to gather dispossessed feelings in
common]{.underline}**, **[to create a new feel in the
undercommons]{.underline}**. Previously, this kind of feel was only an
exception, an aberration, a shaman, a witch, a seer, a poet amongst
others, who felt through others, through other things. Previously,
except in these instances, feeling was mine or it was ours. But in the
hold, in the undercommons of a new feel, another kind of feeling became
common. **[This form of feeling was not collective]{.underline}**,
**[not given to decision]{.underline}**, **[not adhering or reattaching
to settlement]{.underline}**, **[nation]{.underline}**,
**[state]{.underline}**, **[territory or historical]{.underline}**
**[story]{.underline}**; **[nor was it repossessed by the
group,]{.underline}** which could not now feel as one, **[reunifed in
time and space]{.underline}**. No, **[when Black Shadow sings "are you
feelin' the feelin?'' he is asking about something else]{.underline}**.
**[He is asking about a way of feeling through others]{.underline}**,
**[a feel for feeling others feeling you]{.underline}**. **[This is
modernity's insurgent feel]{.underline}**, its inherited caress, its
skin talk, tongue touch, breath speech, hand laugh. Tis is the feel that
no individual can stand, and no state abide. **[This is the feel we
might call hapticality]{.underline}**. **[Hapticality]{.underline}**,
the touch of the undercommons, **[the interiority of
sentiment]{.underline}**, the feel that what is to come is here.
Hapticality, **[the capacity to feel though others]{.underline}**,
**[for others to feel through you]{.underline}**, for you to feel them
feeling you, this feel of the shipped is not regulated, at least not
successfully, by a state, a religion, a people, an empire, a piece of
land, a totem. Or perhaps we could say these are now recomposed in the
wake of the shipped. To feel others is unmediated, immediately social,
amongst us, our thing, and even when we recompose religion, it comes
from us, and even when we recompose race, we do it as race women and
men. **[Refused these things,]{.underline}** **[we first refuse
them]{.underline}**, **[in the contained]{.underline}**, **[amongst the
contained]{.underline}**, **[lying together in the ship]{.underline}**,
**[the boxcar]{.underline}**, **[the prison]{.underline}**, **[the
hostel]{.underline}**. **[Skin]{.underline}**, against epidermalisation,
**[senses touching]{.underline}**. Trown together touching each other we
were denied all sentiment, denied all the things that were supposed to
produce sentiment, family, nation, language, religion, place, home.
Tough forced to touch and be touched, to sense and be sensed in that
space of no space, though refused sentiment, history and home, we feel
(for) each other. A feel, a sentiment with its own interiority, there on
skin, soul no longer inside but there for all to hear, for all to move.
Soul music is a medium of this interiority on the skin, its regret the
lament for broken hapticality, its self-regulatory powers the invitation
to build sentimentality together again, feeling each other again, how we
party. Tis is our hapticality, our love. Tis is love for the shipped,
love as the shipped. Tere's a touch, a feel you want more of, which
releases you. **[The closest Marx ever got to the general antagonism was
when he said "from each according to his ability, to each according to
his need" but we have read this as the possession of ability and the
possession of need]{.underline}**. **[What if we thought of the
experiment of the hold as the absolute fluidity]{.underline}**, **[the
informality]{.underline}**, **[of this condition of need and
ability?]{.underline}** **[What if ability and need were in constant
play and we found someone who dispossessed us so that this movement was
our inheritance]{.underline}**. **[Your love makes me strong, your love
makes me weak]{.underline}**. What if "the between the two," the lost
desire, the articulation, was this rhythm, this inherited experiment of
the shipped in the churning waters of fesh and expression that could
grasp by letting go ability and need in constant recombination. If he
moves me, sends me, sets me adrift in this way, amongst us in the
undercommons. So long as she does this, she does not have to be. **[Who
knows where Marx got this inheritance of the hold]{.underline}**,
**[from Aristotle denying his slave world or Kant talking to sailors or
Hegel's weird auto-eroticism or just being ugly and dark and
fugitive.]{.underline}** Like Zimmy says, precious angel, **[you know
both our forefathers were slaves]{.underline}**, which is not something
to be ironic about. This feel is the hold that lets go (let's go) again
and again to dispossess us of ability, fll us with need, give us ability
to fll need, this feel. We hear the godfather and the old mole calling
us to become, in whatever years we have, philosophers of the feel.

#### Permutation do both -- refuse the alternative's attempt to disrupt the multitudinous potential of our politics -- "to know the end is to think one knows the totality of the landscape"

**Bey 19**. Marquis Bey is a Ford Foundation Dissertation Fellow and PhD
candidate in the English department at Cornell University \["MARQUIS BEY
\| CRITICAL PRAXIS TOWARD NO "END," Febuary 2019, *Critique and Praxis
13/13*\]//vikas

**[Fundamental here is both the critical and the praxis]{.underline}**.
If critical, which is to say an adjectival critique, **[is a ruthless
interrogation of the established and
institutionalized]{.underline}**---and this, of course, **[is in the
vein of Marx]{.underline}**'s 1843 letter to Arnold Ruge, **[in which he
urges the undertaking of]{.underline}** "die rücksichtlose Kritik alles
Bestehenden"---**[an interrogation of that which settles into a
normative hold circumscribing salvific excesses]{.underline}**; **[and
if praxis is a doing]{.underline}**, **[an agential enactment that bears
on sociality]{.underline}**, **[then a critical praxis marks an
interrogative social enactment]{.underline}**. What kind of politics
might this lead to? **[What kind of sociality might this
engender]{.underline}**, and who might show up to this coalitional
gathering? **[Those maroons, subversive intellectuals, fugitives,
queers, black feminists, and rebellious workers meet to conspire
together]{.underline}** in the undercommons: a non-place where everyone
is black, queer, insurgent, because **[one is changed by entrance into
the undercommons inasmuch as the undercommons is not a place you enter
but a groove that enters you]{.underline}**. Critical praxis becomes a
radical invitation to not only do but to be done by the undercommon
insurgency that places a demand. And such an interrogation must suspend
the presumption of the end goal. We know from Moten and Harney, and Jack
Halberstam, that what we think we want before the crucial point of
crisis that precipitates our insurgency will necessarily shift after
we've attained the limits of what our coalitional knowledge could
compile. **[It is not because we are insufficient]{.underline}**, **[as
if insufficiency is a deficiency rather than a willingness to risk
getting at the outer limits]{.underline}** of where we dared to think;
it is because we cannot, and must not, assume that the logics and
rubrics---radically altered as they may be---**[we have when moving
within the maelstrom of the hegemonic in order to exceed that maelstrom
can operate to our benefit]{.underline}** when we've breached the
hegemon. We will need new rubrics and metrics, unrubrics and unmetrics,
because a radically other-world requires radically other means to love
it, to caress it, to be all the way in it So why is there no "end"?
**[To assert this might seem to sidestep what Foucault claims in the
Preface of Anti-Oedipus]{.underline}**: **[to be]{.underline}** "less
**[concerned with why this or that than with how to
proceed]{.underline}**." Refusing to bank on the "end" is, at least in
part, how to proceed. "**[An abdication of political
responsibility?"]{.underline}** Moten and Harney write, anticipating the
accusation. **["OK. Whatever]{.underline}**. **[We're just
anti-politically romantic about actually existing social
life]{.underline}**." **[One's concern must be an ethical one
that]{.underline}**, I want to submit, **[perhaps in supplementation to
an oversight in Moten and Harney]{.underline}**, not only sets its
sights on social life that "actually" (I shiver at the implicit hubris
of this word, its presumptive facticity) exists but, more substantively,
fertilizes the conditions of possibility for otherwise and unsung and
unknown emergence. **[There is no "end" because to know the end is to
think one knows the totality of the landscape]{.underline}**, **[a line
of thinking that in fact cannot account for that which falls outside of
the dictates of existential]{.underline}** and ontological
**[legibility]{.underline}**. **[There might always be something else
just outside]{.underline}**, **[and we cannot foreclose the
discussion]{.underline}** when we think it is over. Fugitive planning
plans for what it cannot plan for by refusing to plan for it. So there
is no end in sight because sight is not the only sense available to us.
(But there is also no end in touch, smell, feel, or taste---or any other
proprioceptive sensibilities.) There is no end in sight because our end
may only be someone else's beginning, another's middle. Thus, our
**[critical praxis]{.underline}**, our **[interrogative social
enactment]{.underline}**, **[does something precisely when it commits to
a political endeavor that is not in its own interest or]{.underline}**
the interest of the **[purportedly most marginalized]{.underline}**;
**[it is doing something when it commits to proliferating
life]{.underline}** where no life is said to be found.

#### Neoliberalism's construction of sameness exports Eurocentric visions and supplants indigenous ones

**Agathangelou and Ling 6** Anna Agathangleou is a professor in the
department of Politics at York University and was the fellow in the
Program of Science, Technology & Society at the School of Government,
Harvard, L.H.M. Ling was a professor of International Affairs Julien
J.Studley Programs in International Affairs (SGPIA) at The New School
\["FEAR AND PROPERTY: Why a Liberal Social Ontology Fails Postcolonial
States," *International Affairs*, Working Paper 2006-07, April 2006,
<https://www.files.ethz.ch/isn/19248/Ling_2006-07.pdf>\] DTS

Constructing the passionate Other, then, serves a vital function for the
Liberal Self. [The Other provides an easy target not just for Europe's
guns, ships, and trade but also a moral and physical landscape on which
to project all those passions and desires not allowed at home by the
dour Protestantism.]{.underline} The Other has none of what Europeans
could identify as "civilization": a Leviathan-like state ("this land is
free!"), religious virtue through self-denial ("they have no morals"),
codified laws to establish order ("they cannot govern themselves"), and
most importantly, repression of "the passions" ("they're animals").
[With Liberal and Neoliberal modernization as doctrine for economic
development since the late 19th century, the Other is seen to lack all
those qualities assigned to the Western, capitalist Self: "rationality,"
"impersonal decision-making," "specialization"]{.underline} (cf. Banuri
1990). [Liberals extend Hobbes' State of Nature to the
world.]{.underline} Since no global Leviathan exists, a "warre of all
against all" must prevail in world politics. [Accordingly, fear and
insecurity of theft, murder, or domination by Others requires a strong,
domestic Leviathan.]{.underline} The Liberal state thus takes on the
autonomous, singular, and self-defined "sovereignty" of the individual
in the State of Nature who, as Hobbes put it, springs forth "like
mushrooms after a rain." This Liberal presumption runs deep. **[Even
those who identify themselves by another theoretical tradition, like
Alexander Wendt (2005) with constructivism, reveal their Liberal stripes
by stipulating that the state must take on the "sovereign" attributes of
a "person" to be held accountable for its actions.]{.underline}**
Counter-intuitively, [Liberals claim to herald an era of "democratic
peace."6 Liberal capitalism's emphasis on uninterrupted transactions,
they contend, maintain mutually-beneficial returns on investment that
raises the stakes for war thereby discouraging it]{.underline}; indeed,
capitalism actually effects the opposite by encouraging cooperation even
under conditions of international anarchy (Keohane 1984). Both
domestically and internationally, capitalist market practices produce
all the fundaments of a democratic society: e.g., a "revolutionary"
middle class, an active civil society, rational pursuit of individual
interest that leads to self-censoring collective action, rule of law,
and so on (Olson 1993; Held 1995). Globalization, as another venue for
Liberal capitalism, embeds democracy "in the depths of people's hearts
and minds" (Sakamoto 1991: 122). Liberals are able to do so only through
its Other. By mystifying or denying its own desires and passions
(Crawford 2000), [Liberals can claim that their states are ruled by
"rational," "objective" interests that promote "democracy" and
"rule]{.underline} of law." Indeed, the Liberal inter-state system can
be understood formally, mathematically, and impartially (cf. King,
Keohane, and Verba 1994).7 In fencing off "passion" for the cultural,
historical, colonized Other, Liberals fold colonial power relations
conveniently into a capitalist world order run by rational elites.
Indeed, for Uday Mehta (1997), [Liberalism has always utilized
"strategies of exclusion" to sustain the bourgeois order. Now the same
logic and practice, he suggests, are applied to world
politics]{.underline}. **[Where previously Liberals subordinated and
exploited domesticated identities in term of gender (e.g., women) and
class (e.g., workers),8 Liberalism exported to the world at large now
takes on racial, sexual, and cultural characteristics.]{.underline}**
[It targets externalized Others like India, for instance, by labeling it
"inscrutable," "child-like," or "barbaric]{.underline}." Accordingly,
the Indian Other needs to be defined and educated by the all-knowing,
worldly, civilized West. [Neoliberalism jazzes up dour, penny-pinching
**Protestant capitalism to emphasize its multicultural, youthful, and
trendy pleasures. Note how the Coca Cola Company, one of capitalism's
enduring icons, captures this Neoliberal appeal with effective
advertising**]{.underline} slogans over the years. A sample from the end
of the Cold War in 1989 to the present indicates the easy, everyday
allure promised by and associated with a bottle of Coke: 1990: You Can't
Beat the Real Thing 2001: Life Tastes Good 2005: Make it Real9 2006:
Welcome to the Coke Side of Life [**Under Neoliberal globalization,
"difference" is marketed into "sameness."** Much like the Benetton ads
featuring good-looking youths from different races, ethnicities, and
cultures but all, nonetheless, wearing the latest Italian
fashions,]{.underline} **[Neoliberals wipe out the violent history that
produced our differentiations of "race/ethnicity" in the first
place]{.underline}** or the fact that the Italian clothing industry
enjoys far greater profits and standing in the global marketplace than,
say, an Ethiopian one. [In this way, Neoliberalism manages difference
into a wholly-owned subsidiary of Western-capitalist, consumer-based
"goodness."]{.underline} Another example comes from the United Nations
Development Programme's (UNDP) 2004 Human Development Report, Cultural
Liberty in Today's Diverse World. [It calls for valuing "cultural
diversity" in all its "vitality" but defeats the UNDP's own purpose by
defining identity in Neoliberal terms only: e.g., "\[c\]ultural liberty
is a vital part of human development because being able to choose one's
identity -- who one is -- without losing the respect of others or being
excluded from other choices is important in leading a full
life]{.underline}" (emphases added, p. 1). [**Identity here becomes a
singular, self-enclosed** social signifier with a right to
representation]{.underline} -- much like the right to consume goods --
in order to lead a "full" life, thereby requiring state-protected
tolerance and other virtues of Liberal pluralism. [We define identity as
a collective legacy, not individual choice]{.underline}. This legacy,
moreover, results from interactions among and within multiple legacies
which themselves are products of such interactions in the past.
[Tolerance in this case is nice but]{.underline} problematic. [It
renders invisible the power plays necessary for "tolerance" to take hold
in the first place. What we need is a concrete working-through of
contending terms of being so that we may sustain and generate
connections for generations to come. **Simply negotiating "turf rights"
between majorities and minorities misses the point and merely postpones
the struggles to come.**]{.underline}

#### Our model of debate resists capitalism and neoliberalism through the attentiveness of care -- our affirmative prefigures counter-capitalist cultures of care that resist the family as the only organizing principle of capitalist social life.

**Carlson and Walker 18** \[Anna Carlson & Briohny Walker; "Free
universities and radical reading groups: learning to care in the here
and now;" *Continuum*; 2018; Date Accessed: 7 July\] DG

Introduction [The social and cultural reproduction of neoliberalism is
in crisis. [The labour required to reproduce neoliberalism's 'conditions
of possibility' is increasingly onerou]{.mark}s]{.underline} (see Fisher
2009; 17; Lazzarato 2009) [[and]{.mark} [responsibility for]{.mark}
managing [this workload continues to be
individualized]{.mark}]{.underline} (see Berlant 2011[),
[feminized]{.mark}]{.underline} (Federici 2006) [[and
outsourced]{.underline}]{.mark} (Nguyen, Zavoretti, and Tronto 2017,
200). Neoliberal [[systems rely on increasingly violent extractive
practices to cultivate new markets]{.mark}, [with disastrous
environmental, cultural and social impacts]{.mark}]{.underline} (see
Lyons, Tager, and Sales 2016). The capitalist futures that once seemed
stable and inevitable (though always already exclusive and illusory)2
are now firmly in crisis (see Berlant 2011; 2; Fisher 2009; 2--3). In
this article, members of the Brisbane Free University (BFU) and Queering
Health Hobart (QHH) explore the [[contradictions of this messy present
through feminist theories of care]{.mark}.]{.underline} In particular,
we draw on our own experiences as participants in these 'alternative
spaces of teaching and learning' (Thompsett 2017, 25) [[to think about
the **labour of social reproduction and the contradictions of caring
under, against and beyond capitalism**]{.underline}]{.mark} (Fraser
2016, 99; Bellacasa 2017, 1). We draw out three key values of
prefigurative, counter-capitalist care: attentiveness, locatedness and
relationality. We explore those values in relation to the looming crises
of capitalism. [[In small and quiet ways,]{.mark} these [values
reproduce subjects and cultures that disrupt the reproduction of
neoliberal logics]{.mark}. By [attending to one another **in the
specific conditions of the 'here and now', we begin to prefigure
counter-capitalist cultures of care**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Accidental)
autoethnography: research as care labour in the 'here and now' This
article draws on rich traditions of queer, feminist and anti-colonial
autoethnographic research (Butz 2010; Mountz et al. 2015).
[[Autoethnography]{.mark} is a method of [self observation, looking at
our everyday practices to develop social and political
theory]{.mark}]{.underline} (Osborne and Grant-Smith 2017). It is [[a
fluid kind of inquiry]{.mark}, focused on the everyday ways communities
figure out how to act in the present.]{.underline} In our case, this
article represents our attempts to explore 6 years of accidental
autoethnographic research (see Poulos 2010; Fujii 2015) in prefigurative
free education projects. Our reflections here are inseparable from years
of conversation and shared theorizing in these spaces.4 These ideas have
been produced collectively, and our fellow participants are actively
engaged in 'theorising their own experiences' (Macoun and Miller 2014,
287). While we remain responsible for the specific content of this
article, we acknowledge that this work is entirely contingent on these
ongoing community discussions.5 We delivered a version of this article
at the CSAA6 Cultures of Capitalism conference in Aotearoa in December
2017. In the discussion that followed our presentation, another
conference participant asked us to expand on how care operated in these
projects. This article is a longer response to that generous
questioner[. [We hope that this response]{.mark} also [serves as a
prompt]{.mark} for those of us engaged in prefigurative7 political
projects, [to acknowledge care as political practice]{.mark}, and to
[think about its role in building viable communities in the
ecologically, economically and ethically precarious present. Learning to
care]{.mark}:]{.underline} thinking free education practices through
ethics of care [[Social reproductive labour]{.mark} historically
[includes things like 'caring for friends and family members,
maintaining households and broader communities, and sustaining
connections more generally']{.mark}]{.underline} (Fraser 2016, 99[).
[Under neolib]{.underline}]{.mark}[eralism, [this labour has expanded to
include the ephemeral work of individual
'brand-building']{.mark}]{.underline} (Fisher 2009),
[['self-care',]{.underline}]{.mark} (Bakker 2007, 542) [[and digital
social labour]{.underline}]{.mark} (Dyer-Witheford 2015). [[This]{.mark}
predominantly [un- or underpaid, feminized]{.mark} and [globalized
work]{.mark}]{.underline} (Bellacasa 2017) is [[central to the operation
and reproduction of neoliberalism]{.underline}]{.mark}. For Fraser
(2016) theorizing, [this work is [thus significant because it reveals a
crisis tendency in capitalist social reproduction:]{.mark} a 'crisis of
care' with overlapping 'economic, ecological and political strands, all
of which intersect with and exacerbate one another']{.underline} (Fraser
2016, 99). In this section, we use our experiences as participants in
free education projects to ask how forms and practices of care can build
the conditions of possibility for alternative, counter-capitalist
cultural and political systems. [[Conceptions and cultures of care are
'deeply implicated in existing structures of power and
inequality']{.underline}]{.mark} (Robinson 2011, 3), [[and care has been
a focal point of feminist]{.underline}]{.mark} (Fraser 2016),
[[queer]{.mark}]{.underline} (Bellacasa 2017) [[and critical race
theory]{.underline}]{.mark} (Collins 2000) [[and feminist urban
justice]{.mark}]{.underline} (Williams 2017, 821). How care is used in
prefigurative political spaces makes visible both the values and norms
that these spaces foster, as well as their contradictions. Do our
practices of care simply facilitate neoliberal cultural expansion? Can
they disrupt neoliberal logics even as they are absorbed or surrounded
by them? And [in the face of a 'crisis of care']{.underline} (Fraser
2016, 99), [[how do we practice militant forms of
care]{.underline}]{.mark} (Fraser 2013) [[that make visible alternative
social relations, rather than simply 'plugging the leaks' in
neoliberalism?]{.underline}]{.mark} BFU and QHH Both BFU and QHH are
'permanent experiments' (Thompsett 2016, 62), growing from complex
relationships to institutions of health and education. BFU began in
November 2012 in the 'empty concrete box' (Sri 2013) of a car park
underneath a bank, in Brisbane's inner-city suburb West End. It hatched
slowly, through long conversations and shared wonder. We hoped that BFU
might be a space in which we could 'reimagine education (. . .)
challenge the divide between the academic sphere and the public forum,
between the sandstone and the street corner' (BFU 2012).8 Over the past
6 years, BFU has hosted numerous free lectures, workshops and public
discussions in public spaces, often in collaboration with other
community organizations. We have also facilitated a weekly radical
reading group and an activist research collective (BFU 2012). QHH is a
reading group consisting of health(care) workers, university students
from diverse disciplines and members of Hobart's LGBTQIA + community.
Together, this deeply cross-disciplinary collective is interrogating the
pressures of institutional 'efficiency' on the goals of patient-centred
care, seeking to explore and pursue approaches to healthcare that centre
difference. QHH arises first from an interest in 'queer health', that
is, the health and well-being of people of non-normative sexualities
and/or genders. However, the group is also driven by a desire to queer
health: to think through contemporary health practices and industries
and the concept of health through queer theory, with an eye on 'the
normative, alienating, even damaging and iatrogenic9 assumptions that
are perpetuated in modern healthcare' (Brendelow 2014, viii). QHH stands
in critique of the lack of meaningful diversity training (Ahmed and Swan
2006, 96) and the limitations of efficiency-driven public institutions,
such as universities and hospitals, to respond with care to the situated
and variable needs of communities and individuals. In its early days,
the reading group met fortnightly in an empty classroom at the
university. The reading group was therefore subject to the limits of
scavenged institutional space, including hasty last-minute rescheduling
and the possibility of late-comers being accidentally locked out of the
building if they arrived after the campus was 'secured' for the night.
Seeking a location that might feel more welcoming to people from outside
of the academy, the group currently meets in borrowed space above a
local pub. Both of these projects have tapped into a resurgence in
prefigurative political organizing, particularly free universities,
radical reading groups and creative urban relocations and occupations
(Graeber 2009, 84; Davis et al. 2011, 76; Avrich and Pateman 1995; Bey
2003). So while [[these]{.underline} [projects]{.underline}]{.mark} are
[[linked]{.mark} [in some ways to]{.mark} energetic processes of
n[eoliberal enclosure within the university]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Osborne and Grant-Smith 2017, 60--61; Lyons, Tager, and Sales 2016) and
the health system (McGregor 2001, 82), they [[do not exist wholly in
opposition to these formal sites. Rather, they gather material, strength
and inspiration from other prefigurative and anti-oppressive political
structures, including worldwide Occupy movement]{.mark}s[, free
universities, prison abolition organizing and Indigenous
activism]{.mark}]{.underline} (such as that of the Brisbane Aboriginal
Sovereign Embassy and the Warriors of the Aboriginal Resistance). QHH
also draws from a depth of history and activism around gender and sexual
diversity (along with other bodily differences), including the Gay
Liberation movement and its contemporary permutations, fat activism and
the Disability Rights Movement. [[These inheritances orient
us]{.underline}]{.mark} (Ahmed 2006, 545) [[toward]{.mark} political
[action situated in the material conditions of the present, unwilling to
separate the political 'ends' from means]{.mark}. They [remind us to
think about how to produce alternative 'sites of possibility' to disrupt
or undermine neoliberal hegemony]{.mark}. We now investigate these
'sites of possibility' [through the interrelated practices of
relationality, attentiveness and locatedness.]{.mark}]{.underline}
Relationality A [[feminist 'ethics of care']{.underline}]{.mark}
(Robinson 2011, 3) [[encourages us to take relational motivations
seriously as crucial features of ethical decision-making]{.mark}. An
ethics of care [describes a way of asking questions about justice
through relationships and responsibilities]{.mark}. This reflects
[conceptions of ethical agency that see all of us as 'interdependent and
as having the potential and responsibility to be caring and cared
for']{.mark}]{.underline} (Williams 2001, 478). In comparison, [l[iberal
(and neoliberal) models]{.underline}]{.mark} [of justice [are]{.mark}
often [framed around 'legalistic principles that assume individuals are
independent and atomistic beings']{.mark}]{.underline} (Williams 2001,
478) [who [focus on abstract rights and rules in making ethical
decisions]{.mark}.]{.underline} The [[values and norms associated with
and reflected in feminist ethics of care also valorize
specificity]{.mark}:]{.underline} as Featherstone and Morris (2012, 341)
point out, [feminist ethics of care [develop theories of value that are
'tied to concrete circumstances rather than philosophical
abstractions']{.mark}.]{.underline} In BFU and QHH, feminist ethics of
care manifest in our [[explicit acknowledgment that]{.mark} the
organizer is more important than the organizing, [the activist valuable
beyond their capacity for activism.]{.mark}]{.underline} Organizers and
participants of BFU and QHH have always maintained that these projects
will not continue if they become unduly burdensome or stressful, or if
they put a strain on the relationships that underpin them. As
organizers, we have each gone through periods of little or no
involvement with these projects, and periods of intensive engagement.
[[By thinking about free education projects as complex webs of
intertwining and overlapping relationships of care, we see a telos that
is relational rather than abstract.]{.underline} BFU]{.mark} and QHH
also resist neoliberal logics of success by refusing to identify key
outcomes or performance indicators (Brown 2015, 2). Both spaces push
against progress narratives that require expansion or 'scaling up'
(Srnicek and Williams 2016). Many of the early conversations within the
BFU included discussions of how to expand the project into the outer
suburbs, or to other cities, and many participants were eager to see the
project grow and take root in other places. These expansions never
really happened, despite the enthusiasm of some participants.10 In
thinking about BFU through the lens of care, we suggest that these
anti-expansionist decisions might be understood both as a result of
pragmatic factors (that the main organizers simply did not have the
energy to set up in communities where we knew no one and had no existing
relationships) as well as an important expression of the values of
relationality and locatedness. [Commonly [under capitalism,]{.mark} and
increasingly with the rise of precarious labour]{.underline} (Lazzarato
2009, 110), [[people are regarded as replaceable or interchangeable
units]{.mark}, 'human resources'. [Place and environment are]{.mark}
also [marketized: where we live and organize is simply the 'blank
slate']{.mark} [on which new markets might take form. We]{.mark}
strongly [desire not to replicate these careless neoliberal dynamics
within these projects.]{.mark}]{.underline} Through organizing free
education projects, we have experienced beyond refute that those we care
about are not interchangeable or reducible to resources. We have learnt
that [[the places in which we live and organize are made up of complex
webs of unpredictable human and more]{.mark}-than-human
[relationships,]{.mark} and that sometimes those relationships intersect
with organizing practices.]{.underline}11 These experiences have made
visible to us the messy histories of ideas and environments and bodies,
lineages that intersect with colonial and capitalist trajectories
(Moreton-Robinson 2012; Behrendt 2008; Todd 2016) and resistances to
them. In learning to learn with these histories, we have experienced
alternative, careful relationships to place and people. Attentiveness
[[The desire to care for each other also leads us to pay attention to
the specific conditions of the 'here and now']{.underline}.]{.mark} For
feminist ethicist Joan Tronto, '[[the first moral aspect of
caring]{.mark} [is attentiveness. . .]{.mark} \[a\]ttentiveness, simply
[recognizing the needs of those around us,]{.mark} is [a difficult
task,]{.mark} and indeed, a moral achievement']{.underline} (Tronto
1993, 127). To recognize the need for care takes time to focus and
engage. To act on this recognition asks more time again. [[Attentiveness
is slow]{.mark}. It [cuts against]{.mark} the grain of [capitalist
temporalities,]{.mark} timelines which are so often driven by efficiency
and progress (Brown 2015, 10) and [characterized by anxiousness,
restlessness and carelessness]{.mark}]{.underline} (Fisher 2009, 7). As
participants in and organizers of free universities and radical reading
groups, we learn to pay attention to (and thus value) practices and
knowledges that directly oppose and actively undermine forms of
colonial, racial and capitalist violence (MoretonRobinson 2012; Martin
Nakata et al. 2012). [But [we also learn for the sake of
learning]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} for [its capacity to bring joy and
meaning to our relationships in the present.]{.mark} It is [through our
experiences of learning to be attentive]{.mark} to different things that
[we open up space for 'other possibilities']{.mark}]{.underline} (New
York based activist Joe Kay, interviewed in Thompsett 2017). In a
practical sense, [these 'other possibilities' are [made visible in the
fundamentally different material and affective experiences of learning
that take place in free education spaces.]{.mark}]{.underline} Learning
at BFU or QHH looks, feels, even smells different from other kinds of
learning. In part, this is because the lines between 'teachers' and
'learners' are blurred by the relationships that underpin these
projects. It is also, we suggest, because we are called upon in these
spaces to pay attention to different things, and acknowledge different
agencies. As one participant, Jonathan, wrote in a submission to a zine
produced by BFU (Sri 2013): the line between organisers and audience
members had been attacked with bleach and a scrubbing brush. I think if
someone had marched in and yelled out: "Who's in charge here?" there
would've been an awkward pause where no one moved, and then suddenly
absolutely everybody would have thrown up their hands and claimed
responsibility' Attentiveness in free education spaces also means paying
attention to the dynamics operating within the space; the 'background'
(Ahmed 2006) affective and material labour that makes learning possible
(Thompsett 2017). In a comment on an earlier draft of this article, one
BFU reading group participant commented on a practice of one of the
other long-term participants, who arrives every week with a stack of
beautiful porcelain tea cups and saucers, a large metal teapot, tea
leaves and fresh milk, often in addition to a home-made cake or
biscuits. The participant wrote that 'his quiet attentiveness to making
sure that people's cups are filled, and how he remembers how everyone
takes their tea, is this incredible and lovely recognition of learning
as embodied, learners-as-bodies'. Another (anonymous) submission to the
earlier BFU zine reminded us of 'some things. . .we could ask ourselves
when embarking on free university adventures' (Brisbane Free University
Participants 2013). These included the provocation to check whether
'hearts and heads' were staying in 'close communication'; and whether
our 'classroom' reflected the kind of world we were imagining (Brisbane
Free University Participants 2013). [The significance of attentiveness,
and therefore of slowness, relates also to the theoretical engagements
taking place within these projects**. [In resistance to the hurried
scholarship of profit-oriented universities, we strive to hold open
spaces in which to experiment with thinking and learning without the
pressure to rush to conclusions or justify time
spent]{.mark}.**]{.underline} Michelle Boulous Walker (2017, xiv), in
her vital engagement with questions of slowness and scholarship,
explains: '\[u\]nder situations of high time pressure, it becomes more
and more difficult to engage with the complex and difficult in
substantial and intense ways'. In embracing slowness and rejecting
notions of linear progress, free universities and radical reading groups
resist the commodification of processes of learning[. While [capitalist
temporalities continue to haunt our lives and projects, these forms of
attentiveness foster small alternatives, cracks in the concrete of
neoliberalization ]{.mark}]{.underline}[(]{.mark}see Fisher 2009[).
[They lead us away from logics of efficiency]{.mark} [and productivity
toward relationships of care]{.mark} and carefulness.]{.underline} In
thinking about these practices of attentiveness (slowness, wonder,
curiosity, care), we see different ways of thinking and working
together. [These [glimpses provide sustenance for our
scholarship]{.mark}]{.underline} (within and outside conventional
universities) [[and glimmers of hope for our collective futures]{.mark}.
Locatedness Space and place have risen as major forces within the
projects, initially through practical challenges.]{.underline} For BFU,
the desire to avoid buying time in commercial meeting places has
oriented us towards public spaces, and failing that, borrowed spaces.
This early [interest [in **'reclaiming' or 're-occupying' the public
realm**]{.mark}]{.underline} (Davis et al. 2011) oriented us toward
engaging with the specificity of Brisbane's urban realm. The particular
difficulty we experienced (and continue to experience) in trying to find
free, public spaces in which we can meet spoke to the rapid enclosure of
the urban public realm through neoliberal processes of gentrification
and privatization (Harvey 2008, 3). These practical difficulties lead us
to ask deeper questions about the political and economic governance of
Brisbane public spaces, and to engage with the historical (and ongoing)
dynamics that shape this settler colonial city (Strakosch 2015; Brand,
Bond, and Shannon 2016). As white settler women in urban Australia,
organizing free education projects in Brisbane's public realm has meant
thinking about colonialism, enclosure and boundaries (see Meyerhoff and
Thompsett 2017). Since it started in 2012, BFU has occupied a series of
spaces along West End's 'Boundary Street'. This street is so-named
because it represents the historical boundary line between the colonial
city and the Aboriginal encampment: the heavily surveilled, policed and
regulated 'boundary' of the city (Brand, Bond, and Shannon 2016; Birch
2010). Brisbane has more Boundary Streets than any other city in
Australia, many of which still bear their historical name, and many of
which continue to be sites of surveillance and over-policing of
Indigenous people (Brand, Bond, and Shannon 2016). These 'boundaries'
encircle the inner city, demarcating and commemorating colonial
histories of (attempted) enclosure, containment and dispossession (M.
Mitropoulos, personal communication, March 28, 2017) . They remind us,
as Povinelli (2017, 31) writes, that boundaries, like frontiers,
'operate as a way of imagining space so that various things can be done
there'. Of course, Boundary Streets are also a key sites of Indigenous
resistance, and West End's Boundary Street has an active and thriving
Indigenous presence (Brand, Bond, and Shannon 2016). The area around
Boundary Street, the Kurilpa peninsula, also has deep (pre-colonial and
continuing) significance for Jagera and Turrbal people as a traditional
meeting point, ceremonial ground and area of trade (Connors 2015). One
of the spaces that BFU regularly uses is called 'People's Park', a small
park close to the centre of Boundary Street. The park was the site of an
art project by local Aboriginal community members, as a stand against
processes of gentrification that saw 'minority groups, Indigenous
groups, the homeless community and low income earners. . .moved out of
the local area' (State Library of Queensland 2018). With support from
the local councillor, community members painted a large mural in the
park, to serve as a reminder that the area is 'steeped in Aboriginal
history, even though the demographics and aesthetics have changed
dramatically over the past few years' (State Library of Queensland
2018). [In [thinking about the significance of place to
counter-capitalist cultures of care,]{.mark} we take up Ahmed's (2006)
suggestion that [the background]{.mark} -- the histories -- is 'what
[allows what comes into view to be viewed']{.mark}.]{.underline} Martin
Nakata et al. (2012, 123--124) also emphasize the importance of the
'field of vision', calling on a radical practice of historicization to
explore the possibilities of 'decolonial knowledge-making' in which 'the
Western' is able to be 'made sense of' as a specific cultural and
political project. This focus on the specificity of place and knowledge,
and their co-constitution and co-production, enables us, as Stephen
Muecke (2012) describes it, to 'keep things alive in their place'. In
learning to pay close attention to place and space, we learn -- as
anthropologist Jeffrey Juris notes -- 'a kind of self-reflexivity, to
think about who we are, and how we relate and interact with the world'
(interviewed in Thompsett 2017, 28). This reflexivity has taken varied
forms. In June 2016, for example, we set up in a small park in the
middle of Boundary Street in Brisbane's West End for a talk called
Decolonising the City, with several Indigenous activists, writers and
artists. We set up, as we usually did, with chairs in loose rows, facing
forward towards a speaker. When the speakers and participants arrived,
they immediately set about moving the chairs into a circle, and they
opened the conversation by asking each of the 50 or so participants to
locate themselves in relation to one another and the place, by way of a
'where is home for you' introduction. In the informal conversation that
followed the discussion, several participants drew attention to the
spatial transformation that had happened within the free university
during that session. Many of us reflected on how different it had felt
in comparison to other free university talks; how transformative it was
to be asked this question in this space, to be reminded of our own
(varied) 'inheritances' (Ahmed 2006, 545) as we gathered to think about
urban decolonization. Locating ourselves in the 'here and now' has been
vital to these projects. For BFU participants and organizers --
Indigenous and non-Indigenous, white settler and migrant Australians,
folks of colour, homeless folks and refugee communities -- 'learning' on
Boundary Street requires us to see these violent histories of the urban
realm as alwaysalready in conversation with academic work and theory. No
matter how varied the topics covered by BFU talks (from moral philosophy
or feminist aesthetics to political economics and harmony-singing
workshops), they are always grounded in the material
interpersonal/relational conditions of the 'here and now'. Given that
neoliberal expansion so often functions through particular spatial
imaginaries of placelessness, hypermobility and globalization (Giles and
Carlson 2017, 8), this ethic of groundedness compels us to 'stay with
the trouble' (Haraway 2016) of our violent and messy present. For QHH,
the spatial relationship to both the university campus and broader
LGBTQIA + communities is significant. As the last state in Australia to
decriminalize homosexuality, Tasmania has a powerfully homophobic
history. The presence of a vigorous LGBTQIA + community and the positive
state-level outcome of the 2017 Same Sex Marriage (SSM) Survey suggests
that Tasmanian activists have been successful in generating cultural
change. However, decisions made by the University of Tasmania (UTas) in
the run-up to the SSM survey (the time period in which QHH arose)
suggests that it might be too soon to relegate Tasmanian homophobia to
the history books. Having at no stage voiced specific support for
LGBTQIA + staff and students, UTas rented campus space to an anti-SSM
campaign group (Ferwerda et al. 2017). These events damaged the sense of
belonging on campus for queer staff and students. This is deeply
significant for QHH, as a free education project already questioning the
capacity of both the university and healthcare systems to care for and
about the full range of human difference. Under these conditions,
choosing initially to meet on campus was imbued with a sense of
significance, even resistance. However, the desire that all members of
the diverse communities the group seeks to centre should feel equally
comfortable in our meeting spaces has since taken the group off campus.
QHH are now growing community and knowledges together in a borrowed
space above a pub. You can imagine how we laughed when we first saw it
and realised we\'d booked a board room. The lessons of space and place
that QHH negotiate do not end there. Attentiveness to place also guides
us toward intersectional thinking. In learning to care for one another
in the here and now, we inevitably meet at intersections (Collins 2000).
The land on which we meet and learn is nipaluna (Tasmanian Aboriginal
Centre 2018). In all we do, we stand in commitment to this place, and to
the unceded sovereignty of the muwinina people. We acknowledge the
custodial rights and responsibilities of the palawa peoples (Riawunna
Centre for Aboriginal Education, 2018), and Elders past, present and
future. Tasmania is a place with its own distinct and devastating
colonial history of attempted genocide of the Indigenous population,
followed by erasure, both of the attempted genocide itself and of the
enduring resilience and resistance of survivors and descendants (Lehman
2018; Sculthorpe and Mansell 2008).Our location on this unceded land, as
well as our engagements with Indigenous participants, communities and
theorists, reminds us that the question of what meaningfully queering
health might look like inHobart must engage with our colonial contexts
and complicities. Otherwise, we risk reinforcing some oppressions even
while pursuing the alleviation of others (see Morgensen 2011). In order
to stand within the tensions and potentialities of thinking with and
through queerness in this place, we read and listen to the words of
those who live at the intersections of queerness and Indigeneity (Baylis
2014; Clark 2015). Of course, these projects are permanent experiments,
and we are always still learning how to engage ethically in this place.
But it is through organizing in these places and communities at we learn
the importance of grounding and locating ourselves in the material
conditions of the present, and taking seriously the challenges and
contradictions that presents. Conclusions [[In thinking about practices
of free education through feminist theories of care, we are learning
what care is and how we might create and sustain
it]{.mark}.]{.underline} As we experiment with prefigurative political
projects, we are experimenting in our own becoming, and that of our
communities. We have found ourselves, and each other, striving in small
and intimate ways to live and learn together. We find moments beyond the
brutal carelessness of neoliberalism, small tastes of something better.
[In [thinking about how we care for one another in these spaces, we
reject political projects that separate means from ends, treating the
'here and now' as simply a testing ground for future expansion.]{.mark}
As capitalist social reproduction falters and corresponding future
imaginaries buckle under the weight of grief and precarity, we stop
where and when we are, and turn towards each other.]{.underline} We have
found in free education projects small and everyday opportunities to
(re)produce counter-capitalist cultures of care. Increasingly, we take
up these opportunities where we find them. Following Paulo Freire, great
figure of emancipatory pedagogies, we work in the present for 'the
creation of a world in which it will be easier to love' (1970).

Canada, and First Nations provided leadership and guidance from early
on. These include the Council of Canadians, Blue Mountain Center,
Detroit People\'s Water Board, Vermont Law School Environmental Law
Center, Blue Planet Project, FLOW for Water, and Food and Water Watch
